GHANA
APPRAISAL OF UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        The Government of Ghana has requested Bank assistance in financing
an agricultural development project in the Upper Region of Ghana. The Proj-
ect was prepared by the staff of the Resident Mission for Western Africa
with Ghanaian staff of the Ministry of Agriculture and other relevant agen-
cies. This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission that
visited Ghana during November 1975.
ii.       Ghana has recently been making strenuous efforts to diversify its
agricultural sector. Rice production has increased significantly, and em-
phasis is now being placed on expansion of cotton, oilseeds and livestock
products which are currently imported.
iii.      Agricultural production in the Upper Region is a complex matrix
of annual crops and livestock competing for a limited area of land. This
is further compounded by the loss of good arable land to onchocerciasis
(riverblindness), trypanomiasis (sleeping sickness) and rapidly increasing
soil erosion. The Project would complement the ongoing onchocerciasis erad-
ication scheme and would assist in arresting soil losses from erosion. Be-
cause of the complexities in production systems and their interdependency,
proposals were put to Government by a Bank identification mission in October
1974 that the Region be developed as a whole, with initial emphasis being
placed on sustainable agricultural production and improved farm incomes.
The proposed Project sets out to achieve these objectives.
iv.       The Project covers some 27,300 km2 supporting 10% of Ghana's popu-
lation including 125,000 farm families and 40% of Ghana's livestock popula-
tion. The Region is susceptible to occasional drought, which although never
disastrous, can considerably reduce yields. The Project would benefit most
of the farmers in the Region.
v.         The Project would provide physical infrastructure, support ser-
vices, radio broadcasting and farm inputs. The Project's design would nei-
ther unduly disrupt existing Government services, nor leave a void after the
development period (1976/77 - 1980/81). The Project would develop around 90
service centers that would provide improved extension and management services
to the Region's farmers; it would, through farm loans and improved supply of
inputs, improve production of 108,000 ha of land (30% of total arable) cur-
rently under cultivation including traditional mixed millet and sorghum,
dwarf sorghum, maize, groundnuts, yams, cotton, rice, cowpeas and tomatoes;
it would provide improved onfarm grain storage, better animal health and
husbandry services, and would establish ten 2,000 ha cattle ranches; it
would develop seven applied research and demonstration units and ensure
proper seed multiplication; it would develop a pilot adult functional lit-
eracy scheme (10% of the present population is literate); it would expand
Ghana Broadcasting Corporation's facilities in the Upper Region; it would



- ii -
improve human nutrition and would train development staff inside and outside
Ghana. It would construct 120 new dams and rehabilitate 100 existing dams to
provide water for livestock, dry season (irrigation) farming and for general
water conservation purposes; through new soil conservation measures it would
protect a minimum of 160,000 ha of land, including the catchment areas of
dams; it would assist farmers in constructing 700 village wells; and stores,
offices and houses for Project staff would be constructed at the service
centers. It would provide vehicles for Project staff and would construct
two 9,000 ton/annum cotton ginneries. Plans for the future development of
onchocerciasis-eradicated areas in the Upper and Northern Regions would be
prepared. Institutionally, the Project would establish the Farmers Services
Company (UR) Ltd. to provide farm inputs; strengthen the Regional Branch of
the Agricultural Development Bank and the Regional Development Corporation;
and strengthen and train the Regional staff of the Ministry of Agriculture.
vi.        The Project would be controlled by the Upper Region Agricultural
Development Project Executive Committee (URADPEC), established under a
special Government Order, and would be managed by the Upper Region Agri-
cultural Development Project Management Unit (URPMU), established at
BolgatanRa.
vii.      The proposed IBRD loan of US$21.0 million to the Government of
Ghana would be on Third Window terms at 4.85% over 25 years including a
seven-year grace period for principal, during which interest and commitment
charges would be paid. The loan would cover 38% of total Project costs,
estimated at US$54.6 million, and 65% of the foreign exchange costs estimated
at US$32.5 million. Of the US$21.0 million, US$3.8 million would be onlent
to the Farmers Services Company (FSC) at 8.85% for 25 years, with seven
years grace for principal, for the purchase of incremental seasonal inputs;
US$2.2 million would be onlent, at 8.85% for 25 years with seven years grace
for principal, to the Agricultural Development Bank for medium and long term
loans; US$0.4 million would be onlent, at 8.85% for 15 years with five
years grace for principal, to the Cotton Development Board for the purchase of
saw gins; US$0.5 million would be equity for the Farmers Services Company to
assist in its establishment; US$0.5 million would be granted to Ghana Broad-
casting Corporation (GBC), for the establishment of a broadcasting network;
US$8.0 million would be for the establishment and operations of URPMU; and
US$5.6 million would be for contingencies. Seasonal, medium or long term
loans to farmers would bear interest plus service charges if any of not less
thaan 12% per annum.
viii.     The United Kingdom would, under parallel financing arrangements,
finance 20% of total costs equivalent to 34% of foreign exchange costs--
US$11.0 million--comprising a loan of E Stg 4.0 million 1/ and a grant for
technical assistance of approximately f Stg 1.0 million. Local sources would
finance the remaining 42% of Project costs including the Ghanaian Government,
US$13.7 million (25%) and the banking institutions, US$8.9 million (17%).
1/   f Stg 4.0 million would finance preselected and competitive (see para ix)
UK manufactured goods which at the time of cost estimation, June 1975,
were valued, including contingencies, at US$8.7 million, when E Stg 1 was
equivalent to US$2.2.



- iii -
ix.       Procurement of radio telephones, broadcasting equipment, cotton
ginnery equipment and buildings, heavy plant and equipment for land develop-
ment, four-wheel drive vehicles, trucks, spare parts, pumps and pipes for
wells, cotton sprayers, insecticides and fungicides, totaling ¢l0.0 million
(US$8.7 million) would be under UK procurement procedures; the itemized goods
are considered competitive with international prices. Procurement of other
vehicles, animal drugs, equipment, transistor radios, aerial photography,
fertilizers, pesticides, tools, livestock and other farm and ranch equip-
ment with a value of more than US$50,000 would be through international
competitive bidding (ICB), and would have an estimated value of US$13.2
million. A maximum limit of US$1.0 million would be set for those items
purchased through contracts of less than US$50,000. Contracts for con-
struction of buildings, houses and the purchase of construction materials
and furnishings valued at US$9.1 million would not be suitable for ICB due to
their dispersed location and small size of individual contracts and orders;
for these items, contracts would be awarded on the basis of competitive
bidding advertised locally and in accordance with local procedures satisfactory
to the Bank; in these cases foreign firms and suppliers would not be precluded
from bidding. Construction of dams, soil conservation works, wells and grain
stores would be undertaken on force account and are likely to involve only
petty contractors used on an ad hoc basis. A large part of Project costs,
US$10.4 million, would be for labor, staff salaries, allowances, hiring of
consultants, vehicle maintenance and operation, overseas training and general
administrative costs, and would be unsuitable for competitive bidding. The
remaining US$14.6 million would be for physical and price contingencies.
Internationally recruited staff (at an average cost of US$43,000 per manyear
for salary and emoluments) and consultants would be employed under terms and
conditions acceptable to the Bank.
x.        Proceeds of the IBRD loan would be disbursed to cover (a) 100%
of the CIF costs of directly imported vehicles (excluding four-wheel drive
vehicles, land development plant and equipment, and trucks), office and
small equipment, sawgins and associated equipment, animal drugs, and aerial
photography, or 86% if procured locally - US$2.9 million; (b) 100% of the
CIF cost of directly imported fertilizers, oxdrawn equipment, batteries,
transistor radios, livestock and other farm and ranch inputs, or 65% if
procured locally - US$6.0 million; (c) 100% of the cost of internationally
recruited staff and overseas training for Ghanaians - US$0.8 million; (d) 50%
of the cost of buildings and construction materials - US$3.6 million; (e) 50%
of vehicle and plant operating costs - US$2.1 million; and (f) an unallocated
amount of US$5.6 million. Disbursements would be made against import docu-
ments, contracts and certified records of expenditure.
xi.       The majority of the Region's 125,000 farm families would benefit
from the Project, since the goods and services provided are not limited to
any one group of farmers. The annual incremental production at full devel-
opment in 1981 would be in the order of 4,000 tons of millet, 12,000 tons of
sorghum, 5,000 tons of maize, 30,000 tons of rice, 11,000 tons of groundnuts,



- iv -
15,000 tons of yams, 7,000 tons of cowpeas, 18,000 tons of seed cotton, 6,000
tons of tomatoes, and 3,000 tons of meat. Intangible benefits include im-
proved water and soil conservation, village water supplies, improved commu-
nications media and trading and social services. The net incremental for-
eign exchange earnings would be equivalent to US$23 million per annum at
full development in 1981. The internal economic rate of return is estimated
at 40%; for the ranching component the return is 31% (financial return 19%),
while that for the crop production component is 33%. The return to the animal
health component is very high at 95%. The Project would increase the average
net income to farmers by ¢90 per annum for crops and ¢80 per annum from
livestock. Current measurable family net incomes are about ¢280 per annum;
thus if Project targets are achieved family incomes from improved crop produc-
tion could be increased by 32%, thus increasing per capita income from the
current 041 to 054 per annum, representing an income growth of about 6% per
annum. Further increases due to livestock could provide an additional 3%.
xii.     On the basis of the assurances obtained during negotiations and set
out in Chapter IX, the Project would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$21.0
million.



GHANA
APPRAISAL OF UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      This report appraises an application made to the Bank Group by
the Government of Ghana for assistance in financing an agricultural devel-
opment project in Ghana's Upper Region. The Project would benefit farm
families whose cash incomes are about 0280/annum (¢41 per capita).
There are an estimated 125,000 farm families in the Region, about one mil-
lion people in all, the majority of whom would receive indirect or direct
benefits through the Project.
1.02      The Project was identified in late 1974 by a Bank mission, and
was prepared by RMWA and Ghanaian staff in early 1975. A preappraisal mis-
sion 1/ visited Ghana in June 1975, followed by an appraisal mission 2/ in
November 1975.
1.03      The Project would affect 10% of Ghana's population, and would be
an initial step to better utilize the human and agricultural resources of
the Region. The Project's principal objectives are to increase and sustain
farm production and family incomes; and its design takes account of past
Bank experience both within and outside Ghana. The production/technological
packages to be introduced to farmers have evolved through Ghanaian and other
West African research and have been tried and tested in the Upper Region
or under similar conditions elsewhere. The Project would strengthen exist-
ing agricultural, animal husbandry and veterinary services already active
in the area, and would strengthen existing institutions, including the Upper
Regional Development Corporation (URDECO) and the Agricultural Development
Bank (ADB). In addition, it would establish a commercial farm input supply
system and a radio broadcasting network.
1.04      The Project is the first broadbased agricultural development project
to be financed by the Bank in Ghana. The Project follows a number of spe-
cialized agricultural loans and credits in Ghana, including the Eastern Region
Cocoa Project, Credit 205-GH; the Sugar Rehabilitation Project, Credit 354-GH;
the Livestock Project, Credit 500-GH; the Oil Palm Project, Credit 531-GH; and
the Ashanti Cocoa Project, Loan 1181-GH. The Eastern Region Cocoa and the
Sugar Rehabilitation Projects are proceeding satisfactorily; the Livestock,
Ashanti Cocoa and Oil Palm Projects have had start-up problems, but these are
being resolved and more rapid implementation is expected.
1/   The mission comprised Messrs. Grimshaw, Hachero, Meyn (Bank), and
Calkin, Lesslie and Reader (Consultants).
2/   The mission comprised Messrs. Grimshaw, Futagami (Bank), Labarre
(Consultant) and White (ODM).



- 2 -
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      Ghana has an area of 235,000 km  (92,000 sq. miles) and a popu-
lation of about 9.5 million (in 1974), which is increasing at between 2.6%
and 3% per year. Two thirds of the population live in the humid southern
half of the country and about one third are urban dwellers. GDP was US$3.04
billion in 1973, and increased at the annual rate of about 3.1% between
1966 and 1973. Per capita GNP, which was about US$300 in 1973, is stagnating,
2.02      The economy is based on the export of a limited number of agricul-
tural and mineral products. Agriculture, forestry and fisheries employ 60%
of the population and contribute 40% of GDP. Nearly half of the balance is
provided by transport and services. Cocoa is the main export commodity,
generating 62% of export earnings.
2.03      Ghana's economic problems are dominated by a balance of payments
deficit and a continuing budgetary imbalance. General neglect of agricul-
ture during most of the 1960s resulted in rapidly growing food imports. The
industrial development strategy of that time broadened the industrial base
but generally had low domestic value-added content, limited employment poten-
tial, and depended heavily on imported inputs. The imbalance in external
payments resulted in a heavy accumulation of debt (both short and medium
term) and in recurring balance of payments crises. The economic and social
policy of the early 1960s involved Government in providing a wide range of
social services, in setting up a large number of state enterprises and in
allocating resources through an extensive system of physical and financial
controls. Since the mid-1960s, there has been a growing imbalance in public
finances due to rapid growth of budget current expenditures, including un-
economic operations of a large number of Government enterprises and a slow
growth in budget revenues.
B. Agricultural Sector
General
2.04      Performance in the agricultural sector has been unsatisfactory.  In
the early 1960s, Government intervened in production activities by investing
heavily in large capital-intensive state farms, but gave little help to the
private sector, mainly smallholders, which produces more than 90% of agricul-
tural production. The state farming strategy which was almost completely
abandoned has not been replaced by an effective program to increase smallholder
productivity. Consequently, agricultural production has generally failed to
keep pace with food demand, and Ghana imports many food and agricultural
commodities that it could produce and exports far less than the country's
agricultural potential would permit.



- 3 -
2.05      To stimulate rapid expansion in agricultural production and reduce
dependence on agricultural imports, Government has introduced a number of
farmer incentives. At the primary level, northern Ghanaian farmers in par-
ticular are provided with highly subsidized fertilizers, improved seed, and
mechanical equipment hire services, backed by an attractive guaranteed price
for paddy and a very high guaranteed price (equivalent to 30 US cents per
pound seed cotton) for cotton. Investment incentives are also provided
in the form of credit at subsidized interest from the Agricultural Develop-
ment Bank, import tariff concessions on farm equipment, tax holidays, and
guarantees by the Bank of Ghana to commercial banks' lending for agriculture.
At the secondary level there is a multiplicity of boards, corporations and
agencies involved directly or indirectly in production, processing and mar-
keting; many of these overlap functionally because of the absence of a well-
planned and coordinated national agricultural development policy.
2.06      While the principal objectives'desc'ribed above, to increase produc-
tion and provide the farming community with higher incomes, are commendable,
their implementation is causing difficulties. First, by subsidizing both
inputs and production (with notable exception of cocoa), an increasingly
greater fiscal burden is being placed on Government, which already suffers
a serious imbalance in public finances (para 2.03). Second, the policies
generally have favored larger farmers. This is because while some programs
have been deliberately directed towards larger farmers (e.g., mechanical
rice growing in the Northern Region), the structure and management of most
programs are so weak that the aggressive larger farmer with his credit-
worthiness and ability to deal with official and commercial institutions is
able to secure the great bulk of the limited farm inputs available. Third,
the subsidy structure is so favorable that operations that would be economicaly
and financially nonviable at market prices can be sustained.
2.07      Implementing the Project within this environment will depend, among
other factors, on adequate management of the boards and other agencies
participating, and on changes in the present subsidy arrangements to enable
rational production decisions to be made and to provide proper incentives for
involving the commercial sector with distribution and sales of farm inputs.
2.08      Fertilizer would be the main input supplied under the Project.  Pre-
viously thesubsidy for fertilizer coveredabout 88% of the real farmgate value;
in 1974, for example, sulphate of ammonia was sold in the Upper Region at ¢40
per ton, compared to a real cost of about 0340 per ton, and the much higher
value compound fertilizer 15:15:15 sold at only 456 per ton. 1/ The relation-
ships between subsidies and farm incomes are shown in the model farm budgets
1/   These subsidies are even greater if the real value of the cedi is taken
into account, i.e., a shadow exchange rate of 01.5-02.5, compared to the
official rate, of ¢1.15 per US$.



- 4 -
at Annex 2.   These show that the full market value of fertilizer used could
be paid by farmers from increased production. However, changes in the fertil-
izer subsidy cannot be considered in isolation; the consequences of such
changes on the rate of adoption of fertilizer by smallholders must be con-
sidered. The latter is less a direct question of production efficiencies
than one of the smallholder's perception of the merit of the debt incurred
in fertilizer in comparison with anticipated future returns. The lower the
fertilizer price the greater the level of 'first time' adoption. Also, there
is the problem that the total removal of or substantial reduction in the sub-
sidy may bankrupt many large farmers and disrupt food supplies, particularly
rice. Government has very recently agreed on a policy that will allow for
the complete phasing out of fertilizer subsidies over the five-year period
1977-1981. The first step will be carried out in December 1976 when Govern-
ment will publicly set the 1977 farmgate price for fertilizer; this price
will be equivalent to an increase- of -about 150%- -over current prices, which
for the Upper Region would reflect a subsidy equivalent to 58%.
2.09      In commodity pricing Government has followed a pragmatic approach
that in some cases, while meeting an immediate need, would appear to create
problems for the future. Thus the current cotton price, which is undoubted-
ly encouraging production, could in the medium to long term create serious
overproduction and fiscal difficulties. The current price of ~0.35 per pound
for seed cotton is very high (85% higher than in neighboring Upper Volta).
Although inflation will gradually erode this price, it is doubtful whether
it is justified at the present time.
2.10      The livestock subsector is subject to retail price controls in the
interest of consumer protection. Retail prices for beef, mutton and goat meat
are fixed. Poultry and pig prices are free, as is the internal market for
live animals. Prices presently paid to foreign suppliers for live animals are
not competitive with those offered by other importers (Ivory Coast, Nigeria),
and deliveries of slaughterstock to Ghana virtually came to a halt in May
1975. Meanwhile, local slaughterstock prices have increased, and a parallel
market on which butchers sell meat at somewhat higher than official prices has
developed. Current meat pricing policies are not conducive to stimulating
production or to substituting for imports.   Local producers should receive
the benefit of free market prices for meat as an incentive for investing in
livestock.
2.11      There are a number of other subsidies complicating the agricultural
sector that make rationalization difficult and could result in unforeseen
consequences. Because of the complex relationship between produce prices and
production costs it is important for Government to determine medium and long
term polices on pricing and subsidies.  During negotiations, assurances were
obtained that by June 30, 1977, a paper setting out Government's agricul-
tural pricing and subsidy policy would be prepared and submitted to the Bank
for comment.



The Upper Region
2.12      Regional imbalance has occurred in the past with the north and west
benefitting least from the development effort. From 1971 until 1974 the
Upper Region's share of the Ministry of Agriculture's budget was 3%, although
it has 10% of the country's population. However, in 1975 the Region's share
increased to about 14% of the total due to large commitments for the Tono
Irrigation Scheme. Government's agricultural development policies for the
Region have generally favored the larger farms, both private and State, and
expensive irrigation schemes. Until recently, little has been done for the
small farmer; and, unfortunately, poor implementation of those projects that
have been initiated has resulted in high cost and, in many instances, nonpro-
ductive operations that have little or no impact on the majority of the
Region's population. Although quantitative data are not available, evidence
suggests that income growth among the majority of farmers is probably declining.
2.13      The Region is endowed with a hardworking community of small farmers
that has developed an ability to survive in a steadily declining environment
caused by increasing population, onchocerciasis and other health hazards, de-
clining soil fertility, worsening soil erosion, sporadic lack of water, and
the absence of proper infrastructure and services. Their ability to survive
is closely associated with a husbandry system of intricate mixed cropping
that is designed to give maximum insurance against vagaries of climate and
disease. The Project described in this report would exploit to the farmer's
benefit these traditional disciplines.
C. Education and Communications
2.14      Education and communication problems which are unique to the Upper
Region require special attention. The Region has the lowest literacy rate in
the country (about 10%). There is a multiplicity of different languages with
no generaly acceptable or understood lingua franca; neither radio programs
nor newspapers are available in languages that are widely understood.
2.15      The Project would not attempt to modify the formal education sys-
tem, but would embody a pilot adult literacy scheme through which partici-
pants would achieve a sufficient understanding of reading, writing and sim-
ple calculation. Earlier literacy schemes in the Region have failed and the
more recent concept of 'functional' literacy as an integral part of rural
development, oriented towards immediate practical application in agriculture,
is not fully understood, nor have its methods been tried in Ghana. However,
the Institute of Linguistics in Tamale has had considerable success with
a pilot literacy program in one of the languages of the Northern Region,
and a similar scheme would be conducted by the Project, to determine the
best and most economic solution for the Upper Region.



- 6 -
2.16      Because of poor communications media, a continuous two-way process
is required to guide and educate the population, including Project staff.
Radio could provide rapid and continuing contact in an area where so many
languages are spoken and so few people can read. The Project would rely
heavily on improved radio communication and broadcasting for its development
phase and, more importantly, to sustain long term development requirements.
D. Institutions
2.17      Ghana has many institutions which have been established to replace
or compete with private enteprise; most agricultural institutions operate
inefficiently. Some of the institutions that would be associated with the
Project are described below, and a chart at Annex 3, Schedule 3, indicates
their linkage.
2.18      The Ministry of Agriculture consists of the Departments of Crop
Extension, Animal Husbandry, Veterinary, Mechanization, Fisheries, Irriga-
tion, Training, Planning and Information. Until recently a Director of
Agriculture in Accra controlled all departments. At a regional level, an
Assistant Director was responsible to the Director for coordination of the
various services offered by the Ministry. Beginning fiscal year 1976 the
post of Director was abolished and now each department has its own head who
is responsible for its operation. There are no assistant directors, and the
Regional Commissioners, appointed by NRC, perform a coordinating role. In
the Upper Region coordination is good and the individual regional departments
work well together.
2.19      Upper Regional Development Corporation (URDECO) was established
as a corporate body under NRC Decree No. 140, dated December 29, 1972, and
started operation on May 1, 1973. Commissioned to enter into "any viable
commerial industry," URDECO is engaged in a variety of activities in the
Upper Region, including retailing essential food commodities, providing
transport and agricultural mechanized services, raising livestock and pro-
ducing food and cash crops. These varied activities have strained URDECO's
manpower and financial resources considerably. Under the Project, URDECO
would give up its directly productive agricultural enterprises and concentrate
on transportation and wholesaling. Its farm mechanization services would be
transferred to the Project's Farmers Services Company.
2.20      The Agricultural Development Bank (ADB) is the main source of
agricultural credit in Ghana. When the Sugar Rehabilitation Project was
approved in 1971 it was recognized that ADB's performance needed improve-
ment, particularly as its lending operations were characterized by sub-
stantial uncollected loars. Consultants were financed under the credit
to review and improve credit management and financial control. The con-
sultants completed their recommendations at the end of 1973 and implement-
ation began in 1974. In November 1974 the contract, to be financed under



the Oil Palm Project, was extended for one year to ensure effectle iL-
plementation of the new procedures. There appears to be little evidence
as yet of any improvement in loan recovery. As of December 31, 1974,
about 60% of its loans were past due. Debt recovery could be improved
if greater pressure were exerted on defaulters, and if loans were made only
to those farmers who received adequate extension and farm input services.
III. PROJECT AREA
A. General
2
3.01      The Project area (details at Annex 4) covers 27,300 km , about
11% of Ghana's total land area. It comprises the whole of the Upper Re-
gion which borders Upper Volta to the west and north, and Togo to the east
(see map). Bolgatanga, the Regional capital, is about 800 km from Accra.
3.02      Annual rainfall varies from 910 mm in the east to 1,050 mm in the
west, and is largely confined to a six-month period from April to September.
Distribution is suitable for annual crop production though a dry spell in
June or July can adversely affect crop yields.
3.03      Soils (see map) have developed mainly from granites and are shal-
low, coarse-textured and concretionary over much of the area; settlement is
heavily concentrated on the deeper, finer soils towards the eastern and west-
ern boundaries. Sheet erosion is serious and is aggravated by destroyed or
neglected soil conservation measures; much of the latter has occurred since
1962 when Government agricultural policy was concentrated on large scale
mechanized farming operations. Most of the fertile valleys are unsettled
due to their heavier soils and the presence of simulium flies, the vector
of riverblindness.
3.04      The area has an estimated population of 930,000, with some 125,000
farm families. Settlement is in the form of scattered compounds that com-
bine to form loosely knit villages. Nine separate languages are spoken,
though none serves as a lingua franca. It is customary for farmers to group
together for specific farming activities. The Project would exploit this
traditional grouping whereby extension services would encourage the forma-
tion of informal farmers associations through which extension and other
services would operate.
3.05      The area's road network is sufficient in quantity but deficient in
quality. Under the recently approved Bank Loan (1182-GH)/IDA Credit (594-GH)
for Ghana Highways II Project, at least 800 km of feeder road  n the Upper
Region would be improved between 1977 and 1980. Health and education
facilities are hardly adequate, and access to water is unsatisfactory during
the dry season; a Canadian (CIDA) financed village borehole program is cur-
rently being implemented.



- 8 -
B. Agriculture
3.06      Agriculture is almost entirely in the hands of smallholder culti-
vators who combine bush farm cultivation of distant fields with permanent
cultivation of nearby compound farms. Mixed farming and px cultivation
were introduced in the eastern sector in the 1930s, and about 8,000 trained
pairs are presently in the area.
3.07      Average farm size is estimated at 2.4 hectares and is mainly planted
to food crops of which millet and sorghum are the most important. Principal
cash crops are groundnuts, rice and more recently cotton. Cowpeas and bam-
bara nuts are important legumes both for consumption and sale. Mixed crop-
ping is almost universally practiced. There is little surplus production
from the area; the crop production complex is in a sensitive balance with
nature. Adverse climatic conditions are accompanied by sometimes serious
food shortages leading to undernourishment, which can be particularly serious
during the dry season. Increased production and improved onfarm storage under
the Project would be accompanied by a significant increase in Regional food
consumption.
3.08      The Region carries the majority of Ghana's cattle population,
300,000 head. Very little improvement has been undertaken in regard to
better management and feeding of stock. However, the Veterinary Department
is fairly active and, with assistance under the Project, could adequately
service animal health requirements. There are large areas of land available
for ranching (Annex 5).
C. Farm Services
3.09      Farm services are limited.  The Agricultural Development Bank and
commercial banks provide credit almost exclusively to larger farmers. The
Ministry of Agriculture, with the assistance of German aid, distributes
limited amounts of fertilizer and provides very sketchy extension services.
The Cotton Development Board provides farm supplies for cotton production
and is responsible for marketing the crop. Various institutions buy farm
products at seemingly guaranteed floor prices, but only when funds are avail-
able. Outside the main towns there appears to be a marked absence of trading
in consumer goods, reflecting the poverty of the area and the nonavailability
of many essential items. Under the Project, the regional services of the
Ministry of Agriculture would be confined to providing farm extension, animal
health, land development and training for crop and livestock farmers. Its
current role in farm input distribution would be transferred to a Project-
established supply company (the Farmers Services Company (UR) Ltd.).



- 9 -
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Description
4.01      The Project has two principal objectives:  first, to increase agri-
cultural production and thus farm incomes; and second, to establish permanent
farmer support services. The Project would do this by redefining the role of
agricultural service and intensifying extension for crop and animal produc-
tion and human nutrition; by developing associated physical infrastructure,
including an intensive effort to halt the rapid rate of soil erosion; and
by developing specialized institutions to service the commercial and finan-
cial requirements of the Region's farmers.   The Project would be carried
out over a five-year investment period, 1976/77 - 1980/81, and would involve:
Farm Development
-    establishing at a rate of about 30 per year, 90 service
centers, to provide improved extension services to small
farmers and management advice to a relatively few larger
farmers. The service centers would each serve about
1,300 farm families;
-    providing farm inputs, including insecticides, seed
dressings, improved seeds, fertilizers, spare parts,
farm equipment, oxen, etc., for cash, seasonal deferred
payments, and medium and long term loans, to increase
production of about 108,000 ha of land (30% of total arable)
currently under cultivation, including traditional mixed
millet and sorghum, dwarf sorghum, maize, groundnuts,
cotton, rice, cowpeas, and tomatoes;
-    providing improved onfarm grain storage;
-    providing better animal health services and improved
animal husbandry, and establishing ten small (2,000 ha)
ranches for local livestock owners;
-    developing seven applied research and demonstration units
together with an associated seed multiplication program;
-    establishing a pilot functional literacy scheme;
-    expanding Ghana Broadcasting Corporation's facilities
through a radio network and regional studies to broadcast
in three of the most important languages of the Region;
-    improving human nutrition and health through agricultural
extension services, the functional literacy program and
radio; and



- 10 -
providing inservice training for Project staff at the
existing Navrongo Agricultural Institute, providing funds
and travel grants for visits to selected projects and
schemes for specialized management training outside
Ghana.
Physical Infrastructure
-    constructing about 120 new small dams and rehabilitating
about 100 existing dams, together with the associated
structural works to enable the development of dry season
gardening, fruit tree nurseries, and fish ponds; to provide
water for cattle, and to improve water conservation;
-    developing soil conservation measures to protect some
160,000 ha of farm land (i.e., 30% of total arable)
including catchment areas of dams;
-    constructing 700 village wells;
-    constructing stores and offices at farm service centers,
district and regional Project offices, and houses for
Project staff;
-    providing vehicles and other equipment; and
-    constructing two cotton ginneries.
Institutional Support
-    establishing the Upper Region Agricultural Development
Project Management Unit (URPMU), to be responsible for
implementing the Project; in addition to coordinating
and administering the Ministry of Agriculture's existing
services in the Region, URPMU would develop other ser-
vices to meet the demands of the Project;
-    establishing the Farmers Services Company (UR) Ltd.;
-    strengthening the regional branch of the Agricultural
Development Bank (ADB); and
-    strengthening the Upper Regional Development Corpora-
tion (URDECO), particularly its transport and wholesale
divisions.



- ii -
Development Planning
-    establishing, under direct control of the Project,
a special Onchocerciasis Survey Unit to prepare, in
consultation with the Coordinating Committee of the
National Onchocerciasis Program of Ghana, a Ten-
Year Development Plan and feasibility studies for
investment projects for areas freed from onchocerciasis
in the Upper and Northern Regions of Ghana.
B. Detailed Features
Farm Development
4.02      Service Centers.  The Project would establish, at the rate of 30
per year, 90 service centers to be located in strategic positions through-
out the Region. Each center would serve some 1,300 farmers within a radius
of 5-6 miles, and would be the focal point for farmers groups or associa-
tions, agricultural extension, nutrition and health services, marketing,
rural engineering (blacksmiths), maintenance of farm machinery and equip-
ment, and the supply of inputs. Initially, centers would vary in size; for
example, those located near the major mechanized rice areas would have greater
storage capacity for fertilizers, would provide fuel and tractor spares, and
would have special farm management advisors; whereas those located adjacent
to existing onchocerciasis areas would be smaller, but would expand as oncho-
cerciasis is eliminated and people move back to the valleys (Annex 6).
4.03      Farm Inputs.  The Project, through the Farmers Services Company
(UR) Ltd. (FSC), would provide inputs recommended by URPMU's extension staff.
Seasonal inputs would be available for cash or on a deferred payment basis.
Loans for oxdrawn equipment and oxen would, on the advice of URPMU, be pro-
vided through the regional branch of ADB. Details of farm development re-
sulting from these inputs are at Annex 2.
4.04      Farm Grain Storage would be improved using simple methods, already
established in the Region, that provide for improving existing traditional
storage systems through provision of cement for plastering grain store
walls, and of a concrete slab to seal the top of the store. Ten percent
of the farmers (12,000) should benefit; the improvement program would be
supervised by the Department of Social Welfare and Community Development
(Annex 9).
4.05      Animal Health Services.  The Project would strengthen veterinary
services by providing vaccinations against rinderpest, contagious bovine
pleuropneumonia, blackleg and anthrax, and brucellosis, and regular treat-
ment against internal parasitic infestation of sheep, goats and calves; the
necessary facilities, including cattle handling pens, to support these ac-
tions would also be provided (Annex 5).



- 12 -
4.06      Ranch Development.  Ten 2,000 ha ranches would be developed near
Tumu (see map) which would be owned and managed by individuals or groups of
cattle owners (to be established under the Companies Code) residing in the
Region, particularly in the west. The ranches would produce cattle for sale
locally or to the meat processing plant at Bolgatanga. Land is readily avail-
able, and would be acquired by the Project and leased to the ranching com-
panies; during negotiations assurances were obtained that prior to ranch
development and as a condition of each ranch loan the land would be acquired
by URPMU and leased for a minimum of 45 years to the participants on terms
and conditions satisfactory to the Bank (Annex 5).
4.07      Seed Multiplication and Research.  The Project would establish,
one at each district agricultural station, seven applied Research and Demon-
stration Units (RDU), to be used primarily for adapting research results,
obtained by the Crop Research Institute's main stations at Kumasi, Manga
and Tamale, to Project conditions. In particular, RDUs would test out rec-
ommended varieties under local management systems, including mixed cropping,
using traditional cultivation methods and normal application rates for fer-
tilizer and insecticides. RDUs would also be used for second stage seed
multiplication and would work closely with FSC in developing with farmers
a certified seed production scheme (Annex 7).
4.08      Radio Broadcasting would comprise establishing an FM radio network
and supporting services; it would be designed to motivate active participa-
tion in the Project; provide community news; stimulate emulation and spread
experience; provide views, criticisms and suggestions, and responses from
farmers; reflect the cultural life of the people; promote mutual awareness
and understanding among linguistic groups; and lend systematic and continuous
support to inservice training of field staff.
4.09      The main station would be at Bolgatanga; because of the large
Project area it would be supported by transmission stations (1 KW) at Tumu,
Lawra, Wa and Bawku. Vehicles and recording equipment would be provided to
enable staff to obtain local material for broadcasting and regular feedback
from farmers. Twenty-two thousand receiving sets would be sold by FSC for
cash or credit. The network would initially broadcast in the three major
Regional languages and English; program content would be determined during
Project implementation, but preliminary proposals include 86 hours per week,
of which 55 would be locally produced, the remaining time being used to relay
national programs from Accra. During negotiations assurances were obtained
that a radio broadcasting engineer, with qualifications satisfactory to the
Bank, would be retained, to prepare, in consultation with GBC, specifications
and tender documents for FM transmission, and to supervise procurement and
installation of broadcasting equipment. (Details at Annex 8).
4.10      Functional Literacy.  The Project would include a functional liter-
acy pilot scheme based on the teaching of information immediately usable during
Project implementation (e.g., agricultural advice, payment documents related to
farm inputs, market receipts, fertilizer bag labels). The scheme would com-
mence in the second year and would involve some sixty classes grouping 1,500
participants in the areas served by four service centers; half of the classes



- 13 -
would be in Dagaari and half in Gurenne speaking areas where almost 70% of the
Region's population lives. Literacy would be provided first in these local
languages and ultimately in English. Particular attention would be paid to
selection of instructors and participants by the villagers themelves, and
close liaison would be maintained with the Bureau of Ghana Languages and the
Institute of Linguistics at Tamale in drawing up programs and preparing
materials (Annex 8).
4.11      Human Nutrition.  The Project would recognize the importance of human
nutrition and health and would provide a specialized nutritionist to develop
simple nutritional programs using agricultural extension workers, functional
adult literacy classes, and radio.
4.12      Inservice Training.  The present ineffective training program for
farmers' sons at the Navrongo Agricultural Institute would be discontinued,
and a series of training courses for Project staff would be introduced. Most
of the courses would be for one to two weeks, would concentrate on a monodis-
cipline approach, and would include simple courses for low level service
center technical staff and salesmen operating FSC stores, to more complex
courses for selected higher level staff in farm management, land planning,
accounting, etc. (Annex 8).
Physical Infrastructure
4.13      Water Development.  The Project would 3construct 120 small earth
dams, each with a minimum capacity of 120,000 m , and would rehabilitate
about 100 (50%) of the existing dams. In each case, particular attention
woull be given to the catchment area of the dam (for new ones, a maximum of
5 km ), spillway design and availability of downstream irrigable land.
Because of the flexibility required in the construction and rehabilitation
of small dams, the work would be undertaken on force account by URPMU.
Small contractors would be used by URPMU on an ad hoc basis (Annex 9).
4.14      It is estimated that the average dam would have surplus capacity
to irrigate an average of 5 ha, and that about 750 ha of land would be
irrigated under the Project. The irrigation program would be simple and
would allow for dry 2season gardening; most farmers would probably cultivate
not more than 100 m ; some 7,500 farmers could participate. Small irrigation
canals would be constructed by hand on each side of the valley below the dams.
Approximately 500 m of canal would be required for each site. Leveling and
bunding would be the responsibility of the farmers (Annex 9).
4.15      There is potential for inland fisheries development, although few
quantitative data are available. It is proposed under the Project that a
series of fish pond complexes be constructed on a pilot basis in associa-
tion with suitable sites and dams (Annex 9).
4.16      To complement an ongoing Ghanaian/CIDA village borehole scheme,
the Project would provide materials for construction, on a self help basis,



- 14 -
of about 700 village wells. Each well would be suitably lined, sealed and
fitted with a pump. The Department of Social Welfare and Community Develop-
ment would oversee construction (Annex 9).
4.17      Soil Conservation.  The Project would undertake measures to pro-
tect a minimum of 160,000 ha of farm land from further serious soil erosion.
Priority would be given to conserving the catchment areas of existing and
new dams. Conservation works would include the construction of cut-off
drains, narrow base contour ditches, waterways, rehabilitation of gulleys,
and general agricultural practices including realignment of ridges, tie
ridging, grass stripping, etc.; tree planting would also be encouraged.
Major conservation works would be constructed by URPMU using machinery pro-
vided under the Project, and URPMU and the farmers would undertake minor
works either individually or in groups (Annex 9).
4.18      Project Buildings.  Offices for URPMU and FSC would be constructed in
Bolgatanga and at each of seven district administrative centers. Very simple
offices would be constructed at the 90 service centers. To provide an incen-
tive for staff to work in the Upper Region, houses would be constructed at
various locations. Prior to construction, Project staff would be housed in
rented accommodation.
4.19      Vehicles and Equipment, ranging from four-wheel drive vehicles for
senior extension officers to motorcycles and bicycles for service center
staff, would be provided (Annex 11).
4.20      Cotton Ginneries.  Two cotton ginneries and associated storage
would be constructed at Wa and Zwarungu. Each would have a minimum through-
put of 9,000 tons seed cotton per annum. The gins would be owned and operated
by the Cotton Development Board (Annex 1).
4.21      Maintenance of dams and soil conservation works would be the respon-
sibility of Project-established Farmers Associations. Farmers are aware of
the problems resulting from non maintenance, and with better communications
and income incentives, regular maintenance is expected. The Project would
be responsible for maintaining buildings, vehicles and equipment.
Institutional Support
4.22      The Project would establish the Farmers Services Company (UR)
Ltd. to provide supplies and mechanical hire services to farmers. Funds
would be provided for employment of specialized staff, and for provision of
equipment, buildings, and working capital (Annex 6).
4.23      The Project would strengthen the regional branch of the Agricul-
tural Development Bank at Bolgatanga through provision of an experienced
agricultural loans officer and vehicles (Annex 6).
4.24      As a servicing organization, the Upper Regional Development Corpora-
tion (URDECO) would have an important role through the provision of transport



- 15 -
services and wholesale supplies for essential commodities which could be
retailed through FSC outlets. The Project would strengthen URDC by providing
an experienced financial controller to facilitate better management and
accounting control.
4.25      The Project would strengthen the Ministry of Agriculture by estab-
lishing a Project Management Unit (URPMU) to coordinate and administer the
Project.  Apart from improving existing functions of the Ministry, it would
develop three new units: a Land Development Division, responsible for land
planning and soil and water conservation; a Department of Planning, Evalua-
tion, and Monitoring; and a Department of Communications and Training. De-
tails at Annex 3.
4.26      Development Planning.  Currently a program to eradicate Simulium
damnosum, the vector of onchocerciasis (riverblindness), is being undertaken in
seven West African countries, including the Upper and Northern Regions of
-Ghafia.  Recently a joint FAO/IBRD mission proposed the preparation of a Ten-
Year Development Plan including investment studies.  The Project outlined
in this report is likely to cover much of the eradicated areas in the Upper
Region and would provide land development and other services to areas freed
of simulium. To ensure proper funding of the planning and feasibility work,
a special Onchocerciasis Unit would be established by the Project's Depart-
ment of Planning, Evaluation and Monitoring. This Unit would cover both the
Upper and Northern Regions and would be supported by consultants (nine man-
years) and staff from Planning Units of the Ministries of Agriculture and
Economic Development. Plans produced by the Unit, in consultation with the
Coordinating Committee of the National Onchocerciasis Program of Ghana, would
comply with guidelines established by the Ministry of Economic Development;
the latter would have access to the Unit and would retain responsibility for
final approval of such plans (Annex 10).

5.03      Costs are incremental based on prices ruling June 1, 1975, and
exclude all identifiable import duties on goods imported directly for the
Project. Physical contingencies comprise 5% of baseline costs and price
contingencies allow for compounded increases in costs of: (a) vehicles,
plant, livestock and farm inputs (excluding fertilizers) and vehicle operating
costs of 16% in 1976, 14% in 1977, 7-1/2% in 1978, and 7% from 1979; (b)
houses, buildings, furnishings and construction materials for dams, wells and
grain storage facilities of 22% in 1976, 18% in 1977, 12% in 1978 and 1979,
and 11% in 1980; and (c) salaries for staff and general administrative and
operating costs of 10-1/2% in 1976 and 7% annually thereafter. Price con-
tingencies are not included for fertilizers, as Bank projections indicate that
average 1980 current prices should not be higher than the June 1975 baseline
prices. Analysis of baseline costs show 71% for production related investments
and 29% for Government support services and planning. Contingencies are
equivalent to 27% of total costs.
B. Proposed Financing
5.04      The Bank Group's contribution would be a loan ("third window") of
US$21.0 million at 4.85%, repayable over 25 years including a seven-year
grace period for principal during which interest and commitment charges would
be paid. This would be equivalent to 38% of total costs and would cover 65%
of foreign exchange costs. Thirty-four percent of foreign costs, 012.7 mil-
lion (US$11.0 million) (20% of total costs) would be funded by the United
Kingdom under a parallel financing agreement comprising a grant for about 50
manyears of technical assistance of approximately f Stg 1.0 million and an
interest-free loan of f Stg 4.0 million 1/ repayable over 25 years including a
seven-year grace period. The remaining 1% of foreign costs, 00.6 million
(US$0.5 million) would be financed from Project-induced other local foreign
exchange earnings.
5.05      The Government of Ghana would finance 25%, amounting to V15.9 million
(US$13.7 million). The remaining 17%, 410.2 million (US$8.9 million), would be
financed by a consortium of Ghanaian financial institutions including the Bank
of Ghana, Agricultural Development Bank, National Investment Bank and the
commercial banks.
5.06      The Bank loan would be made to Government, which would dispose of
the funds as follows:
(a)  04.4 million (US$3.8 million) would be onlent to FSC at
8.85% for 25 years, with seven years grace for principal,
to purchase incremental seasonal farm inputs;

UK manufactured goods which at the time of cost estimation, June 1975, were
valued, including contingencies, at US$8.7 million, when E Stg 1 was equiv-
valent to US$2.2.

and 06.8 million (US$5.9 million) for fertilizers, to be paid to the Cotton
Development Board and Farmers Services Company respectively. Details of the
proposed Project financing are at Annex 11, Table 12, and are summarized as
follows:
