5.11      It was agreed that retroactive financing of up to US$750,000
(¢0.825 million) would be made available from May 1, 1976 for employment of
key staff, purchase of related support vehicles and equipment, and con-
struction of houses and offices at Bolgatanga.
C. Procurement
5.12      Procurement of radio telephones, broadcasting equipment, land
development heavy plant and equipment, cotton ginnery equipment and build-
ings, four-wheel drive vehicles, trucks, spare parts, pumps and pipes for
wells, cotton sprayers, insecticides, and fungicides, totaling 010.0 million
(US$8.7 million) would be under UK procurement procedures that would be com-
petitive with international prices (Annex 11, Table 10). Procurement of
other vehicles, animal drugs, equipment, transistor radios, aerial photography,
fertilizers, pesticides, tools, livestock and other farm and ranch equipment
under contracts with a value of more than US$50,000 would be through inter-
national competitive bidding (ICB) in accordance with Bank guidelines. Such
procurement is estimated to have a value of 015.2 million (US$13.2 million),
and a maximum limit totaling US$1.0 million would be set for those items
purchased through contracts of less than US$50,000 under local procedures
satisfactory to the Bank. Domestically manufactured goods would be allowed a
15% preference when comparing domestic bids with those of foreign manufac-
turers. Contracts for the construction of buildings, houses, and the purchase
of construction materials and furnishings valued at 010.5 million (US$9.1
million) would not be attractive to foreign contractors due to the dispersed
location of the units and the small size of individual contracts; for these
items, contracts would be awarded on the basis of competitive bidding adver-
tised locally and in accordance with procedures satisfactory to the Bank; in
these cases foreign firms and suppliers would not be precluded from bidding.
It is likely that the construction of service center buildings and village
wells would be under the supervision of the Technical Division of the Ministry
of Social Welfare and Community Development, who may use petty contractors or
force account. Construction of small dams and soil conservation works would
be undertaken by URPMU on force account. An estimated 012.0 million (US$10.4
million) would be for incremental labor, staff salaries, allowances, hiring of
consultants, vehicle maintenance and operation, overseas training and general
administrative costs that would be unsuitable for competitive bidding. The
remaining C17.0 million (US$14.6 million) of estimated Project costs would be
for contingencies. Ghana has no preferential import tariff arrangements.



- 22 -
5.13      To protect the Project from procurement delays, assurances were
obtained that within two months of loan effectiveness Government would take
such action required to ensure that the goods imported directly under the
Project would be exempt from any import restrictions and duties.
D. Disbursement
5.14      The proceeds of the Bank loan (US$21.0 million) would be disbursed
over five years to cover 38% of total Project costs against the following
categories:
Category 1.  100% of the CIF costs of directly imported vehicles and equip-
ment (other than farm equipment), sawgins and associated equipment, veter-
inary drugs, and aerial photography (or 86% of the cost of these items
if locally procured), totaling US$2.9 million (43.4 million);
Category 2. 100% of the CIF cost of directly imported fertilizers,
oxdrawn equipment, batteries, transistor radios, livestock and other
ranch and farm equipment (or 65% of the cost of these items if procured
locally), totaling US$6.0 million (46.9 million);
Category 3. 100% of the cost of internationally recruited staff,
including their salaries, allowances and recruitment costs, and
overseas training for local staff, totaling US$0.8 million
(01.0 million);
Category 4. 50% of the cost of buildings (excluding buildings for
FSC and CDB), including furnishings, and construction materials
for dams, soil conservation, wells and grain stores, totaling
US$3.6 million (t4.1 million);
Category 5.  50% of vehicle and plant operating costs (excluding FSC),
totaling US$2.1 million (02.4 million); and
Category 6. An unallocated amount totaling US$5.6 million
(06.4 million).
5.15      Disbursements would be against import documentation, contracts,
and, for Category 5, certified records of expenditure. For disbursement
made against certified records of expenditures, documentation would be
retained by Project agencies for review by Bank/UK supervision missions.
Any surplus funds would be cancelled.



- 23 -
E. Budgetary Control, Funding Procedures,
Accounts and Audits
5.16      Budgets.  Annual budgets would be prepared by URPMU and FSC for
approval by URPMU's Executive Committee and FSC's Board, respectively.
URPMU and FSC would submit monthly cash flow statements to their respective
chairmen for approval.
5.17      Funding Procedures.  Ghana Government would set up an URPMU account
with a commercial bank in Bolgatanga with initial funds of 03.0 million.
Government would arrange for suitable overdraft facilities for URPMU,
guaranteed by Government, to an amount, reflected by the approved URPMU
annual budget, of up to 03 million. The above requirement would be a condition
of loan effectiveness.
5.18      Subsidies for farm inputs (para 2.08) would be reduced.  Project
costs and FSC cash flows are prepared on a gross cost basis, and assurances
were obtained during negotiations that Government would annually reimburse
FSC on the basis of an agreed formula (Annex 6, Appendix 1, Annexures 1 and 2)
for the cost of selling inputs at subsidized rates; that such payments would be
made as and when required on the basis of the annual sales budget; and that CDB
would be adequately funded to cover the subsidy payment on seed cotton.
5.19      Accounts and Audits.  Separate Project-related accounts for URPMU,
FSC, ADB, GBC, and CDB would be maintained under acceptable commercial
accounting procedures, and would be designed to provide cost accounting and
Project evaluation data. All accounts would be audited annually by private
independent auditors approved by the Auditor-General and acceptable to the
Bank. Audited accounts, balance sheets and operating statements would be
submitted to the Bank within four months of the end of the financial year.
Assurances to these effects were obtained during negotiations.
VI. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Institutions
6.01      Successful Project implementation would require changes in the
administrative arrangements for agriculture and rural development now in
existance in the Upper Region. The Project would be the first of its kind
in Ghana and would require good management and careful control. Project
actions would affect or involve many Government Ministries, departments
and corporations; for the Project to succeed a high degree of cooperation
would have to be assured. The greater part of the Project would consist of
activities falling within the purview of the Ministry of Agriculture, and



- 24 -
consequently primary responsibility for the Project would rest with the
Commissioner for Agriculture. Determination of Project policies, financial
control of URPMU and certain administrative powers would be delegated by the
Commissioner for Agriculture to the Upper Region Agricultural Development
Project Executive Committee (URADPEC) which would be chaired by the Regional
Commissioner. Day to day responsibility for implementing the Project would be
further delegated, to a Project Manager who would head URPMU. URADPEC and
URPMU, with terms of reference acceptable to the Bank (Annex 3, Schedule
1), have been formally established by Government. Creation of this "new
level" within the structure of Government would cut across the existing chains
of command that now run from departmental headquarters in Accra to regional
representatives of these departments located in the Upper Region. To ensure
that the Project would benefit fully from the experiences of the departments,
that Project actions would comply with overall departmental policies, and that
there would be "feed back" from the Project to the departments, two specialized
committees would be established on which senior members of departmental
headquarters would serve: first, a Project Coordinating Committee (PCC) that
would deal principally with administrative and social issues at a regional
level; and second, a Project Technical Committee (PTC) that would concern
itself with agricultural and related matters. These two committees, whose
composition and activities are spelled out in more detail in paras 6.04 and
6.05, would provide support to URPMU, and their findings and recommendations
would be made available routinely to URADPEC, which would be responsible for
resolving any differences in views between URPMU and the Committees.
Government has established URPMU and URADPEC under terms of reference
and with responsibilities acceptable to the Bank. (Annex 3, Schedule 1).
6.02      Upper Region Agricultural Development Project Executive Committee
would, inter alia, determine Project policy, exercise budgetary and audit
control, approve appointments of professional staff, and coordinate Project
activities with those of associated Government Ministries. URADPEC would meet
regularly in Bolgatanga and would comprise the Regional Commissioner as
Chairman; senior representatives from Accra of the Ministries of Finance,
Agriculture, Labor, Social Welfare and Cooperation, and Economic Planning;
representatives of the Attorney General of Ghana, the Director General of
Ghana Broadcasting Corporation (GBC), the Bank of Ghana and Agricultural
Development Bank (ADB); the Regional Administrative Officer; the Project
Manager; a member of the House of Chiefs; and two bona fide farmers residing
in the Upper Region to be appointed, on the advice of URPMU, by the Region's
House of Chiefs.
6.03      The Project Coordinating Committee (PCC), chaired by the Regional
Administrative Officer, would meet once a month to coordinate relevant re-
gional operating departments and agencies. Other members would include
the Manager and Chief Administrative Officer of URPMU; the Managing Director
of FSC; the Regional Directors of Social Welfare and Community Development,
and Education; the Regional Managers of GBC, ADB, and Ghana Highways Authority
(GRA); representatives of the Ghana Water and Sewerage Corporation, the
Secretariat of the National Coordinating Committee of the Onchocerciasis



- 25 -
Program, and the Upper Regional Development Corporation (URDECO); and the
General Manager of the Cotton Development Board.
6.04      The Project Technical Committee, chaired by the URPMU's Chief of
Field Operations, would be established to ensure adequate technical coordi-
nation and feedback between the Project, the heads of technical departments
of the Ministry of Agriculture, and the country's research organizations.
Members would include departmental heads (Ministry of Agriculture, Accra),
representatives of the Crop Research Institute (CRI), ISSER, and URPMU's
Planning and Evaluation Officer. PCC and PTC would be established as a
condition of loan effectiveness.
6.05      The Upper Region Project Management Unit (URPMU) would carry out
the project in collaboration with two other agencies - the Farmers Services
Company (UR) Ltd. (see para 6.06), and the Ghana Broadcasting Corporation
(GBC) (see para 6.07). URPMU would consist basically of the existing per-
sonnel of the Ministry of Agriculture now located in the Upper Region, to-
gether with additional staff, recruited locally and internationally, needed
to provide the additional expertise that is required for the successful im-
plementation of the Project. An exception to this general rule would be
staff of the Irrigation Department who would, as now, remain under the direct
control of their Accra headquarters and be responsible for the implementation
of large scale irrigation projects in the Region. The Irrigation Department
would hand over, however, the responsibilities it now has for small dam con-
struction and soil conservation to the Project. In an endeavor to develop
a long term capability for providing heavy plant and equipment support for
regional land development purposes, a special "Soil and Water Conservation
Account" (SWCA) would be established, thereby enabling URPMU to establish
commercial operating rates and pay commercial wages and output bonuses to
SWCA operators. URPMU would have the form and basic functions of a regional
department of agriculture but would benefit from a high degree of autonomy
and from the fact that its implementation policies would be developed by a
committee, URADPEC, on which there would be strong representation from the
Upper Region itself. This regional type of organization is in line with
Government's policy of decentralization and has the advantage that at the
end of the Project investment period it can be left in place, with appropri-
ate staff reduction, to function as a regular component of the Ministry of
Agriculture and responsible for that Ministry's activities in the Upper
Region. Details of URPMU operations are at Annex 3.
6.06      The Farmers Services Company (UR) Ltd. would be established as a
condition of loan effectiveness under the 1963 Companies Code Act No. NRCD
289, with Regulations (as drafted at Annex 6, Appendix 1) acceptable to the
Bank, to ensure proper commercial servicing of the Region's farmers. It would
supply all farm inputs and would take over the current responsibilities for
mechanical hire services to farmers from URDC and MA. The Company (see
organogram at Annex 6, Appendix 2) would be part owned by Government, farmers,
and a consortium of banking institutions.



- 26 -
6.07      Regional Broadcasting Network would be owned, operated and staffed
by the Ghana Broadcasting Corporation (GBC). The network would be controlled
by GBC's Regional Manager who would be a member of the Project Coordinating
Committee. During negotiations assurances were obtained that at least 70% of
broadcast programs would originate from and be directed to the Upper Region,
and that the annual broadcasting budget and programming policy would be
jointly approved by URADPEC and GBC.
B. Staffing
6.08      The Project would need a high level of management efficiency and
technical competence to achieve its objectives. It is both large and com-
plex; some 125,000 farmers and their families could have their lives altered
through its implementation. Given this heavy responsibility, it is essental
that the Project be staffed in such a way as to permit its prompt execution,
to sustain its impetus, and to make modifications in the Project as need
emerges. Compared to some other West African countries, Ghana is partic-
ularly well-off in having well-educated personnel. However, Ghana has still
not had experience in carrying out a project of this size and complexity. The
most critical period will be the initial two or three years, when the basic
structure has to be developed, systems tested and modified, and staff trained
at all levels. This will require experienced personnel of proven ability for
its key positions. Wherever possible, qualified and experienced Ghanaians
would be recruited, but some foreign, internationally recruited staff would
also be required. The qualifications and job descriptions for most of the
important Project posts are at Annex 3, together with tables indicating those
positions where it is likely that international recruitment will be needed.
Most of the latter positions would be filled by Ghanaians by the end of the
third year. During negotiations assurances were obtained that for URPMU
the Project Manager, Chief of Field Operations, Chief Accountant, Senior
Communications and Training Officer, Senior Planning Evaluation Officer,
Senior Land Development Officer, Ranch Development Officer, Plant Breeder,
Senior Irrigation Officer; and for FSC, the Managing Director and Chief
Accountant, would be appointed having experience, qualifications, and terms
and conditions of service satisfactory to the Bank. At the same time agree-
ment was reached on those staff positions to be filled under international
recruitment. (Annex 11, Table 11) The latter would be funded under the Bank
loan and a technical assistance grant that has been offered to Government by
the United Kingdom.
6.09      The method of staff recruitment and terms and conditions of employ-
ment would vary. For URPMU Ghanaian Government staff it is unlikely that em-
ployment conditions would vary substantially from existing employment regula-
tions. However, inducements would include improved housing and transportation.
Staff from the Cotton Development Board would be seconded on CDB terms and
conditions. All other Ghanaian staff would be employed on three-year renew-
able contracts. FSC staff would be employed on conditions established by
the Board which would follow normal commercial practice. To ensure that
sufficient staff are made available, Government agreed, during negotiations,



- 27 -
that Project staff recruitment would be given priority and that once appointed
to URPMU an officer would not be transferred for a minimum of three years
unless approved by URADPEC.
6.10      Internationally recruited staff would be employed either on a
direct-hire basis by Project agencies or seconded under bilateral technical
assistance arrangements, and in some instances employed through consultancy
contracts. The Agricultural Projects Management Unit (APMU) of the Bank's
West African Region has been requested to assist in the recruitment of inter-
national staff. The Bank's Project Preparation Facility, established in
December 1975, may be employed to prefinance the cost of appointing such staff
prior to loan effectiveness. A condition of loan effectiveness would be that
URPMU's Project Manager and Chief of Field Operations, and FSC's Managing
Director, had been appointed.
C. Staff Training
6.11      The head of the Communications and Training Department, who would
play a key role in staff training, would be critical to the long term suc-
cess of the Project. Training would focus on inservice management and tech-
nical training at all levels of URPMU and associated agency staff. The
Communications and Training Department would be responsible for determining
type and duration of courses, selecting candidates, and evaluating results.
Senior specialist staff from operational departments would give courses in
related subjects. Training for junior staff and farmers committees would
take place at Farmers Service Centers and at the Navrongo Agricultural In-
stitute. Medium and senior staff would generally attend courses at Navrongo,
but when necessary would attend training courses outside the Region. Where
possible, institutions such as the University of Ghana, ADB, and the Crop
Research Institute would assist in this training program.
6.12      An incentive scheme would be introduced to allow selected staff
to travel abroad either within or outside Africa for short periods of up
to two months to carefully selected projects or courses which are directly
relevant to the staff member's field. The training program for staff would
be further extended through the use of the Region's radio network, which
would broadcast special staff training programs directed particularly at
lower level field staff.
D. Farm Inputs: Procurement and Distribution and Credit Procedures
6.13      The Project, through FSC, would be responsible for the procurement,
distribution and sale of fertilizers, seeds, insecticides, sprayers, oxdrawn
equipment, oxen, spare parts, transistor radios and other necessary farm and
ranch supplies.



- 28 -
6.14      The Farmers Services Company (UR) Ltd. would have an authorized
share capital of t12.0 million. Initial subscribers would include the Gov-
ernment (shares held by Ministry of Finance), the Bank of Ghana, National
Investment Bank, and the three commercial banks. Government would initially
be the majority shareholder with a financial equity of t1.0 million (US$0.8
million) provided from Bank and UK external funds, and equity in kind com-
prising existing land and storage facilities. A subscription of f4.1 million
is required from the banking and investment institutions. Assurances were
obtained during negotiations that in the event of equity participation failure
by a commercial bank or investment agency, Government would undertake to
increase its own equity correspondingly. The farmers would obtain equity
through a compulsory 2-1/2% mark up on the CIF and/or store price of purchased
inputs which would be credited to their share accounts. TheCompany's Board of
Directors would comprise the Project Manager as Chairman, the FSC Managing
Director, three representatives of banking and investment equity holders, two
representatives of the Ministry of Finance, and three farmers representing the
Farmers Associations. FSC's Chief Accountant would be Secretary to the Board.
Estimates indicate that FSC would be financially sound with a 13% financial
rate of return.
6.15      The Company would own and operate a retail distribution network,
and through 90 stores would supply farmers with farm inputs which would be
sold on a commercial basis (including a minimum 20% mark up to cover over-
heads and profit) for either cash or deferred payments (supplier's credit).
The Company would operate a revolving fund, for purchased farm inputs, com-
prising a 06.2 million loan from Government (source IBRD/UK) to cover in-
cremental foreign costs (8.85% for 25 years with seven years grace for
principal), and short term overdraft facilities of about e3.5 million from
the commercial banks. During negotiations assurances were obtained that the
Company would be exempt from duties, taxes and import restrictions for its
first five years of operations.
6.16      The Company would provide inputs to only those farmers approved
by URPMU's technical staff; this, to some extent, would reduce the possi-
bility of purchases of inputs for further retailing either in or outside
Ghana. Furthermore, any farmer requiring deferred payment privileges would
have to be approved by his Farmers Association, which would have collective
liability. Interest plus service charges for goods purchased on deferred pay-
ments would result in a differential of 12% above cash prices; this would re-
flect the cost of borrowing by the Company, bad debts, and the additional
cost of administration. A discount would be made for early repayments. Far-
mers Associations would be informed of defaulters and would be requested to
recover outstanding loans. If this fails, members of defaulting associations
would not be eligible for deferred payment purchases.
6.17      In instances of crop failure, FSC Board would reschedule debts.
Any massive rescheduling on this basis would place a financial strain on
the Company, and the shareholders, other than the farmers, would undertake
to underwrite adequate additional funds in proportion with their holdings.



- 29 -
6.18      Medium and Long Term Credit.  The Company would act as agent for
the Agricultural Development Bank and would provide oxen, oxdrawn equipment
and transistor radios to the farmers at 12% interest over five years. The
Company would retain 1-1/2% for its services. Loan approval and recovery
would be the same as for deferred payments. Farmers requiring loans for
oxen would be required to match each oxen purchased on loan with one of
their own, both to be branded to establish ownership. A down payment of
at least 20% would be required on loans for purchase of farm equipment.
6.19      Credit to ranch companies established under the Project would
be made directly by ADB on the advice of URPMU's Animal Husbandry Services
Division. Seasonal credit would be at 12% and long term loans would be at
12% for up to 13 years, with a five-year grace for principal.
6.20      Farm credit provided by commercial banks and ADB to large farmers
could be used for purchasing inputs from Company stores. This would help
to improve repayment records of large farmers which in the past have been
poor due to lack of inputs and resultant low production. To strengthen
ADB's regional office in Bolgantanga, the Project would provide an experi-
enced loans officer to assist existing loan staff.
6.21      At certain selected service centers, diesel fuel, etc., would
be available for cash. As there are very few retail outlets for basic con-
sumer goods in the remote rural areas, FSC would slowly build up retail
outlets at its centers for goods supplied through URDECO, thus establishing
an important link in the growth of rural trade. FSC would, where possible,
use URDECO transport services, and would initially, on a leasehire basis,
provide URDECO with ten trucks to expand its transportation system. If URDECO
failed to respond, the vehicles would be withdrawn and operated by FSC.
For full details of the operational procedures and functions of FSC, see
Annex 6.
6.22      During negotiations assurances were obtained that FSC would limit
its business to Project participation for five years, and would not amend its
regulations without prior consent of the Bank.
6.23      A bar chart at Annex 3, Schedule 7, details the organizational
and decision making process and approximate timing of Project implementa-
tion.
VII. PRODUCTION, MARKETS AND PRICES, FARMERS BENEFITS AND
FINANCIAL IMPLICATIONS TO GOVERNMENT
A. Production
7.01      Due to the complexity of cropping mixtures and patterns throughout
the Region, incremental production resulting from the Project is difficult



- 30 -
to express in terms of single crop yields. From a detailed study of re-
search undertaken in northern Ghana and at similar latitudes elsewhere in
West Africa, the potential for crop yield improvement has been assessed and
an attempt made to express existing yields in terms of pure stand equivalent
which takes account of yield benefits due to mixed cropping. These are pre-
sented in the form of single hectare budgets in Annex 2. Overall, the Proj-
ect would improve the production of about 108,000 ha (30% of total arable)
currently being cropped. The farmer grows a whole range of crops, the pro-
portions of which change from year to year in response to his needs and to
the market. It is impossible to define these proportions exactly, but an
estimate has to be made for determining Project costs and benefits. In
preparing the estimates, account was taken of future pricing trends, farmer
preferences, and market demands. A summary of target yields and expected
1981 incremental production is given below.
Area       Average       Incremental
Developed    Yields        Production

Maize                               4.0         2,000            5.0
Rice                               19.0         2,200          29.6
Groundnuts                         25.0           900           11.2

B. Markets and Prices
7.02      Market prospects for Project production are generally good, al-
though prices when expressed in constant terms are projected to decline
over the development period. Although the Region is already a net ex-
porter, under- and malnutrition are prevalent throughout much of the rural
population, and consequently a proportion of incremental production is
expected to be directly consumed; marketed output is expected to comprise
mainly paddy rice, cotton, groundnuts, cowpeas and yams. Nationally, self-
sufficiency in rice appears to be near, at least at current price relation-
ships, but realization of projected world prices for any quantities exported
would require an improvement in present milling standards. There are ample
crushing facilities in the country and all groundnut production is expected
to be processed locally, pubstituting for imported oils and providing pro-
tein cake for the growing animal feed industry. Only small surpluses are
anticipated for other food crops and these should be readily absorbed either
domestically or in neighboring Upper Volta where grain deficits are expected
to persist. The demand for meat is far in excess of supply, as it is through-
out West Africa.



- 31 -
7.03      The Project would not involve itself directly in produce marketing,
but it would, through URPMU's Planning, Evaluation and Monitoring Department
and FSC, collect accurate marketing information for dissemination to the
farmers through the media of radio broadcasting, news sheets, etc. By pro-
viding storage areas at farm service centers, it would facilitate group and
bulk produce assembly of interested farmers' groups; and, possibly through
the aid of FSC and the commercial banks, the Project would develop a system
of bonded storage that would enable farmers to receive an initial cash pay-
ment for produce (Annex 1).
7.04      Future economic farmgate prices have been projected from Bank
commodity forecasts using a shadow rate of exchange to reflect the economic
value of foreign exchange in terms of cedis, while comparable forecasts, based
on recent local prices, have been made for domestic crops such as yams and
cowpeas (Annex 1, Tables 5-13).

C. Farmers Benefits
7.05      No attempt has been made in the report to produce a typical farm
budget since farm sizes and production patterns vary considerably through-
out the Region and any such budget would not be realistic. Direct benefits
accruing to participating farmers can be evaluated by an analysis of the
changes in crop area, input use, and yields that the Project aims to achieve.
The data below provide an order of magnitude of the net returns per hectare

Tomatoes                908.8        2.27          1,697.1         3.03        187
/1   Expressed at 1981 prices in 1975 constant cedis terms on the basis of
unsubsidized inputs. US$1 = ¢1.154.
7.06      Benefits accruing to those participating in the livestock compo-
nent of the Project are varied and complex. The veterinary health improve-
ment proposals are based on the assumption that further increases in offtake
are unlikely to occur under predevelopment conditions, and new investments
should result in an increase in the value of output per breeding cow of about
060/annum by 1990; the improved output from sheep and goats (shoats) would
increase from 02.7 to 011.5 per shoat unit. The average livestock owner
(40% of the Region's population) is likely to net an additional 0200 per
annum. The ranching component is commercial and enclave and results, in
the 15th year, in an annual increase in income after tax of 014,000 per ranch
representing a 19% financial rate of return. This income could be divided
between as many as 20 shareholders, all of whom would be local cattle owners.
7.07      Currently the average farm income due to crop production is about
0280 per annum. The Project would, by 1981, increase this by 32% to 0370
per annum, thus raising per capita income from 041 to 054 (6% per annum).
Depending on the results from the livestock investment, incomes could be
further increased by as much as 080 per annum by 1990.
D. Financial Implications
7.08      Like other rural development projects, the Project does not signi-
ficantly increase directly-generated revenue to Government, as there are no
Government taxes or cesses that can be applied to farmers' produce except



- 33 -
FSC's corporation tax, which would be about ¢1 million in 1985. However,
there are likely to be substantial indirect revenues through further develop-
ment effects, although such revenues cannot be quantified at this stage. All
loans made by Government to ADB, FSC and CDB would be fully repaid within 25
years. The net average cost to Government from Years 1-5, exclusive of
external debt repayment, would be C4.0 million; from Years 6-25, 02.7
million; and from Years 22-30, 07.4 million (Annex 11, Table 6).
7.09      The financial implications for FSC, ADB and CDB are at Annexes 6
and 11. In each case, on the assumption of adequate management and controls,
there is a positive cash flow that provides sufficient incentives for partic-
ipants.
VIII. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
8.01      The Project would benefit in one way or another the Region's 125,000
farm families, principally by substantially improving net incomes. Quantifica-
tion and identification of beneficiaries is not easy. The Project would
improve the production on about 108,000 ha (30% of total land cultivated each
year). If the Project is moderately successful perhaps only half the farmers
(60,000) will benefit directly. However, the Project would not exclude any
farmer, and given the proper incentives of timely supply of inputs, credit,
extension services and favorable prices, it would be quite conceivable for 90%
of the farmers to receive direct benefits from the Project. The overall
economic rate of return is 40%; for the ranching component the return is 31%
at a cost of 01.0 million; while that of the crop production component is 33%
at a cost of 044.8 million. The return to the animal health component is
very high (95%) at a cost of 02.7 million. Important assumptions used in the
calculations at Annex 11 are:
(a)  Commodity and fertilizer economic prices are based on the
Bank's projections up to 1985 and 1985 prices thereafter.
Economic prices of insecticides and pesticides, seeds,
farm implements and feed are assumed constant throughout
the period. Family and hired labor are priced at a shadow
rate of t0.8 per manday, compared to the financial rate
of 01 per manday, to reflect the average opportunity cost
and labor productivity in the area.
(b)  Foreign exchange is priced at 01.50 per US dollar, as com-
pared to the prevailing financial price of 01.154 per US
dollar.
(c)  The 1981 production targets are expected to be maintained
through Year 20 of the project.



- 34 -
(d) All costs associated with the Project are incremental and
are included in the calculation of the economic rate of
return.
(e) All identifiable taxes and duties on both inputs and outputs
are excluded.
8.02      The Project would be sensitive to increased costs and lowered
benefits due to lower than anticipated yields or prices. Farmer response
estimates are considered conservative; however, analysis shows that if Project
administrative and service costs remain constant but response is less than
anticipated with a commensurate reduction in farm inputs, the Project would
still be economically viable if the area under improved production were
reduced to 55,000 ha. A detailed sensitivity analysis is at Annex 11, Table
19; its main features are summarized below:
Benefits                       K          - 20           -30          K

Rate of return (X)             40           26            18         23
K = appraisal base estimate - variation expressed as percentage.
8.03      The Project would have important secondary benefits that would
accrue to the people of the Region and to the economy as a whole. The most
important are:
(a) The Region's population would benefit from:
(i)  a general improvement in communications media and
information;
(ii) improved domestic water supplies, nutrition and food
storage;
(iii) increased farmer participation in local development
and decision making; and
(iv) new institutions specifically designed to serve the
Region for the foreseeable future.
(b)  The Project could have a profound effect on national de-
velopment policies affecting the agricultural sector; in
particular the Project would, because of its size, have a
marked impact on commodity pricing marketing policies and
input subsidies. The Project would train considerable num-
bers of Government service staff in managerial and tech-
nical disciplines who could assist in the development of



- 35 -
other regions. Because of Ghana's policy of decentraliz-
ing administration to a regional level, the Project would
be replicable.
(c) The Project could result in substantial net savings in
annual foreign exchange, estimated by 1981 in 1975 con-
stant terms to be equivalent to 012 million for crop
production, and as much as t11 million for beef.

GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Marketing, Markets and Prices
A. Background Summary
1.        In Ghana, cereals, legumes and other crops are marketed through a
vigorous but loosely connected free enterprise system. The extent of Govern-
ment interference (by monopoly buying or floor price arrangements) varies be-
tween regions, being confined to rice and cotton in the Upper Region. Never-
theless, official maize buying activities in other parts of the country can
affect farmers and consumers in the Region. There is an overall production
deficit situation for annual industrial crops (cotton and oil seeds) and
some foodgrains. The supply/demand gap is bridged by imports and a degree
of undernutrition, particularly in the Upper Region. Imports are controlled
through licenses and duties of up to 50% ad valorem; a form of price control
system is operated at wholesale and retail level for imported foods and those
crops purchased at fixed prices by Government agencies.
2.        The 3.3 million urban dwellers situated mainly in the southern half
of the country account for 34% of the population; they provide a continuing
demand, resulting in about 50% of cereal and root crop production entering
market channels. Despite this, the somewhat arbitrary Government production
and pricing policies for individual crops causes both supply and price dis-
tortions which could have serious repercussions, particularly if attempts
are made to compensate by increasing official intervention. This situation
is exacerbated by the naturally extensive nature of production, relatively
poor communications and the consequent wide and erratic price spreads both
between markets and over time.
3.        Relative to production there has been considerable overinvestment
in agro-industry, particularly for seed crushing, wheat milling, and tomato
canning. The same situation applied to rice until very recently, but the
problem now has changed to one of occasional local undercapacity due to im-
perfect mill siting.
B. Food Crop Marketing
4.        Dietary preferences vary markedly throughout the country and range
from the predominantly rootcrop, plantain, maize pattern of the southern and
middle belts, to primarily cereal (millet/sorghum) in the north. Mobility
of population combined with conservatism in taste results in a fairly brisk



ANNEX 1
Page 2
inter and intraregional trade which is characterized by a large number
of participants. Trade at retail and, to a lesser extent, wholesale level,
is dominated by women, particularly in the south. Scales are not used nor
is there any standardization of measures between or in individual markets.
Trade is normally in small lots on a cash basis, although regular sala-
ried customers seldom experience difficulty in obtaining credit to pur-
chase larger quantities for home consumption. 1/
5.        Statutory intervention in food crop marketing occurs through the
Rice Milling Unit (Ministry of Agriculture) which pays an annually fixed
price at six buying stations, 2/ and the Food Distribution Corporation
which has recently taken over the price support operations of the Grains
Board. The Food Production Corporation 3/ also has a marketing arm. Whole-
sale and retail prices for rice, maize and wheat products are fixed by the
Price and Income Board; enforcement is difficult, however, particularly in
the more remote areas and in times of shortage; prices fluctuate freely at
local level. The Food Distribution Corporation attempts to intervene at
farm level postharvest and alleviate some of the later effects of shortages,
especially in the urban areas; this activity is ineffective in the Upper
Region due to shortage of storage facilities and lack of transport.
Present Marketing Deficiencies
6.        Quantitative market information is scanty; however, observations
and data that are available reveal a number of persisting problems. None
of these are unusual, but in the context of the Upper Region geography pro-
vides an important complicating factor. Direct proximity to neighboring
countries where dietary habits are more closely related to those in the
Region than are those of people in much of the rest of Ghana inevitably
means that flow of produce and inputs will be very sensitive to external
influences and policies. Bawku, for example, has long been a center of
local international arbitrage and transit, and the extent of the border
is such that these movements can never be wholly controlled. Any unilat-
eral change in circumstances or conditions in neighboring countries could
thus have considerable effect in the Upper Region and vice versa, causing
local distortions of trade which could affect the Project if the cause re-
mained undetected for long.
1/   In Bolgatanga the basic unit for this type of business appeared to
be one bag (204 lbs). Deferred payment charge around 02 per ¢24 per
month.
2/   Situated at Wa, Tumu, Navrongo, WaleWale, Bolgatanga and Bawku.  RMU
have mills at Bolgatanga, Bawku and Wa, the latter being inoperative
at present.
3/   Current plantings in the Upper Region total about 1,270 ha, of which
800 ha is late millet. Farms are at Wa, Tumu, Chiano, Navrongo,
Bolgatanga and Tivi; there are plans to produce an annual total of
1,600 ha groundnuts, 400 ha sorghum, and 800 ha late millet on a
nucleus estate/outgrowers basis.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
7.        Although crops are sold out of the Upper Region, consumption falls
some way below desirable levels and a high incidence of malnutrition has been
recorded, 1/ particularly in women and young children. The "hungry season,"
extending from April through to at least the early millet harvest, is an
undeniable fact of rural life and in many families food intake at the end
of the dry season meets only about 60% of normal energy requirements. There
are a number of contributing causes, apart from inadequacies of initial
production; from the marketing standpoint the most important is that many
smallholders do not achieve the potential value of their produce. The
marketing system is unable to absorb large quantities over a short period
(particularly postharvest) except with a substantial discount. The converse
naturally applies and price movements are consequently substantial (Table 1).
8.        The smallholders' inherent lack of bargaining power is compounded
by lack of cash and inadequate information. A price collection service is
operated by MA 2/ but speed of acquisition and analysis is low and provision
for dissemination in the markets nonexistent; communication difficulties are
in any case enhanced by the absence of a common language.
Marketing and the Project
9.        The Project would improve the present situation by:
(a) ensuring rapid availability of accurate market
news and advice through the media of the Proj-
ect-established broadcasting service;
(b) providing facilities for group bulking and
marketing operations;
(c) investigating and developing, if feasible,
a bonded store system.
Market Information Services
10.       A section headed by a marketing economist would be established
in the Project's Planning, Evaluation and Monitoring Department to collect,
analyze and disseminate market information. Prime emphasis would be on
speed and accuracy in providing price information, while a continuing
effort would be made to build up a comprehensive picture of regional pat-
terns of consumption and trade. The section would operate on a district
1/   By nutritionists working in the Christian Services Committee's Nutrition
and Children's Rehabilitation Project, operating in Bawku since 1962.
2/   At the following Upper Region markets:  Bolgatanga, Zebilla, Bawku,
Widana, Navrongo, Sandema, Fumbisi, Tumu, Nandom, Lawra, Babile,
Tangasia and Wa.



ANNEX 1
Page 4
basis in order to minimize language problems, with a marketing officer and
market recorder (F.A. 2) based in each district office. Specific and gen-
eral market information would be collected daily and transmitted to the
head office, which would, in turn, regularly relay important news to the
districts. Dissemination would be by radio, news sheets and simple graphic
board notices at service centers, while extension staff would be briefed
regularly. Instruction and advice on basic marketing principles and prac-
tices would also be provided to groups and associations by district mar-
keting offices.
II.       Assembly and weighing facilities at FSC service centers would be
available for use by groups. No initial fee would be required, but produce
stored on behalf of farmers groups would have to be in quantities acceptable
to FSC, and bear rent and costs of insecticidal dusting or other prophylac-
tic treatments. Produce owners would be required to provide all labor.
12.       In order to reduce market pressures, particularly at harvest
time, the practicality of developing a source of marketing or consumer
funds against the security of a harvested crop would be sought. The co-
operation of the Grains Warehousing Company (now in process of formation),
ADB and the commercial banks would be enlisted through FSC. The objective
would be to provide an acceptable alternative source of cash, albeit rather
short term, so that farmers are not forced to sell crops at any specific
time. Difficulty may lie in agreeing base values, but the actual mechan-
ics of the operation would be simple, the pledged grain being placed under
triple lock (Bank, farmer, FSC) in an FSC store pending repayment of the
advance or expiry of agreed time period. Fumigation and storage charges
would be for borrowers' account, in addition to interest.
C. Cotton Marketing
13.       Present Situation.  Cotton marketing is carried out by the Cotton
Development Board (CDB) staff in mobile buying teams operating a two-round
schedule over the 12-week marketing season. Cotton is assembled at access
points convenient to the growers, who are supplied with 1.5 meter square
cloths able to contain about 45 kg seed cotton. Purchases are for cash on
an accept or reject basis, the cloths being weighed on truck by steel yard
or spring balance. CDB operating areas do not coincide with regional bound-
aries, but seed cotton purchases from smallholders are estimated as follows:



ANNEX I
Page 5
---------------------'000 tons---------------------
1970/71    1971/72    1972/73    1973/74    1974/75
Upper Region                  168          482        625         476         723
Northern Region                88          334         607        468         684
Total North                   256          816      1,232         944       1,407
Other Regions                 138          368        619         672         608
Total Ghana /                 394        1,184      1,851       1,616       2,015
/1   Excluding State and seed multiplication farms.
14.       The use of mobile teams to buy and carry cotton is rather uneconom-
ical and requires a high degree of organization. This point is underscored
by the 1973/74 operations in the Upper and Northern Regions where five teams
took 191 working days to buy the crop, averaging five tons (i.e., less than a
truckload) per team day. In 1975, shortage of funds prevented timely buying
of the crop and collection was not completed until the end of April.
15.       Ginning.  CDB own ginneries at Wa and Tamale, each equipped with
a 3 x 40 saw Platt gin with a combined effective capacity of 2,700-3,000
tons over the about 140-day ginning season (December-April). The gin at
Wa is now being replaced with 90 saw Murray gin which will increase total
capacity to roundly 4,000 tons. Cotton grown in the south is ginned under
contract in another 90 saw gin owned and operated by Ghana Textile Manufac-
turing Company.
16.       The present ginning process is expensive (about t0.175 per kg
lint), mainly because of the high labor requirements and inadequate facil-
ities and management at the ginnery. GTMC ginning charges are equivalent
to 00.0825 per kg, less than half CDB's own costs.
17.       Storage.  CDB has about 1,200 tons covered storage available for
all purposes at Tamale and about 500 tons at Wa. This is inadequate as
presently utilized and losses of cotton seed have occurred as a result
of weather damage. There are no plinths or similar facilities at ginnery
sites for the storage of seed cotton.
18.       M4arketing and the Project.  Under the Project, seed cotton pro-
duction is projected to reach 16,100 tons in Year 5, and more than quadru-
ple present output in Year 3. Primary marketing arrangements would there-
fore be revised and additional storage and ginning facilities erected in
order to efficiently absorb and process the increased crop.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
19.       Primary Marketing.  There are a number of potentially suitable
ways in which the crop could be bought; progressive improvements would be
sought within the following broad outline. The Region would be divided
into marketing areas (ten initially), each containing approximately equal
extents of actual or potential cotton lands, and cotton marketing facili-
ties 1/ established by FSC at selected service centers in these areas.
As a guide, a 500-ton throughput per center per (12 week) marketing season
would be aimed for in Year 3.
20.       Buying Arrangements.  The one grade 'accept or reject' princi-
ple would be retained for as long as practicable relative to condition
and quality of seed cotton offerings. At foreseeable stages of develop-
ment cleanliness will be the most important factor, particularly as trash
content affects both ginning efficiency and outturn. A second grade might
have to be introduced at a later stage if it became clear that the single
price system was resulting in careless husbandry and, for example, an in-
crease in the proportion of stained but otherwise sound cotton.
21.       Buying would remain the responsibility of CDB staff working to
a schedule agreed with Project and FSC management; sellers dissatisfied
with a buyer's decision would be able to appeal to a Project produce in-
spector, whose decision would be final. FSC would supply salesmen and
(if necessary) a cashier. The cash sale basis would be maintained, actual
payment being made following weighing and acceptance and after the seller
had loaded or stacked his seed cotton as required by FSC supervisor, thus
minimizing direct handling costs.
22.       CDB would be responsible for ensuring the adequate cash funding
of buying staff.
23.       FSC would be responsible for transporting seed cotton to the
gin(s) at a premium partially reflecting the consequent savings in aver-
age transport and overhead costs by CDB. This practice would be care-
fully developed in order to preserve farmer incentives without disrupt-
ing ginnery storage and organization.
Ginning and Storage (Tables 14 - 16)
24.       Additional ginning capacity would be required by Project Year
2 and again during Project Year 4. This first requirement would be met
through the construction of a new ginnery at Zwarungu which would econom-
ically handle cotton from the eastern half of the Region as well as any
production from Tumu district in excess of the Wa ginnery capacity.
1/   Separated preinspection, buying and bagging areas, suitable plinths,
additional security fencing if necessary. See Annex 6.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
25.       By Year 3 Project production developments should provide suf-
ficient evidence as to whether the latter capacity increases should be
achieved by addition to the Zwarungu gin or replacement of existing ma-
chinery at Wa. Provision has been made for 2/128 minus one gin plants,
each of which would have a practical operating capacity of about 9,500
tons seed cotton, 1/ which would require a guaranteed supply of 500 tons/
week.
26.       Seed cotton awaiting ginning would be stored on plinths in a
reception area directly tributary to the gin suction room. Incoming vehi-
cles would be received over a weighbridge, there being a final checkweigh
process for seed cotton into the suction room. Reception areas would be
built at both Zwarungu and Wa.
27.       Five hundred tons covered storage suitable for lint bales and
seed would be erected at Wa and 1,500 tons at Zwarungu, with an additional
2,000 tons being constructed as dictated by the eventual gin development
program. Additional lint and seed output above this capacity would be
absorbed by increased movement rates to mills and export (or local
crushers).
D. Rice Marketing and Milling
28.       For many years rice supplies have been deficient in Ghana and import
controls have raised internal prices well above world market equivalents,
thereby equating supply and demand through high domestic prices. Conse-
quently market levels for paddy have been generally above the guaranteed
floor price; the Government-owned Rice Milling Unit, which operates the
floor pricing mechanism, has been only a residual buyer, despite a large
and underutilized investment in rice mills. Efforts to increase produc-
tion have included a higher floor price. Marketed output is now well
above the capacity of the private millers who had previously enjoved the
crean of the trade; RtU purchases have risen accordingly, viz:
1/   Based on 140 day season, 3.5 tons per hour, 80% utilization.
Manufacturers estimate capacity at about 4.6 tons seed cottor/tiour,
approximately equivalent to 13.5 kg/lint/saw/hour, but actual per-
formance depends on climate, condition and cleanliness of seed cot-
ton. In Ivory Coast similar machines process the equivalent of only
2.25 tons/hour (7 kg/lint/saw/hour) whereas in Nigeria nominally lower
capacity machines operate at 9 kg/lint/saw/hour. This factor would
not be critical, however, before Year 4.

1971/72              4,350                  98
1972/73              2,965                  98    - 122.50
1973/74              7,395                 122.50 - 151.90
1974/75             31,815 /L              177.60
/1   Of which 27,820 in Northern and 3,850 in Upper
Region.
29.       The floor price is now at roundly import substitution level (0178
v ¢167) while the controlled price for rice is very much higher (Z617 ex-
mill v 0400) and also relatively out of line with other food prices.  The
state of self-sufficiency which is believed to have been brought about by
the recent sharp production increases may therefore be more apparent than
real, although consumption has grown at only about 3% per annum in the
past and there are no reliable indicators to relative price and demand rela-
tionships.
30.       Milling.  Nearly half RMU nominal capacity is situated in the Upper
Region but of this only the Bolgatanga mill is operational, the mill at Wa
being unserviceable and that at Bawku inoperative due to lack of throughput.
In contrast, the larger mills functioning in the Northern Region (four RMU,
three private) are now operating near to capacity.
31.       Prior to the upsurge in paddy production the bulk of local rice
entering the market was milled in small village mills (0.3 to 0.8 tph), the
resultant product having a higher and more consistent quality than that from
the larger units. These village mills, of which there are an unrecorded num-
ber in the Upper Region, are mainly operated on a custom fee basis and still
carry on an active trade; most of the paddy is parboiled, recovery rates are
around 65% and milling charges about 01.00 per bag (012.25 per ton), the
miller also keeping any recovered bran. While there are no reliable data
available from larger private mills, their equivalent costs are likely to
be very much higher. Comparisons with previously reported figures are inap-
propriate because until recently all larger mills have operated below capac-
ity; however, operating costs at an RMU mill were recently computed at 035.49
per ton of paddy milled. 1/ Over the seven months, November 1974 through May
1/   WARDA recorded a five-month milling period over 1974/75 in which 5,150.4
tons were processed at a cost of 0182,789.82. A higher average cost
than this has been assumed in price calculations (Tables 7-8).



ANNEX 1
Page 9
1975 RMU mills at Tamale processed 7,182.5 tons of paddy and produced 34.6%
grade I (10% broken) and 64.3% grade II (40% broken) on a 62% recovery (63.3%
net). Grading accuracy is open to doubt, however, as subsequent visual in-
spection revealed a higher percentage of broken grains in both samples.
32.       Future milling developments are planned to match the continued
emphasis on increased production; consideration is being given to expanding
existing capacity by up to 26 tons per hour. 1/ General economic and finan-
cial considerations could make it more desirable to increase present effec-
tive capacity by resiting some existing mills, thus enabling a reduction in
the purchase program. Consideration is also being given to installing
parboiling equipment at RMU and Mencilo mills in Tamale: this could be
a far more practical step towards improving final milled quality than
introducing electronic sorting equipment, which is also proposed.
33.       Present deficiencies stem primarily from the almost unqualified
emphasis that is being put on production per se. This could lead to severe
rice marketing problems, even in a controlled environment. Urgent action
needs to be taken to improve product quality, which is now affected by the
following factors:
-    inadequate quality control at time of purchase,
resulting in mixed grades and varieties and a
high foreign matter content;
-    poor stock control after purchase resulting in
a comingling of previously separated lots;
-    inadequate mill management and maintenance in
a continuous running situation;
-    poorly conditioned grain entering the mill
process (paddy should be milled at a moisture
content of between 13% and 14%; during the
dry season some samples have been as low
as 4%).
34.       With existing equipment and under present milling conditions, ini-
tial paddy quality and buying standards probably have the greatest effect
on rice quality; strict purchasing grades for paddy should therefore be
established and enforced. World market prices are forecast to decline in
the face of increasing self-sufficiency among traditional importers, and
emphasis on paddy quality and cleanliness is thus in the best interest of
farmers if a reasonable return relative to potential market values is to
be achieved.
1/   RMU, six mills totaling 18 tph; private sector, three mills totalling
8 tph.



ANNEX 1
Page 10
Rice Marketing and the Project
35.       Paddy production is expected to increase by about 150% under the
Project, with the marketed surplus reaching nearly 24,000 tons, equivalent
to the probable effective RMU capacity in the Region. There will, however,
be other output tributary to these mills from irrigated areas such as Vea,
although this may be counterbalanced by the capabilities of small village
units. In any case it is clear that there would be a very close balance
between supply and available milling capacity.
36.       The Project's Planning, Evaluation and Monitoring Unit would
therefore conduct a survey of production milling and marketing so that an
adequate appraisal could be made of desirable future investment in process-
ing. The survey would be completed in PY2 and, apart from determining pro-
duction areas, would pay particular attention to market requirements, pres-
ent mill numbers and capacity, and the relative attractions and economics
of various mill types. FSC and RMU would cooperate in the design, conduct
and analysis of the survey, especially in regard to defining possible means
of mill procurement and ownership and the practicability of small integrated
production and processing ventures, possibly based on service centers. Any
resulting feasibility studies or investment proposals would be prepared by
FSC for submission to ADB, NIB, or other suitable institution.
E. Markets and Prices
37.       Historically, Ghana is a net importer of foodgrains and feedstuffs;
however, since the 1974 ban on rice imports and the increasing regulation
of oils and fats imports, only wheat remains at a significant level, 96,300
tons in 1973 (Table 3). Import and price controls, together with high du-
ties, probably mask the true situation, but there are signs that the overall
deficit may be easing and some individual commodities moving into apparent
market surplus.
38.       Expressed in constant terms, world market prices are forecast to
decline steadily over the life of the Project, to achieve a new stability
around 1980. Nevertheless, the residual nature of world trade means that
small changes in total supply or demand can have disproportionate effect
on prices, and thus there may be sharp seasonal variations about the trend.
Current and recent prices in Ghana are remarkably close to straight import
substitution levels and, where feasible, world price forecasts have been
used as a basis from which to calculate future farmgate prices.
39.       Local conditions will naturally have important short term effects,
but these cannot be forecast, while longer term considerations are catered
for in the yield figures and other assumptions made in crop budgets. In
determining individual crop values import duties or taxes have been omitted
from both financial and economic calculations on the grounds that it is
unreasonable to suppose that (a) in a situation of occasional deficiency



ANNEX 1
Page 11
prices would maintain a level equivalent to full import parity, or (b) in a
situation of general or increasing shortage consumers would be penalized by
the maintenance of duty (presently 50%). A shadow exchange rate 01.50 =
US$1.00 has been used in calculating economic prices; identifiable taxes
and commercial profits have been omitted.
40.       Because of the currently low average per capita availability and
consumption it is expected that quite an important proportion of incremental
food grain production will be consumed by growers; additional marketed quan-
tities are unlikely to have any important local effect on prices.
Crop Prices (Tables 5-13)
41.       Millet and sorghum are the staple cereals in the Upper Region, the
latter being used both for food and brewing into pito. Changing production
patterns are expected to result in the disappearance of the millet surplus
and a small increase in that of sorghum, largely from acceptable dwarf var-
ieties. Historically there have been only slight differences in value be-
tween the two grains; an import substitution value for sorghum has been
calculated and adjusted for millet to reflect slightly better storage prop-
erties.
42.       Maize, although nationally the most important, is grown on only
a small scale in the Region and does not benefit from the official price sup-
port system. Output in the east is mainly consumed green, that in the west
being taken through to grain. Total production in the country has achieved
a near balance to marginal surplus (small stocks being held by FDC) and price
forecasts have therefore been based on an import substitution basis in Tema/
Accra.
43.       Rice is expected to account for the largest proportion of marketed
incremental production although local consumption of rice is generally rather
low. Traditional preparation is by parboiling and handpounding (occasionally
machine milling); this product is very much preferred to that from the larger
mills, which enjoys only a small sale in the Region.
44.       A national self-sufficiency situation appears to have been achieved.
In order to assure this position it is expected that production efforts will
be maintained, the country becoming a net exporter in 1977, with 20% of mar-
keted output being exported in 1980. Prices have been calculated accordingly,
based on forecasts for Thai rice, 25-35% broken. This also implies an
improvement in milling performance (see paras 31 to 39), although export
quality rice could be made available by adjusting domestically marketed
grades.
45.       Groundnuts are an important cash and food crop; local processing
is relatively limited, however, as shea butter is preferred to groundnut
oil. Consequently the bulk of marketed production is bought for commercial
crushing at Tamale, Tema and Accra. There is a mill building at Bawku owned



ANNEX 1
Page 12
by GIHOC, but the machinery has been cannibalized to maintain other plants.
Nevertheless, there is a substantial unutilized crushing capacity in the
country which would easily absorb incremental output. Although projected
production considerably exceeds current soft oil imports and could be re-
garded as potential exports, groundnuts have been priced on an import sub-
stitution basis because of the overall chronic deficit in the vegetable
oil sector; in addition there is an important unsatisfied demand for pro-
tein for the growing poultry feed industry which could be met from local
crushings.
46.       Cowpeas are a widely traded and important cash crop and source of
protein. Forecasts have been based on postharvest prices in Kumasi; a
decline is expected through 1980, with a subsequent recovery in line with
general expectations for vegetable protein sources.
47.       Yam prices have been based on those reported in the Upper Region
and are expected to decline in sympathy with other starchy foods. No change
in marketed quantities is expected.
48.       Cotton is imported in considerable quantities (Table 3).  On cur-
rent production forecasts domestic output will still be an import substitute
in 1980, when demand from local textile manufacturers is expected to reach
about 100,000 bales (equivalent to 52,000 tons seed cotton). Although im-
ports are allowed in free of duty, local lint and seed cotton prices have
been set with scant regard to world levels on each other. Calculations for
future producer prices have been made on the assumption that a direct rela-
tionship with international levels will be developed and pricing inconsis-
tencies and subsidies removed. 1/ Cotton seed is presently exported and
seed cotton prices have been calculated on the basis of this practice con-
tinuing.
49.       Tomatoes are grown throughout the Region.  Demand is brisk, par-
ticularly for the main crop which comes between those of the other main
producing areas, being harvested during February/March. There is a GIHOC
tomato processing and canning plant at Pwalugu (Upper Region - 20 miles
from Bolgatanga), but this has hardly operated due to inability to compete
with prices on the fresh market; puree imports continue at over 1,000 tons
per year. Price data are almost nonexistent, but in 1974 GIHOC guaranteed
40.20/kg; this has been used as a basis for future prices, equating back to
40.14/kg after allowing for losses and packing.
1/   At IBRD forecast rates of inflation the present producer price, if
maintained unchanged, would equate to world levels in 1980.
December 8, 1975

GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm and Crop Development
1.        A general description of the Project area and the farming systems
practiced is contained in Annex 4. The influence of the environment on crop
production and the potential for yield improvements with regard to research
undertakings in the area are discussed below.
A. The Environment
2.        Climate.  Average annual rainfall varies from 950 to 1050 mm per
annum with 85% falling between mid-April and mid-September. While the pat-
tern is well suited for annual crop production, distribution is often erra-
tic, with harmful consequences on crop yields. This usually results from
insufficient rains in April and June, too heavy rains in August or insuffi-
cient rain in September, drought stress being compounded by excessive run-
off and poor soil moisture-holding capacity. Distribution is most erratic
in the east; this is displayed in the more regular distribution at Wa com-
pared with Navrongo and Bawku in the rank probability analyses of Tables 1
through 3.
3.        Technology introduced through the Project would serve to lessen
drought stress to some extent. In particular, contour bunding and tie
ridging would be encouraged, while work-oxen development would permit more
timely land preparation in adverse seasons. Expected crop yields resulting
from the Project have, however, been risk discounted for adverse weather con-
ditions.
4.        Soils.  Upper Region soils are formed mainly from granites and
tend to be coarse-textured and stony. Inherent fertility is low and their
natural abrasiveness is frequently augmented by laterization. Valley soils,
which range from sandy clay loams to silty clays, have higher natural fer-
tility but are more difficult to work and subject to seasonal waterlogging
and floods; coupled with the presence of tsetse and simulium flies 1/ in the
larger valleys this has concentrated agriculture on the more easily worked
upland soils.
1/   The sectors of sleeping sickness and riverblindness respectively.



ANNEX 2
Page  2
5.        Groundwater laterites occur over much of the Region and limit the
potential for intensive cultivation particularly mechanical tillage; these
limitations appear to be well recognized by local farmers. Cultivation
constraints are further compounded in the uplands by severe risks of sheet
erosion. Excessive runoff can result, especially during the first rains
a fter the drv season which come as thunderstorms and line squalls preceded
by a 5-10 minute period of high winds and frequent dust storms.  Conspicuous
examples of sheet erosion are commonly seen; with overall neglect of soil
conservation measures along with rising population pressure, these dangers
wiLl increase.
6.        Even the best upland soils have poor chemical properties; reason-
able crop yields can only be expected with judicious use of fertilizers.
Nitrogen and phosphates are the principal deficiencies: this is supported
bv good yield responses in trials and demonstration plots, as well as by
an established and significant trade in fertilizers within the farming com-
munity itself. 1/ Nitrogen response can be somewhat variable since the free-
draining soil is low in organic matter and poor in its ability to hold inor-
ganic fertilizers. Split dressings of nitrogen in moist soil immediately
after rainfall are necessary, as well as efficient utilization of farmyard
manure.
7.        Responses to potassium, on, the other hand, have not been identi-
fied; the soils appear to be adequately supplied and maintained bv the ash
of crop residues after the annual burn. Although continuous check should
be made for deficiency symptoms, these are unlikely to occur during Proj-
ect implementation and no allowance for potassium has been included in the
farm budgets. Some trial results have shown that potassium application
can reduce yields by upsetting the ratio of exchangeable Mg and K in the
topsoil.
8.        Secondary nutrient deficiencies, particularly sulphur and boron,
are possible and could explain the somewhat low yields often achieved in
trials and demonstration plots. Limited research investigation is rather
inconclusive, however, since a sulphur trial with groundnuts in northern
Ghana showed a negative yield response to sulphur applications at 15 and
30 kg/ha, while older records have recorded significant groundnut yield
responses to single superphosphate over triple superphosphate. CDB have
identified sulphur deficiency in cotton when nitrogen and phosphorous
were applied in the form of 20.20.0 compound fertilizers.
9.        While this subject would require further investigation during
Project implementation, it is likely that sulphur would be suitably sup-
plied using single superphosphate as a basal dressing. Calcium ammonium
'J   3,500 tons were sold in Upper Region in 1974.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
nitrate is favored over sulphate of ammonia due to its less harmful effects
on soil pH. Boronated superphosphate could be used for cotton if deficien-
cies are identified.
B. Agronomy
Millet (mixed with Sorghum)
10.       Millet is the prime staple of the Region and is grown both as an
early and a late crop, the former yielding when food stocks are at their
lowest.
11.       Despite the importance of millet, prospects for substantial yield
improvements are low. Limited results are available from OAU/STRC/JP 26
regional trials and from variety trials undertaken at Nyankpala in 1966/67
in cooperation with the Rockefeller Millet Improvement Program in India.
Despite promising results elsewhere, 1/ variety trials in northern Ghana
have been disappointing and inconclusive and no recommendations can be
made.
12.       Traditionally grown millet is usually planted in mixed stands,
principally with guinea corn sorghum (see para 14 below). Treatment with
household waste, stock manure and night soil is widely practiced. Already
about 10,000 hectares receive some dressing of inorganic fertilizers; the
latter practice is expected to increase and would be encouraged by the
Project with more widespread availability of farm services. Although low
in absolute terms, attractive yield responses have been demonstrated at
Nyankpala from organo mercurial seed dressings and fertilizer application,
nitrogen being applied at planting and four to six weeks subsequently.
The proposed treatment is as follows:
1/   The Nigerian variety Ex-Bornu has frequently yielded over 4,000 kg/ha
at Samaru.

Sorghum
13.       Background.  Most of the traditionally grown sorghums are of the
open headed guinea race types with loose, often pendulous penicies. Both
white and red grained types occur, the former being favored for 'tuo' (por-
ridge) while the latter is used for 'pito' brewing. Sorghum stalks, which
are around 5 m high, are important for thatching, fencing and as a source
vf fuel.
14.       Most sorghum is grown on the compound farm and is usually inter-
propped with millet and cowpeas. Planting takes place during May/June
after establishment of the early millet crop; the latter is usually
thinned to 3-5 plants per station on ridges spaced 90 cm apart while
the sorghum is thinned to 1 plant per station. Two or three weedings and
earthing ups are normally carried out. The crop flowers at the onset of
the dry season in late September and matures on residual soil moisture by
late November.
15.       Pests and Diseases.  Sorghum midge (Contarinia sorghicola) is a
serious pest throughout the Region; eggs are deposited in the spikelets at
flowering time and the white larvae which hatch out feed on the young seeds
causing yield losses of up to 50%. Control can be effected by spraying with
Sevin at planting and again ten days later. Other insect pests include stem-
borers (principally Sesamia and Coniesta), army worms and shoot flies, but
are of little importance under present systems of agriculture. The para-
sitic weed, Striga hermonthica, is believed to cause yield losses of over
10% and is particularly associated with low fertility areas. Bird damage
can cause heavy losses, particularly on early maturing varieties, when no
alternative grains or cereals are available to the birds.



ANNEX 2
Page  5
16.       The most important disease is covered smut (Sphacelotheca) which
is estimated to cause yield losses of up to 10%; this can be largely con-
trolled by seed dressing. Other diseases include leaf spots (Cercospora),
rusts (Puccinia), eye spot (Glacercospora) and sooty strips (Ramulispora),
but are of little economic importance.
17.       Varieties.  Although numerous variety trials have been undertaken
in the past, research recommendations at this stage are somewhat inconclu-
sive. Varietal assessment has tended to use grain yield as the primary in-
dicator although taste and straw yields will be decisive factors in local
acceptability. In addition, the improved early maturing photo-insensitive
varieties of sorghum, which offer considerable advantages in terms of yield
potential and drought avoidance, usually produce their grain under wet, humid
conditions. Grain quality can be severely reduced by mold; successful breed-
ing for mold resistance is underway in northern Nigeria.
18.       Presently recommended improved varieties include the Nigerian SK-
MDW, a late dwarf variety of around 110 days to flowering, and the Nigerian
"Naga White" of around 80 days to flowering. Limited trial results in Ghana
have shown promising yields of over 2,500 kg/ha, but the somewhat scant rec-
ords also report demonstration results where subsLantially lower yields of
the improved varieties have been achieved, in some instances lower than
local selections of the long duration varieties. 1/ Although Naga White
has proved acceptable for tuo it is not favored for pito brewing, which is
the major outlet for sorghum grain in the Region; straw yield is also sub-
stantially lower than the local varieties.
19.       The outstanding yields achieved with improved varieties in re-
gional variety trials in West Africa, 2/ together with breeding progress
for disease and striga resistance, will inevitably lead to their accept-
ance. Until these yield increments are clearly and continuously demon-
strated to farmers, however, it is likely that taste preferences and impor-
tance of straw yield will prevent wide scale adoption in the short term.
A proposed treatment under the Project is as follows:
1/   It was not possible to 'screen' these records according to husbandry
and supervision variations.
2/   Hybrid yields of over 7,000 kg/ha have been achieved at Samaru.
