Maize
20.       Background.  Maize is an unimportant crop in the eastern sector of
the Region where it is cultivated mainly in crop mixtures in swampy areas
primarily as a vegetable crop for eating green; in the west, however, it is
grown by many farmers as a partial alternative to early millet. Most produc-
tion is consumed on the homestead, largely as a green crop, and there is no
significant surplus marketed as grain.
21.       Pests and Diseases.  Stemborers (principally Sesamia and Coniesta)
can be serious but are more important in second season crops grown in south-
ern Ghana. Other insects do not appear to be major field pests though local-
ized attacks of army worm (Spodoptera) can cause considerable damage to maize
seedlings. High storage losses occur, however, from weevil damage (Sito-
philus and Tribolium spp). Disease problems include rust (Puccinia poly-
sora), leaf blights (Helminthosporium turcicum and H. maydis), maize streak
virus, grey ear rot and downy mildew; the former are becoming increasingly
controlled by breeding resistant genes into recommended varieties. Striga
hermonthica is a common weed pest in the area.
22.       Varieties.  Due to the limited importance of maize in northern
Ghana, breeding work has been mainly directed towards southern conditions,
and varieties treated in regional trials have shown disappointing perform-
ance in the north. While these results can be partially explained by more
reliable rainfall in the south, trial supervision and lack of attention to
northern conditions are almost certainly as important. In particular, com-
posite varieties bred at Kumasi (Ghana Composites 2, 4 and Golden Crystal)
have performed well at comparable latitudes in Nigeria 1/ and in more
1/   For example, Ghana Composite 4 was the highest yielder (7,558 kg/ha)
in OUA/STRC/JP 26 uniform maize trials in Samaru in 1972.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
northerly latitudes in Upper Volta. Local taste preferences, however,
are presently limiting large scale adoption of composites in southern
Ghana, where local varieties command premium prices due to their higher
pounding qualities and the reduced fermentation period required in prep-
aration of the local maize cakes, 'kenkey.' 1/
23.       In view of the low research backing for advanced technology in
northern Ghana, problems of taste preferences for home consumed maize and
present overproduction in Ghana (3,000 tons were exported in 1974), large
scale maize development is not envisaged under the Project. New technology
is included, therefore, largely to reduce overall area required for home
consumed crops. A summary of the proposed technology is given below:
Treatment               Unimproved         Advanced
Variety                       Local             Composite 4,
Crystal Yellow

Seed Dressing                 None             Dieldrex A
Fertilizer

- P                   None             150 kg SSP
Yield (kg/ha)                   750             2,000
Rice
24.       Background.  Rice is grown by 15-20% of the Region's farmers, pre-
dominantly as a cash crop. Production is mainly in the hands of hand culti-
vators and is principally confined to lowland valley soils where natural
rainfall is supplemented by lateral moisture accumulation. Some large
scale mechanical cultivation is practiced in the larger valleys mainly in
the western and southern parts of the Region.
1/   This effect is mitigated in the case of cash crop maize production
since an attractive guaranteed purchase price (which does not extend
into northern Ghana) is offered by the Grains and Legumes Development
Board. As a result GLDC has accumulated a stock of around 25,000
tons of maize which it is unable to sell. Successful work is under-
way, however, on new methods of preparing traditional dishes with
improved varieties.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
25.       Rice is planted in June or July and is direct seeded by broad-
casting or dibbling. Two weedings are normally undertaken though these
are often late or inadequate due to difficulties in identifying weed spe-
cies. At harvest, the rice is tied into bundles and stacked in a central
area for drying and threshing. Locally consumed rice, presently a minor
constituent of the diet reserved for special occasions, is parboiled before
milling. Surplus over domestic needs is generally sold as paddy immediately
after harvest; parboiling is not practiced prior to commercial milling in
Ghana.
26.       Pests and Diseases.  Pest damage appears to be of little economic
importance though stemborers and armyworms are occasionally troublesome
and bird damage can be serious prior to harvesting. Common diseases are
blast (caused by Pyricularia oryzae), brown blight (Cochliobolus niyabeanus)
and stack burn (Trichoconis padwickii); presently recommended improved var-
ieties display blast resistance and most diseases are checked with organo
mercurial seed dressings.
27.       Varieties.  Traditional upland varieties are of Oryza glaberrima
mixed types but these have been largely replaced by Oryza sativa varieties
introduced since World War II. Previously recommended varieties C4-63 and
IR5 are now being replaced by IR20 which has good resistance to blast and
drought and lower susceptibility to lodging. Recent trials have shown IR442
to consistently outyield IR20; this variety is being multiplied for release
next year. Research is continuing for high yielding varieties of short and
long duration to cater for varying conditions of slope and seasonal inunda-
tion.
28.       Lowland rice trials under controlled water conditions have fre-
quently recorded high yields in excess of 5,000 kg/ha. Farmer yields are
substantially lower, however, and despite husbandry and land management
improvements under the Project, advanced yields over a wide range of con-
ditions are expected to average around 2,200 kg/ha. Proposed treatments,
involving seed dressing, basal application of nitrogen and phosphorus
followed by nitrogen top dressing at the booting stage, are summarized
below:



ANNEX 2
Page 9
Treatment               Unimproved         Advanced
Variety                  Local & Improved        IR442
Seed Rate                    80 kg/ha            90 kg/ha
Seed Dressing                 None               Dieldrex A
Fertilizer
- N                   None              150 kg CAN
- P                   None              150 kg SSP
Yield (kg/ha)                  900               2,200
Groundnuts
29.       Background.  Groundnuts are grown by most farmers, largely as a
cash crop, and enjoy a steady trade to the south for crushing due to their
high oil content (45-50% by weight). In traditional cultivation systems,
groundnuts are usually grown in mixed stands with cowpeas or bambara beans
and are often associated with poor soils unsuitable for grain production
due, for example, to striga infestation.
30.       Both ridge and flat plantings are commonly practiced by farmers,
the operation usually being undertaken during June once planting of the
main cereal crops has been completed; plant spacings of 1 to 1.25 meters
between ridges and 25-30 cm between plants are practiced. Fertilizers are
presently applied to about 25% of the groundnut area, but application rates
are low and rarely exceed 50 kg per ha of single superphosphate.
31.       Pests and Diseases.  Rosette (a virus disease transmitted by aphids)
is common in the Region but can be effectively controlled by close spacing
which allows easy movement of aphid predators from plant to plant. Cercospora
(leaf spot) and Sclerotium (wilt) are also common but their incidence is
generally reduced by seed dressing and early plant establishment. Provi-
sional fungicidal spray recommendations for Cercospora control are available
but their technical and economical viability under Upper Region conditions
has yet to be ascertained. Direct pest damage appears to be unimportant
though termites are occasionally troublesome; heavily infected areas can
be treated with Aldrin dust.
32.       Varieties.  Varietal selection in northern Ghana has been directed
towards high oil content, pest and disease resistance, full season maturity
(130 to 140 days) and a long dormancy period. Trial results at Manga over
five years have recorded high yields (1,250 kg/ha shelled nuts) from the
Chinese variety Lung Tou Yotao and the Philippine variety Tirik, the latter
being preferred due to its higher oil content (50%), longer dormancy and
maturity (130 days), and high shelling percentage (73%).



ANNEX 2
Page 10
33.       The proposed level of improved technology would include thorough
seedbed preparation with incorporation of farmyard manure whenever possible,
followed by timely planting shortly after the first rains. Single super-
phosphate at the rate of 150 kg/ha should be applied in a band in the old
furrows before splitting to make new ridges. Seed would be of the variety
Tirik which should be shelled shortly before planting and dressed with
Dieldrex A. Seed spacing should be two every 15 cm (6") on ridges spaced
75 cm (30") apart 1/ giving a seed rate of 70 kg/ha. Two to three weed-
ings should be carried out during the period from planting to pegging or
until sufficient foliage cover has developed to suppress weed growth.  A
summary of the proposed technology is given below.
Treatment               Unimproved          Advanced
Variety                        Local            Tirik
Seed Rate                        50                90
Seed Dressing                  None             Dieldrex A
Fertilizer
- P                   None              150 kg SSP
Yield (kg/ha)                   450               900
Yams
34.       Background.  The white yam is a minor crop in the Upper Region.  It
is found mainly in the western sector where it is grown in small mixed
stand plots almost entirely for consumption.
35.       The crop is usually grown on mounds which are constructed at the
end of the wet season. Planting is by setts sectioned from harvested tubers
or from seed tubers that have developed following careful harvesting of an
earlier crop; planting extends from December to March. The mounds are
usually protected by a vegetative mulch. The vine sprouts and develops
with the onset of the rains and is usually trained to climb on stakes of
sorghum or millet stalks. Weeding is important during the first three
months; two to three weedings are usually undertaken and the mounds re-
built to replace soil washed down by rainfall. Some harvesting is carried
1/   Research results show that 60 cms (2') ridge spacing yields around 25%
higher than 90 cm (3') spacing. A 75 cms (30") spacing is recommended
due to the practical problem of narrow ridge formation.



ANNEX 2
Page 11
out in August, when tubers are carefully cut and removed, leaving the crown
and vine undisturbed, from which will grow a second, smaller seed tuber; the
main harvest period, however, is during November and December following the
drying and dying back of the vines.
36.       Pests and Disease.  The main pest is the yam beetle (Heteroligus
meles) which bores into the tuber damaging the growing point and reducing
tuber size. Nematodes (mainly Scutellonema bradys) can seriously reduce
yields and allow entry of fungi which reduce the value and keeping quality
of tubers; the nematode problem is aggravated by using already infested
tubers as planting material. Dieback, a disease causing premature drying
of tubers, is associated with the incidence of nematodes. "Shoestring" can
also reduce yields; the causal agent is unidentified at present but the dis-
ease is less common than dieback.
37.       Varieties.  Due to its vegetative propagation, no improved varie-
ties of yam have yet been developed. However, successful development of a
yam seed germination technique at IITA, Nigeria, means that there is a very
high prospect of improved varieties becoming available in the near future.
38.       Present yields are low in the Region (around 4-5 tons/ha) with
corresponding small tuber size; a gradual decline in yields is also appar-
ent particularly in areas where increasing population pressure has reduced
fallow duration. Research results have, however, shown good responses to
nitrogen application when used in conjunction with husbandry improvements
including staking, higher setts weights (ideally 0.5 kg sectioned from
tuber tops) and sett treatment with Aldrin 2.5 prior to planting; a recom-
mended fertilizer application rate is 250 kg/ha CAN applied during May. 1/
A marked reduction in nematode damage may also prove possible by a propaga-
tion technique using vine cuttings which has been developed at IITA. A
summary of the proposed technology is given below:
Treatment               Unimproved         Advanced
Variety                       Local             Local
Setting Rate               2,000 kg/ha         4,000 kg/ha
Sett Dressing                 None             Aldrin 2.5
Fertilizer - 1                None              250 kg CAN
Yield (kg/ha)                  4,000            9,000
1/   The plant relies on food stored in the sett for the first 14-20 weeks.



ANNEX 2
Page 12
Cowpeas
39.       Background.  Cowpeas occupy an important place in traditional
diets and are a significant source of income for local farmers. Both seeds
and leaves are consumed while surplus production is usually marketed soon
after harvest in November when local tax payments are due. The crop is
seldom planted sole and normally forms Dne part of a number of crop mix-
tures of varying complexity; under these circumstances yields vary widely,
but due to the range of insect pests to which it is susceptible, the crop
rarely averages more than 200 kg/ha. Intercropping offers some protection
against insect attack and the crop provides valuable ground cover and pro-
tection against erosion.
40.       Pests and Diseases.  Cowpeas are susceptible to a wide range of
pests which become especially important under intensified production. Major
pests include leaf eating beetles (Ootheca mutabilis), which can cause com-
plete defoliation and transmit cowpea yellow mosaic; Meruca testulalis, which
causes damage to flowers, pods and seeds; Alcidodes leucogrammus, a weevil
which causes stem girdling; and Eucrysops malathema, the flower and pod moth.
Storage damage is mainly caused by bruchids and weevils. Local varieties
usually show reasonable resistance to diseases, of which Cercospora leaf
spot and cowpea mosaic virus are probably the most important.
41.       Variety.  Little work has been undertaken on cowpeas in Ghana and
attempts elsewhere to introduce improved varieties have usually faced diffi-
culties of taste preferences or cooking qualities. Local selections from
Ghana and limited trials with imported varieties have shown promising yields
of over 2,000 kg/ha. While potential from varietal improvements is consid-
erable, the intensity of insect attack is such that substantial yield re-
sponses would result from pest control with the varieties presently used
in the Region; since ULV applications have proved successful in Nigeria
this would provide an obvious link with cotton production (see para 47
below). Suggested treatment is as follows:
Treatment               Unimproved         Advanced
Variety                       Local             Local
Seed Rate                     15 kg/ha          25 kg/ha
Fertilizer - P                None              50 kg SSP
Sprays                        None              10 liters Endosulphan
in 4 applications
Yield (kg/ha)                 200               900



ANNEX 2
Page 13
Cotton
42.       Background.  Traditionally cotton has been a minor crop in Ghana
with production limited to hand spinning and weaving of village cotton tex-
tiles. Commercial production was started in 1968 following establishment
of the Cotton Development Board (CDB) and realized a gradual expansion in
area to 4,400 ha in 1974/75, of which around 1,300 ha was grown in the Upper
Region. To boost production, the seed cotton price was increased drama-
tically in 1974 from ¢0.08 to 00.35 per lb (00.18 to 00.77 per kg); esti-
mated 1975 planting in the Upper Region is roundly 2,500 ha though farmer
demand is well in excess of this figure.
43.       Seed cotton yields have been disappointing in the Upper Region
with an overall average of under 500 kg/ha during the period 1970-1974.
The main cause centers on untimely operations, namely in planting, sup-
plying and weeding, but particularly in distribution of seed, fertilizer
and insecticides; ti se problems are likely to be compounded following
expanded CDB operations unless major organizational changes occur in the
input delivery system.
44.       Pests and Diseases.  The main cotton pests in the Region are
red bollworm (Diparopsis costanea), American bollworm (Heliothis armigera),
spiny bollworm (Pectinophora gossypiella); leaf rollars (Sylepta derogata),
and cotton stainers (Dysdercus spp). Also occurring are red spiders
(Titranychus telarius), cotton aphids (Aphis gossypii) and jassids
(Empoasca devostans). The leaf eating Cosmophilia and false codling
moths (Cryptophlebia leucotreta) occur increasingly and the previously
used Endrin (12%)/DDT (US) has not proved fully effective. Research on
the best complex insecticidal formula is underway and good results have
been achieved with a DDT/PCC/methyl parathion formulation sprayed at 12.5
1/ha over six sprays.
45.       Bacterial blight or black arm disease (Xanthomonas malvacearum)
is endemic but largely suppressed by using resistant varieties. Pink boll
rot (Glomeselloc gossypii) and damping off (Rhizoctonia) are largely con-
trolled by seed dressing.
46.       Varieties.  The originally used variety, Allen 333, has degener-
ated from frequent multiplication, and is gradually being replaced by BJA
592, a product of IRCT research in Mali. BJA 592 is a medium staple 1/
cotton grown on a strong tall plant that is robust enough to withstand
low rainfall conditions. Seed is multiplied by CDB at Nyankpala research
station (base multiplication) and on selected farms in Wa district.
1/   Fiber length 33/32 to 34/32 inches.



ANNEX 2
Page 14
47.       The Project would aim to establish a network of advanced cotton
producers capable of applying husbandry practices appropriate to intensive
cotton production. This would involve early planting, sole cropping, high
fertilizer application and pest control using ULV sprayers; the latter tech-
nique has been successfully introduced to smallholders elsewhere in Africa
and offers considerable advantages of maintenance and speed without requir-
ing ready access to a supply of water. The crop would be planted during
the first half of June at a spacing of 75 x 20 cm giving a plant population
of 125,000 - 150,000 per ha after thinning, with an average two plants per
hole remaining. Basal fertilizer dressings would be followed by nitrogen
top dressing at the end of first weeding four weeks after planting; two
to three weedings and reridging would be required. Since spotting of eggs
demands considerable skill, a precautionary spray would be given five weeks
after germination and continued at ten-day intervals for as long as six
weeks. Improved quality at harvest would be encouraged by timely picking
and use of cotton picking bags. A summary of proposed technology is given
below:
/1

Variety                      BJA 592           BJA 592

Yield (kg/ha)                  600             900

Horticultural Crops
48.       Background.  Dry season vegetable production is practiced by about
2,000 farmers in the Region; important crops include tomatoes, onions, maize,
and peppers. Areas cultivated per grower are small, however, and while pro-
viding important ingredients for the local diet, there is no substantial and
regular regional export.
49.       There is considerable scope for developing horticultural crops,
both in terms of yield per hectare and through expansion of dry season cul-
tivation. This is expected to prove highly attractive to growers since
there are few competing demands on labor, while sound husbandry practices
could generate valuable returns; about 800 ha of irrigated gardens would be



ANNEX 2
Page 15
developed by the Project's Land Development Department. While this would
include a range of crops suitably rotated, for simplicity the potential ben-
efits from this development are expressed in the form of tomato production
which has a ready outlet.
50.       Farmers would be advised on sound rotation to reduce nematode
build up from successive plantings of tomatoes. Management practices would
include proper nursery establishment and transplanting, repeated fertilizer
application at rates up to 900 kg/ha, and treatment against pests and dis-
eases. Advanced treatment techniques such as soil fumigation would be in-
vestigated during the Project. Existing tomato yields are low at around 7.5
tons/ha, compared with a potential of over 25 tons; a summary of recommended
practices is given below:

Other Crops
51.       Bambara nuts are an important source of protein in local diets,
and have the advantage of being able to produce reasonable yields in grav-
elly soils. Both bunch and spreading types occur, of which the latter are
lower yielding but produce a preferred darker-colored bean; black beans,
which are occasionally found, are highly prized and used in rituals.
52.       Little is known of bambara nut and its potential for improvement.
However, research is being conducted at the University of Ghana which may
result in technical recommendation during Project implementation.
53.       Kenaf production in the Region has been promoted by the Bast Fiber
Development Board 1/ whose operations extend presently to about 400 farmers
growing less than one acre of kenaf each. They are located mostly in the
eastern sector, but good potential is said to exist in Tumu and other parts
of the west. The Board supplies inputs and sometimes cultivation assistance,
paid for at delivery of the crop. As sufficient details of the economics
of fiber production are not yet available, no recommendations can be made
at this stage as to whether the Project should assist in kenaf production.
Present yields obtained with an application of 200 kg 15/15/15 fertilizer
per hectare are below two tons per hectare. At prices of 00.175 per kg
of handstripped kenaf, net returns per manday would remain below those
of improved sorghum and cotton; there are strong indictions that with at-
tractive cotton prices kenaf farmers will tend to shift towards cotton. To
judge the possibility of increasing kenaf prices to farmers, processing and
marketing costs would have to be investigated. Market prospects appear to
be good as Ghana's local production of fibers for its Kumasi jute factory
amounted to less than 10% of the country's demand. Furthermore, hitherto
untried exotic varieties could be introduced. This is intended for 1975.
C. Production
54.       The overall crop production objective of the Project would be
to produce a reliable and widespread distribution of crop inputs for the
Region's farmers, with supporting dissemination of and instruction in im-
proved husbandry practices; this would support a wide range of crops, only
the most important of which have been included in the foregoing technical
analyses.
1/   Under the Ministry of Trade and Industry.



ANNEX 2
Page 17
55.       Previous development work has shown that farmers are willing to
adopt new practices which offer real benefits; there is already an estab-
lished trade in purchased inputs in the Region. Immediate production im-
provements are expected simply from a wider distribution of goods and ser-
vices in the Region; thereafter improvements would be sustained through
demonstration, instruction and deliberate concentration of technical and
managerial research into problems confronting the smallholder.
56.       Against the background of research conducted within Ghana and
proven technical practices in other similar ecological zones, physical
budgets for crops discussed in Section B have been prepared and presented
in Tables 4 to 14. Their financial and economic attractiveness has been
assessed using farmgate values based on subsidized actual values, and com-
puted financial values in the absence of Government subsidies; the deriva-
tion of these farmgate values is detailed in Annex 1.
57.       Since significant quantities of fertilizers are already used in
the Region, it has been necessary to present the budgets under three cate-
gories, of which 'Advanced' represents the development objective of the
Project. The definitions applied to each category are as follows:
Unimproved      -  low technology, no purchased inputs;
Improved        -  improved technology with low level
of control over quality and timing
of inputs;
Advanced        -  high technology and high level of
control over quality and timing
of inputs.
58.       Projected developments under the Project are given in Table 15;
the crops have been selected according to comparative net returns per hec-
tare, manday requirements, projected trends in world market prices 1/ and
the probable impact on staple food prices of large local surpluses. Both
yields improvement and area developed are expected to result from an over-
all package of improvements including purchased inputs, timely husbandry
operations and improved labor productivity; the latter subject is dis-
cussed in Section D.
59.       Due to the almost universal practice of mixed cropping, presen-
tation of budgets in terms of sole cropping does not necessarily represent
their true value to the farmer or provide an accurate indication of incre-
mental benefits to the Project. During the last decade, mixed cropping has
attracted increasing professional attention and comparisons under trial
1/   See Annex 1.



ANNEX 2
Page 18
conditions have often shown substantial positive yield advantages. 1/ In
the case of work conducted at Samaru (Nigeria), mixed cropping yield in-
creases have frequently exceeded +50%, though at Nyankpala these have been
lower, varying from -10% to +30%.
60.       The extremely low crop yields frequently reported for smallhold-
ers are often judged without regard to yields from other crops that may
have been growing in the same plot. In calculating the pure stand equiv-
alent yields for the various budgets, an upward adjustment has been made
to represent more closely the effective yield that is achieved in a mixed
cropping regime. In addition, the production projection given in Table 15
has been calculated using the existing production estimates given in Annex 4,
to which have been added the product of developed area and the incremental
yield per hectare given in the crop budgets.
D. Farm Mechanization
61.       Tractors.  There are presently about 120 tractors operating in the
Upper Region which are mainly involved in rice cultivation in the larger
valleys; total area under mechanized rice is around 3,500 hectares. Most
of the tractors belong to private farmers who have received loans from ADB
and commercial banks or have purchased second-hand machinery from the Min-
istry of Agriculture. The Ministry's own hire unit has only ten service-
able tractors and eight combine harvesters; a separate hire service is
operated by the Regional Development Corporation which has four combine
harvesters.
62.       The use of advanced mechanization in the Upper Region has a rec-
ord similar to that achieved elsewhere in Ghana: expensive, inefficient
and executed with little regard to its long term effects on the soil.
While valuable lessons should have been learned from this experience,
these are still often ignored or overlooked; as a result mechanization
1/   No standard methodology has yet been established to evaluate yield re-
sponses under mixed cropping. Since conversion to financial values is
influenced by the relative values of component crops while special lay-
outs (such as alternative rows) permit a proportion of a plot to be
ascribed to each crop, no account can be made of the influence on
relative yield of each crop caused by the treatment given to the
plot. Mixed cropping trials studied during appraisal were reana-
lyzed by taking the yield of each component plot in a (one hectare)
plot and calculating the area of a sole crop plot which would have
given the same yield. The sum of these calculated areas for each
component crop, when compared with the area of the mixed plot, gave
an estimate of area gained or lost from mixed cropping.



ANNEX 2
Page 19
runs the danger of becoming a modern and expensive form of shifting culti-
vation. The results of a soil survey of Ghana which delineates broad areas
where soils can be safely mechanized, are shown in Figure 1; this indicates
the cautious approach to mechanization that must be exercised over most of
the Region.
63.       The Project does not envisage any significant expansion in trac-
torized farming in the Upper Region, but would aim to improve the efficiency
of those already existing and ensure that sound land management is practiced.
This would be primarily the responsibility of the Farm Management Section
of the Agricultural Technical Services Department whose attention would be
focused on the Region's medium and large scale farmers. Hire services would
be transferred to the Farmers Services Company (FSC) 1/ and operated at com-
mercial rates. Farmers already owning tractors would receive technical ad-
vice and assistance on suitable land management practices and the Project
would carefully monitor soil conditions on intensively cultivated units.
The Project would also act as an agent for future ADB loans and ensure
that suitable soil analyses and conservation measures are undertaken prior
to loan approval. In addition the field efficiency of the FSC Mechanical
Services Unit would be improved through mobile servicing facilities, access
to fuel supplies at selected service centers and the establishment of easily
accessible and closely spaced blocks of land which will permit high machine
output.
64.       Work Oxen.  There are presently estimated to be 8,000 pairs of
trained work oxen in the Region though the number that is actually opera-
tional is not known. Most are concentrated in the eastern sector where ox
cultivation and mixed farming systems have been gradually introduced over
the last 40 years. Ox training is undertaken by the Ministry of Agricul-
ture, Christian Services Committee (CSC) and more recently by GGADP. A
total of six training stations are presently operating in the Region sup-
ported by a number of itinerant trainers at village level; annual training
capacity is about 300 pairs. Farmer response continues to be good but the
high price of bullocks (around 0350-400) is out of reach of the non-cattle
owner.
65.       A further obstacle is the lack of sufficient numbers of suitable
plows and spare parts. A limited number have been imported by CSC from
Nigeria and by GGADP from Germany. Negotiations are in progress between
German and Ghanaian firms for local manufacture of a suitable plow and
spare parts in Tamale. Ridging and split ridging is practiced for upland
crops, while for paddy in the valleys a moldboard plow is needed. Whether
a suitable plow with exchangeable bodies can be developed depends on the
1/   See Annex 6.



ANNEX 2
Page 20
development of a suitable detachable ridging body. Meanwhile the Nigerian
plow "Emco" has proved best for ridging and the German "Eberhardt" for
plowing.
66.       There is considerable scope for expanding ox cultivation both
in terms of labor productivity and as a means of improving soil fertility
through production and application of farmyard manure. This would be
achieved under the Project through expanded bullock training facilities
with supporting technical instruction on mixed farming practices. Bawku
farmers already practice a form of rotational grazing (see Annex 4); the
Project would introduce fodder crops into this rotation as well as encour-
age supplementary feeding.
67.       The latest livestock statistics for Upper Region 1/ indicate a
cattle population of 340,000 head, including 24,400 bullocks, 31,100 bulls
and 155,100 cows. It is estimated that within this herd an additional 10,000
head could be converted to draft oxen. This would be achieved, particularly
in the western sector, within existing farmer herds and through private
transfer of stock between farmers. While credit would be available for
all implements, this cannot be recommended for bullocks due to their high
cost in relation to the farmers' income and to the risk of abuse; bullock
credit would therefore be limited and only available to farmers who can
provide a matching animal. As necessary, the Project would secure bullock
supplies from within the Region and from neighboring countries using when
necessary the services of the Cattle Development Board. The following
progression is expected:

68.       Income from work oxen would result either from increasing total
area under cultivation or from undertaking contract cultivation for other
growers. Parallel to growth in marketed production, an increased use of
ox carts is expected that would provide an additional source of revenue for
owners.
69.       Hand Tools.  Improved hand tools could make an important contribu-
tion to labor productivity. Many of the incremental labor requirements of
the advanced farm budget concern operations for which simple hand propelled
equipment is available; this includes seed planters, fertilizer drills, seed
dressing drums and crushing and shelling equipment.
70.       The suitability of such equipment would need to be carefully tested
in the area; no specific and quantified recommendations can be made at this
stage. Detailed study of small equipment is now underway at IITA and IAR,
Nigeria; the Project would benefit from this experience.
71.       Cost estimates in Annex 11 include a block allowance for purchase
of intermediate technology equipment which would be thoroughly tested at dis-
trict agricultural stations and with local farmers prior to release. Most
agricultural hand tools are manufactured locally by the Region's large num-
ber of experienced blacksmiths. The Project would encourage local manufac-
ture of implements when this is technically feasible.

Schedule 1

GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Establishment of Project Management Unit
1.        The Government has published an '`Order" in the official Government
Gazette establishing the Upper Region Project Management Unit (URPMU) and
the Upper Region Agricultural Development Project Executive Committee
(URADPEC). The Order sets out the objectives and responsibilities of URPMU
and URADPEC and includes the following:
2.        The URPMU would be managed by a Project Manager and its objectives
would include:
(a) development of the Upper Region with a view to increasing
agriculture production and raising the living standards
of the rural population;
(b) establishment of 90 farm service centers, at a rate of 30
per year, that would provide crucial services to farmers
including extension and management advice;
(c) improvement of animal health services including provision
of vaccines;
(d) development of small scale ranching schemes;
(e) establishment of applied research and demonstration
units;
(f) operation of a pilot adult functional literacy program;
(h) establishment, through Ghana Broadcasting Corporation,
of a regional FM radio broadcasting network;
(i) improvement of human nutrition;
(j) staff training;
(k)  construction of dams, fish ponds and soil conservation
works, and the establishment of a special unit to be
known as the "Soil and Water Conservation Working Account:'
to construct the works;
(1) provision of material assistance to villagers for building
700 village wells;
(m) construction, through Cotton Development Board, of two
ginneries and associated storage at Wa and Zwarungu
respectively;



ANNEX 3
Schedule 1
Page 2
(n) development of supporting infrastructure such as houses,
buildings, etc.;
(o) evaluation and monitoring of ongoing Project actions,
and preparation of new plans for expanded development
in the Region; specifically, this would include planning
for those areas freed of onchocerciasis in both the Upper
and Northern Regions; and
(o) any other activities to ensure the success of the Project.
3.        URPMU would be controlled by the Upper Region Agricultural
Development Project Executive Committee (URADPEC), responsible to the
Commissioner of Agriculture, and would be made up as follows:
-    Regional Commissioner - Chairman;
-    Senior representative of Ministry of Finance (Accra);
-    Senior representative of Ministry of Agriculture (Accra);
-    Senior representative of Ministry of Labor (Accra);
-    Senior representative of Ministry of Social Welfare and
Cooperation (Accra);
-    Senior representative of Ministry of Economic Planning (Accra);
-    Representative of Director General of Ghana Broadcasting
Corporation (GBC);
-    Representative of Bank of Ghana;
-    Representative of Agricultural Development Bank;
-    Regional Administrative Officer;
-    Project Manager;
-    Managing Director of Farmers Services Company
-    a member of the House of Chiefs;
-    two bona fide farmers residing in the Upper Region, to be
appointed by the House of Chiefs.
4.        URADPEC would be responsible to the Commissioner of Agriculture
and would have the following responsibilities:
(a) Policy: URADPEC would determine agricultural and development
policy, and would consult with Government and the World Bank
when any major changes in policy may be required.
(b) Finance:
(i) URADPEC would approve the annual budget and
would ensure that sufficient Government funds
are readily available to the Project;
(ii) URADPEC would appoint independent auditors;
(iii) URADPEC would submit audited statement of accounts
to the Commissioner of Finance within four months
of the closure of each financial year;



ANNEX 3
Schedule 1
Page 3
(iv) URADPEC would approve the opening of URPMU bank
accounts; and
(v) the Chairman of URADPEC would approve the monthly
cash flow statements submitted by the Manager of
URPMU.
(c) Staff and Personnel Matters: in consultation with the
Manager of URPMU, URADPEC would be responsible for approving
the appointment of all senior URPMU staff and employees,
their terms and conditions of employment, and annual
review of salaries and allowances.
(d) Award of Contracts: URADPEC would on the advice of the
Manager of URPMU be responsible for approval of any single
contract valued at more than 050,000.
(e) Interdepartmental Coordination: URADPEC through its
members would be responsible for the coordination at
Ministry level for those issues that may affect the
proper development of the Project.
Meetings of URADPEC
5.   (a)  The Chairman would preside over all the meetings of URADPEC
which he attends and in his absence the present members
would appoint one of their number to preside over such a
meeting.
(b) The Chairman of the URADPEC would invite members to meet
at such time and place as he may think suitable, provided
that no more than three months would elapse between any one
meeting and the next. In the absence of the Chairman, the
Project Manager would call URADPEC to meet whenever he
deems this necessary.
(c) The URADPEC may call any person to attend any of its
meetings without that person having a counted vote.
6.        URPMU would be responsible to URADPEC for day-to-day operations.  In
particular it would be responsible for carrying out the objectives as stated
in para 2 above. URPMU would be managed by a Project Manager who would report
directly to the Chairman of URADPEC. The main duties of URPMU would be:



ANNEX 3
Schedule 1
Page 4
(a) implementation of an agricultural development Project
within the Upper Region following the policy guidelines
of the URADPEC;
(b) preparation of annual budgets and cash flows for approval
by URADPEC. In particular URPMU would submit to the
Chairman of the URADPEC monthly cash flow statements
indicating any changes in requirements for Government
funds due to changes in Project expenditures. Unless
informed to the contrary within five days of dispatch
of such statements to the Chairman of URADPEC, the Man-
ager would be authorized to make the proposed expendi-
tures;
(c) recruitment of URPMU staff, in consultation, when
necessary, with other Government departments, and
for senior staff, with approval of URADPEC;
(d) advertisement, negotiations, calling for tenders, and--
after approval from URADPEC for those of more than
050,000--conclusion of such contracts with persons and
organizations either inside or outside Ghana for the
erection of buildings, construction of civil works and
supply of goods, services and materials as may seem
requisite for the proper discharge of its duties;
(e) operation of URPMU bank accounts; and
(f)  preparation of reimbursement claims against URPMU
expenditure, for submission to external financing agencies.
7.        URPMU would be responsible for evaluating and planning new agri-
cultural projects for the Upper Region, and specifically for preparing plans
for those areas freed of onchocerciasis in both the Upper and Northern
Regions. URPMU would be responsible for advising the Irrigation Department
of the Ministry of Agriculture on the suitability of selected land for pos-
sible irrigation schemes.
8.        URPMU would be responsible for monitoring the collection and dis-
semination of market information to the Region's farmers, and would cooperate
with FSC and other agencies in improving marketing practices.
9.        During the development of the Project (first five years), URPMU
would not be restricted from importing goods into Ghana, and such goods
would be exempt from duty and taxes.
10.       URPMU would establish a special heavy plant and equipment unit,
including contingent workshop facilities, to be known as the "Soil and



ANNEX 3
Schedule 1
Page 5
Water Conservation Working Account" (SWCA). The account would have a sepa-
rate bank account, would operate on a commercial basis in respect to costs
and operating charges, and would be free to hire operating staff at commer-
cial wage rates, including the payment of bonuses based on work output. SWCA
would take over the operations of the Department of Irrigation's workshops
at Zwarungu, and would, at cost, provide a maintenance service for the
Irrigation Department's plant and equipment.
11.       The Manager of URPMU would be an ex officio member of the Board
of the Upper Regional Development Corporation and would be Chairman of
Farmers Services Company (UR) Ltd.
12.       URPMU would prepare quarterly and annual reports for simultaneous
submission to URADPEC, Commissioner of Finance, Commissioner of Agriculture,
and external financing agencies.
13.       URPMU's overdraft facilities, arranged with its banks, would be
guaranteed by Government up to an amount equivalent to the approved URPMU
annual budget.
14.       URPMU would be delegated any additional duties and powers by
URADPEC to ensure the attainment of the objectives set out in this Order.

ANNEX 3
Schedule 4
Page 1
GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Management Unit Organization
Description of Functional Departm2nts
Administration and Personnel Department
1.        The Administration and Personnel Department would be the respon-
sibility of a Chief Administrative Officer. The Department would control all
personnel matters, recruitment, staff transportation, office management,
security and allocation of houses and offices. It would consist of a number
of executive officers each with specific responsibilities. For staff employed
on permanent and pensionable terms, the Department would work closely with the
Establishment Division of the Ministry of Finance to ensure proper transfer of
staff in and out of URPMU, adequate salary increments for performance, and
promotion gradings. For all other employees the Department would be respon-
sible for preparing employment contracts, and advertising for and recruiting
staff in consultation with other department heads and the Project Manager.
2.        It would ensure proper filing and record keeping particularly
for staff benefits and transportation use. It would manage office operations
and be responsible for stationery requirements and servicing office equipment.
Finally, it would be responsible for URPMU security, matters and control of
URPMU's vehicle pool.
Accounts and Procurement Department
3.        This Department would be the responsibility of the Chief Accountant.
The Department would, along with the Project Manager and department heads, be
responsible for the preparation and control of annual budgets. In consultation
with the independent auditors, to be appointed by URADPEC, it would establish
appropriate accounting systems that would provide adequate financial control
and cost retrieval systems commensurate with management's requirements for
decision making and evaluation.
4.        The Department would have responsibility for coordinating and
reporting on the flow of funds to both URPMU and other agencies that draw
on Project funds. This would include the Farmers Services Company, Agricul-
tural Development Bank, Cotton Development Board, and Ghana Broadcasting
Corporation. In particular, the Department would have to ensure adequate
cash flow arrangements with Government and commercial banks.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 4
Page 2
5.        The Department would be responsible for the preparation of invita-
tion for bids and contract documents on the recommendations of the department
concerned. It would evaluate bids and monitor contract obligations of the
suppliers. It would make payments for goods procured and would prepare and
control the proper documents for reimbursement of funds from the external
financing agencies.
6.        The Department would be responsible for the collection and account-
ing of revenues due to URPMU, and would operate an internal audit. The
Department would be responsible for stores control and for the audit of store
control books (excluding FSC stores).
7.        The Department would employ a Legal Officer (lawyer) to assist the
Project generally with all legal matters.
Field Operations Department
8.        This Department would be URPMU's main operational department respon-
sible for Project implementation. It would be headed by a Chief of Field
Operations and would be made up of a number of divisions:
(a) Agricultural Technical Services Division, headed by the Senior
Agriculturist, would be responsible for all aspects of crop
production:
(i) Extension Services would be reorganized as an intensive
operation providing technical advice to farmers. It would
be organized on a district basis and would no longer be
responsible for the distribution of farm inputs. Each
district would be the responsibility of a District Devel-
opment Officer (DDO) who, apart from being responsible for
extension, would be the overall coordinator of all URPMU
activities within the district. Responsible to the DDO
would be from ten to twenty Technical Officers (TO), each
in charge of a farm service center that would serve an
average of 1,300 farmers each. Each TO would be assisted
by two Field Assistants (FA); thus the farmer to staff
ratio would be about 400 to 1, which under the circum-
stances is very adequate. During the early years direct
contact would be more; the ratio in the beginning might
be on the order of 100 to 1. Depending on the success
of the radio broadcasting program the ratios could be
further widened after five years. Extension staff would
be responsible for teaching farmers new technology and
husbandry methods. They would assist both rainfed and
irrigation farmers, and would be responsible for checking
farm sizes, crop areas and requests for farm inputs on
cash or deferred payment basis. An experienced irriga-
tion agronomist with a good background in irrigation



ANNEX 3
Schedule 4
Page 3
management would be employed to work with extension
staff. He would have special responsibility for the
existing Vea irrigation scheme and for developing
small scale irrigation schemes adjacent to URPMU-
constructed dams. Major efforts would be made to
develop informal farmers associations at service center
and village levels; by doing so the extension and
administrative effort would be more concentrated and
effective. Farm mechanization advisors would encourage
and advise blacksmiths and other rural "engineers" to
center their activities at or near farm service centers.
(ii) Cotton Development Section. A special section would be
established under the Agricultural Technical Services
Division to continue the Cotton Development Board's policy
of intensive extension to new cotton growers. This is
particularly important as cotton is a new crop in the
area. Currently, 58 trained cotton agents operate in
the Upper Region. Under the Project they would be
seconded to URPMU on CDB employment conditions. In
those areas where cotton is developed at least one Cotton
Production Assistant (CPA) would be posted to each farm
service center. A CPA would be responsible for training
farm service center Field Assistants and up to 50
farmers. After two years intensive training, the exten-
sion responsibilities would be taken over by the Field
Assistant and the CPA would train another 50 farmers.
CPAs would be supervised by District Cotton Development
Supervisors (one per district). The Cotton Development
Section would be headed by a Senior Cotton Development
Officer, at Bolgatanga, who would be responsible to the
Senior Agriculturist in charge of the Agricultural
Technical Services Division.
(iii) Human Nutrition is included under the Agricultural
Technical Services Division since it reflects on crop
development. Currently there are food deficits in the
hungry season that the Project would endeavor to reduce.
Apart from general crop extension activities the extension
workers would, under the guidance of a Senior Nutritionist,
teach farm families methods of improved nutrition. Most
of this work would be done in the dry season. The Section
would coordinate its activities with ongoing work of the
Christian Services Committee located near Bawku.
(iv) Farm Management. Each district would have a Farm
Management Officer who would provide planning assistance
to the larger farms. The Officer would work with the



ANNEX 3
Schedule 4
Page 4
farmer in developing plans that could be submitted to
ADB for loan application. The vital function of the
service is the recognition that the larger farmer
desperately needs sound farm management advice to enable
a self-sustaining farming system to be developed. A
Senior Farm Management Officer experienced in management
would be stationed at Bolgatanga to supervise and advise
the district officers.
(v) Plant Quarantine and Produce Inspection. URPMU would
continue the plant quarantine and checking system cur-
rently in operation at key border crossing posts. It
would also employ seven district Produce Inspectors (PI)
who would act as arbitrators in quality disputes between
farmers and buyers. Further details at Annex 1.
(vi) Research and Seed Multiplication would be an integral part
of the Project and would include the reactivation of district
agricultural stations. The Project would also support CRI
in its efforts to improve cereal breeding, particularly
adaptive agronomic research and varietal improvement of
maize, sorghum and millet. Full details are at Annex 7.
(b) Land Development Division. This Division would be the respon-
sibility of a Senior Land Development Officer. Land Development
Officers (LDO) would be posted to each district; during the first
year of the Project they would be trained by the Senior Land Devel-
opment Officer. Land development in the early stages would consist
of planning catchment areas to provide skeletal layouts for soil
conservation works, waterways and lines of access. For details see
Annex 9. Each LDO would be responsible for planning, in conjunction
with the District Development Officer, a minimum of 6,000 ha per
annum. As catchment area plans are prepared they would be approved
by the Senior Land Development Officer. The LDOs would have the
responsibility for siting and planning dams; each district would have
a special committee consisting of Project staff and local authorities,
to determine priorities for dam sites and other developments. Dam
site and earth works plans would be kept simple and would receive
approval of the Senior LDP prior to commencement of construction.
Each LDO would be responsible for a small team of surveyors and
linesmen. URPMU would employ professionally qualified engineering
graduates as LDOs, some of whom may be transferred from the Irri-
gation Department.
Special cartographic services would be provided at the headquarters
drawing office in Bolgatanga; the same service would be responsible
for record keeping, filing of maps, designs, etc. LDOs would be
responsible for supervising the SWCA units during the construction
phase, to ensure that design criteria are maintained.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 4
Page 5
The Soil and Water Conservation Working Account (SWCA) would be
under the control of the Senior Land Development Officer; its
heavy equipment units would construct the dams and conservation
works. It would operate on a "commercial basis" and would pay
tractor operators and unit foremen inducement bonuses for work
output. SWCA would have its main office and workshop in
Zwarungu, five miles from Bolgatanga. A Senior Mechanical
Engineer would be in charge of SWCA operations, but construction
work would be initiated only after clearance from the Senior
Land Development Officer. Because of the delays envisaged in
procuring heavy equipment, it is unlikely that any major construc-
tion work would be undertaken in PY 1. However, this period
would be used for organizing SWCA workshops and training staff
and operators, using existing serviceable equipment. SWCA would
be fully operative in PY 2, during which all the units (three)
would work together as a single group, thus allowing better
control. Thereafter the units could be split, one working in
the east, another in the west, and a third would have special
responsibilities for dam rehabilitation. The development program
is quite large; SWCA would have to be staffed with experienced
staff to operate efficiently (details at Annex 9).
The construction of village wells and farm grain stores would
be planned by the Land Development Division, but it would use as
its agent for implementation the Department of Social Welfare and
Community Development. The latter currently assists farmers in
building houses, schools, clinics, etc. Under the Project the
Department would be given the responsibility for the village
well and farm storage program. Using existing staff and liaising
with farmers associations and agricultural staff, the Department
would, with URPMU-procured inputs, provide supervision and
demonstrations for well and grain store construction.
(c) Animal Husbandry Division, headed by a Senior Animal Husbandry
Officer, would establish a program for improving the Region's
livestock. Each district would have an Animal Husbandry
Officer (AHO) who, in conjunction with the DDO, would develop
and extend simple methods of livestock improvement to the
farmers.  The Division would teach farmers better methods of
livestock management, including improved calf rearing and stock
feeding.  Small scale onfarm stall fattening of old work oxen
and other suitable animals would be developed, the latter to
be tested initially on the District Agricultural Research and
Demonstration farms. The Division would be responsible for
ox training, and would arrange for ox training sessions and
demonstrations at the various service centers, following up
such demonstrations with actual onfarm ox training.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 4
Page 6
The Division's main development work during Project implemen-
tation would be the establishment of ten ranches in Tumu
District. The ranches owned and operated by the Region's
cattle owners would be supervised by a Ranch Development
Officer. The latter would be recruited internationally.
Details are at Annex 5.
(d) Veterinary Services Division would be headed by a Senior
Veterinary Officer, and would continue to provide stockowners
with animal health services. Full details are at Annex 5.
(e) Fisheries Division. The Region's existing Fishery Department
is small and poorly staffed. The Project envisages the employ-
ment of a Specialist Fish Culturist who would develop a regional
fisheries policy and would recommend practical management guide-
lines for the Region. The main activities of the Division would
be concentrated on the fish ponds at Vea dam and at three other
larger dams in the Region. The Division would be the respon-
sibility of a Senior Fisheries Officer.
(f) Forestry Division. The existing Forestry Department in the
Upper Region is currently the responsibility of the Ministry
of Lands and Mineral Resources. Forestry staff would be
seconded to URPMU and would form a special Forestry Division
within the Department of Field Operations. A Senior Forestry
Officer would head the Division.
9.        The Department of Field Operations would be closely coordinated at
field and headquarters level. Radio communications systems would be installed
at all district offices and certain key staff would have mobile radios in
their vehicles. Relevant departmental staff would be expected to prepare, on
a regular basis, the basic agricultural content required for GBC regional
broadcasting programs. Senior and specialist staff would be drawn upon to
lecture at training courses at Navrongo Agricultural Institute, and would be
consulted about training requirements for their own staffs.
Communications and Training Department
10.       For complete details see Annex 8.
Planning, Evaluation and Monitoring Department (PEMD)
11.       A description of the activities of the Planning, Evaluation and
Monitoring Department, including the development of investment plans for those
areas freed of onchocerciasis, are detailed at Annex 10.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 4
Page 7
12.       The Head of the Planning, Evaluation and Monitoring Department
(PEMD) would be an experienced Senior Planning and Evaluation Officer who
would report directly to the Project Manager. The Senior Planning and
Evaluation Officer would be responsible for three sections:
-    an evaluation section;
-    a market monitoring and information section; and
-    a planning section whose main function would encompass
the Onchocerciasis Survey Unit, responsible for the
preparation of a ten-year development plan as well as
future investment programs.
13.       Details of staff requirements for the evaluation and monitoring
sections are at Annex 3, Schedule 6, Table 1; and for the staffing of the
Onchocerciasis Survey Unit, details can be found at Appendix 1 of Annex 10
and Annex 11, Table 1.

GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Job Descriptions and Qualifications of Senior Staff
Project Manager
1.        The Manager should be an experienced administrator holding senior
positions in either Government or, more preferably, commercial organizations.
Candidates with experience in successful agricultural development projects
involving superior administrative requirements would be given preference.
The Manager need not have formal qualifications in agriculture but should
have proven management capabilities. He should be between 35 and 50 years
old, active, and able to command respect.
2.        The Project Manager would be responsible for coordinating all
Project activities. He would have direct responsibility for the day-to-day
operations of the Upper Region Project Management Unit (URPMU) and would be
Chairman of the Board of the Farmers Services Company (UR) Ltd. (FSC). His
duties would include:
(a) control and coordination of URPMU funds, with the assistance
of the Chief Accountant;
(b) coordination of Project activities, particularly coordination
of URPMU with associated agencies;
(c) responsibility for URPMU personnel matters, with the assistance
of the Chief Administrative Officer;
(d) reporting URPMU progress to Government on the basis of quarterly
and annual reports;
(e)  other responsibilities as set out in URPMU's terms of reference.
3.        The Project Manager would be responsible to the Chairman of the
Upper Region Agricultural Development Project Executive Committee (URADPEC).
His duty station would be Bolgatanga and his initial appointment would be
for three years.
Chief of Field Operations
1.        The Chief of Field Operations should have had formal training in
agriculture and experience in broad-based agricultural development. He
should have had at least ten years experience in a senior position under



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 2
similar development projects or in large public corporations. He should be
able to demonstrate a successful career and a proven record of obtaining
quantifiable results.
2.        He would head the Field Operations Department, which would involve
responsibility for ensuring that the Project's agricultural and related
infrastructural policy is properly executed. He would therefore be directly
responsible for the Agricultural Technical Services Division, the Land
Development Division, the Animal Husbandry Division, the Veterinary Services
Division, the Fisheries Division and the Forestry Division. His duties would
include specifically:
(a) departmental budget preparation and control;
(b) chairmanship of the Technical Coordinating Committee;
(c) quarterly and annual report writing;
(d) membership in the Board of Farmers Services Company (UR) Ltd.;
(e) collating technical input requirements and developing control
systems to be operated in association with the Farmers Services
Company;
(f) advising the Project Manager on departmental staff requirements
and, in conjunction with Department of Communications and
Training, developing suitable training courses for staff and
farmers;
(g) developing suitable broadcasting information for farmers and
staff, to be programmed by the Ghana Broadcasting Corporation;
(h) developing overall applied research policies within the Region;
and
(i) advising the Project Manager on field operational policies and
annual program of development targets.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager, his duty
station would be Bolgatanga, and his initial appointment would be for three
years.
Chief Accountant
1.        The Chief Accountant would be a professionally qualified accountant
with additional training in business management. He should have had at least
ten years experience in a senior position in a large public corporation or



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 3
commercial company. If possible, he would have a knowledge of banking and
credit procedures. Previous employment working with high ranking government
officials would be an advantage.
2.        His main duty would be the control of URPMU funds on behalf of the
Project Manager. His duties would include:
(a) preparing budgets, cash flows and estimates of expenditure;
(b) ensuring correct application for URPMU funds and their reim-
bursement;
(c) responsibility for URPMU stores control;
(d) coordinating flow of funds, accounting methods and accounts
controls with those other agencies which participated in the
Project; these include the Farmers Services Company, Cotton
Development Board, ADB's Bolgatanga branch office, and the
Ghana Broadcasting Corporation. Applications for reimburse-
ment of funds from external financing agencies would be
processed through his office;
(e) internal auditing of URPMU accounts; and
(f) training accounting staff.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager, his duty
station would be Bolgatanga, and his initial appointment would be for three
years.
Chief Administrative Officer
1.        The Chief Administrative Officer would be a senior and experienced
administrator from the civil service or a public corporation. He should have
a thorough understanding of Government procedures and should be sufficiently
mature and resolute to handle staff relations problems. He would head the
Project's Administration Department.
2.        His duties would include:
(a) responsibility for URPMU personnel;
(b) control of URPMU transportation;
(c) maintenance of office registry;
(d) control of office administration and development of day-to-day
procedural arrangements;



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 4
(e) responsibility for URPMU security matters;
(f) preparation and control of administration budget; and'
(g) the training of administration staff.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager, his duty
station would be Bolgatanga, and he would be appointed initially for three
years.
Senior Communications and Training Officer
1.        The Communications and Training Officer should be experienced in
information services, including broadcasting, and should have had experience
in organizing training programs. He would head the Communications and Train-
ing Department. He should be a good organizer and should be able to communi-
cate effectively.
2.        His responsibilities would include:
(a) managing the Communications and Training Department;
(b) developing detailed training courses for URPMU and associated
agency staff;
(c) arranging overseas training or visits;
(d) activating a visual aids section;
(e) ensuring the proper role of public relations;
(f) developing with field operational staff adequate information for
radio broadcasting, and subsequent liaising with Ghana Broadcasting
Corporation;
(g) developing a Project information center, including publications
of extension and other farm literature;
(h) preparing and controlling the Department budget; and
(i) controlling Navrongo Agricultural Institute which, under the
Project, would be used specifically for staff training.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager, his duty
station would be Bolgatanga, and his appointment would be for three years.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 5
Senior Planning and Evaluation Officer
1.        The Senior Project Evaluation Officer should be a qualified agri-
cultural economist with field experience in agricultural evaluation, planning
and monitoring. He should have a good background in evaluation methodology.
2.        He would be head of the Evaluation, Planning and Monitoring Depart-
ment, and his duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of Department budget;
(b) development and implementation of evaluation systems to quantify
the results of Project actions at both farm and regional levels;
(c) assistance in drawing up and evaluating additional Project plans,
and evaluation of any new plans concerning agriculture that might
be prepared by other agencies for implementation in the Region;
specifically, controlling a special Onchoceriasis Survey Unit
that would be responsible for planning investment schemes in
areas freed of onchoceriasis in the Upper and Northern Regions
of Ghana; and
(d) development of a market monitoring system to provide frequent
information to farmers on product prices and market prospects
throughout the Upper Region.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.  His duty
station would be Bolgatanga, and his appointment would be initially for three
years.
Senior Agriculturist I/C Agricultural Technical Services
1.        The Senior Agriculturist would be an experienced senior agricul-
tural officer with at least ten years agricultural experience in Ghana. He
should have proven competence in agricultural administration, good knowledge
of extension systems, an understanding of farm management and farming systems,
and a previous association with research.
2.        He would be in charge of the Agricultural Technical Services
Division and his duties would include:
(a) preparation of division budgets and their subsequent control;
(b) liaising with crop research institute in the development of
seven regional applied-research units;
(c) developing seed multiplication and certified seed production
schemes;



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 6
(d) developing farm management capability to assist larger
farmers;
(e) recommending technical input packages, quantities and timing
of supply;
(f) establishing an extension service to support rainfed and
irrigation farmers;
(g) supervising a special cotton development section;
(h) controlling planting materials imports to the Upper Region
from Upper Volta through effective quarantine systems;
(i) improving human nutrition by education programs via agricultural
extension workers;
(j) developing small scale engineering industries such as village
blacksmiths;
(k) advising ADB Bolgatanga on the technical merit of large scale
farmers loans applications; and
(1) providing specialized services to large scale irrigation
schemes developed by the Department of Irrigation.
3.        The Senior Agriculturist would be directly responsible to the
Chief of Field Operations. His duty station would be Bolgatanga and his
initial assignment would be for three years.
Senior Land Development Officer
1.        The Senior Land Development Officer would be an experienced land
planner. His experience would include photogrammetric interpretation of
aerial photography, land use analysis, skeletal land planning, design and
construction of small earthdams, design and construction of soil conservation
work, and administration.
2.        The Senior Land Development Officer would head the Land Development
Division and his responsibilities would include:
(a) land planning and land use survey;
(b) preparation of skeletal development plans;
(c) location and design of dams;
(d) design of soil conservation works;



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 7
(e) preparation of plans, drawings and general cartographic work;
(f) control and coordination of the Soil and Water Conservation
Working Account (SWCA);
(g) implementation of the village wells and farm grain stores
program through the Department of Social Welfare and Community
Development; and
(h) preparation and control of divisional budgets.
3.        The Senior Land Development Officer would be directly responsible
to the Chief of Field Operations; his duty station would be Bolgatanga, and
his initial assignment would be for three years.
Senior Animal Husbandry Officer
1.        The Senior Animal Husbandry Officer should have experience in
improvement programs for cattle, sheep, goats and poultry. He should have
had at least five years relevant experience, preferably in the Upper Region,
and should be a good organizer.
2.        He would head the Animal Husbandry Division and his duties would
include:
(a) preparation and control of Divisional budget;
(b) development of ten small ranches (2,000 ha each) in Tumu district,
with assistance from a specialized ranch development officer;
(c) development of small scale farm fattening schemes;
(d) development of simple livestock management and animal feeding
systems in conjunction with the applied research units; and
(e) training of farmers in methods of ox training.
3.        The Senior Animal Husbandry Officer would be directly responsible
to the Chief of Field Operations; his duty station would be Bolgatanga,
and his initial assignment would be for three years.
Senior Veterinarian
1.        The Senior Veterinarian should be an experienced officer with at
least ten years experience in Ghana.
2.        He would be head of the Veterinary Services Division and his
duties would include:



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 8
(a) preparation and control of veterinary services budget;
(b) responsibility for all aspects concerning animal health,
including the operation of animal vaccination campaigns
against endemic diseases;
(c) the monitoring of tsetse fly and trypanosomiasis; and
(d) collection of samples for analysis at Pong Tamale veterinary
laboratories.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Chief of Field Operations;
his duty station would be Bolgatanga, and his initial appointment would be
for three years.
Senior Fisheries Officer
1.        The Senior Fisheries Officer should have worked in inland fisheries
for at least five years, should be experienced in fish culture and should
have proven administrative ability.
2.        The Senior Fisheries Officer would head the Fisheries Division and
his duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of the fisheries budget;
(b) development of Vea fish pond complex and other ponds to be
developed under the Project;
(c) the stocking of dams with fish bred in the ponds; and
(d) extension to farmers on the catching, feeding and processing
of fish.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Chief of Field Operations;
his duty station would be Bolgatanga and his initial appointment would be
for three years.
Senior Farm Management Officer
1.        The Senior Farm Management Officer should have at least five years
experience in practical farm management and two years in advisory work. It
would be advantageous if he had successfully managed a commercial farm in
Africa.
2.        He would head the Farm Management Section and his duties would
include:



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 9
(a) preparation and control of his section's budget;
(b) provision of farm management advice to farmers;
(c) preparation of physical and financial farm plans;
(d) checking farm plans for technical soundness prior to ADB
making loans to large farmers;
(e) advising farmers on farm plan implementation; and
(f) training staff in farm management.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Senior Agriculturist, and
his duty station would be at Bolgatanga. His initial appointment would be
for three years.
Field Research Officer
1.        The Field Research Officer should be qualified in agriculture or
agricultural botany, should have had at least five years experience in agro-
nomic research, and should have managed a research program. If possible, he
should have had experience in seed multiplication and certification schemes.
2.        He would be responsible for the applied research and seed multipli-
cation and demonstration unit, and his duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of section's budget;
(b) establishing seven district applied research units;
(c) setting out simple agronomic trials;
(d) multiplying breeder seed;
(e) coordinating his activities with the Crop Research Institution
of Kumasi and Tamale;
(f) organizing simple cultural and crop demonstrations;
(g) developing an outgrower second and third stage seed multipli-
cation scheme for grains and legumes; and
(h) undertaking seed certification and germination testing.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Senior Agriculturist; his
duty station would be Bolgatanga, and his initial appointment would be for
three years.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 10
Senior Nutritionist
1.        The Senior Nutritionist should be fully qualified in human nutri-
tion and should have good experience in improving nutritional standards in
Ghana.
2.        The Senior Nutritionist would head the Nutrition Section and his
responsibilities would include:
(a) preparation and control of the Section's budget;
(b) training assistant nutrition officers;
(c) training agricultural extension workers in human
nutrition;
(d) preparing nutritional advisory programs for radio
broadcasting and demonstrations; and
(e) following up and evaluating nutrition programs in the
field.
3.        The Senior Nutritionist would be responsible to the Senior Agri-
culturist; his duty station would be Bolgatanga, and his initial appointment
would be for three years.
Fruit Tree Specialist/Horticulturist
1.        The Fruit Tree Specialist should be a qualified horticulturist with
at least five years experience in budding, grafting and nursery development.
Experience with tropical fruits would be advantageous.
2.        He would be responsible for the development of fruit tree nurseries
and his duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of budget;
(b) nursery development;
(c) selection of suitable varieties;
(d) production of root stock;
(e) budding and grafting;
(f) training nursery staff;
(g) training farmers in fruit tree multiplication;



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 11
(h) training staff and farmers in management of fruit trees.
3.        He would be responsible to the Senior Forestry Officer; his duty
station would be Bolgatanga, and his initial appointment would be for three
years.
Senior Mechanical Engineer
1.        The Senior Mechanical Engineer should be a fully qualified mecha-
nical engineer experienced in heavy machinery, plant maintenance, and work-
shop control. He should have had at least ten years experience with either
a Government organization or, more preferably, with a commercial company.
2.        He would head the Soil and Water Conservation Working Account and
his duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of SWCA budget;
(b) control of workshops and plant;
(c) ordering spare parts;
(d) developing operating and maintenance schedules;
(e) fuel control;
(f) training mechanics; and
(g) implementing controlled recording systems for unit costing
and performance purposes.
3.        The Senior Mechanical Engineer would be directly responsible to the
Senior Land Development Officer; his duty station would be Bolgatanga and
his initial assignment would be for three years.
Mechanical Engineer (two)
1.        The two Mechanical Engineers should be experienced in maintenance
of heavy plant and light vehicles respectively. Each should have had at
least ten years appropriate experience.
2.        They would work in the SWCA workshops at Zwarungu and their duties
would include:
(a) control of spare parts stores;
(b) maintenance of heavy plant and light vehicles;
