Page 12
(c) operation of mobile workshops; and
(d) training mechanics.
3.        They would be responsible to the Senior Mechanical Engineer; their
duty station would be Bolgatanga, and their initial appointment would be for
three years.
Ranch Development Officer
1.        The Ranch Development Officer should be experienced in commercial
ranching, with at least ten years experience managing a minimum of 5,000 head
of cattle. Preferably he should have ranched successfully in East or Central
Africa.
2.        He would be responsible for the development of ten group or company
ranches, each 2,000 ha in extent, and his duties would include:
(a) acquiring land;
(b) detailing physical planning;
(c) selecting applicants (technical selection);
(d) reviewing applicants' existing herds;
(e) assisting successful applicants in preparing ranch plans and
loan requests to ADB;
(f) assisting in the formation of companies;
(g) assisting owners in managing their ranches;
(h) responsibility for sales and purchase policies, and for the
control of stocking rates; and
(i) training local men as ranch managers.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Senior Animal Husbandry
Officer; his duty station would be in Tumu District, and his initial assignment
would be for three years.
Fish Culturist
1.        The Fish Culturist should be a fully qualified biologist spec.al-
izing in inland fisheries. He should have had at least ten years experience
in fish farming or fish farming research.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 13
2.        He would be responsible for quantifying the financial and technical
parameters for intensive fish-pond farming and less intensive fish production
in dams. His duties would include:
(a) advising the Senior Fisheries Officer on the technical require-
ments for the construction of fish ponds;
(b) providing technical advice on fish pond management;
(c) experimenting with fish species (local to Ghana) and developing
feeding regimes;
(d) developing fish breeding techniques; and

Functional Adult Literacy Officer
1.        The Functional Adult Literacy Officer should be fully qualified and
experienced in functional adult literacy.
2.        He would lead a small pilot scheme to test functional literacy in
two of the main languages of the Upper Region. His duties would include:
(a) assisting in selecting the two areas and defining their
parameters;
(b) preparing a phased program;
(c) training staff;
(d) implementing the program;
(e) advising Ghana Broadcasting Corporation on back-up radio
services for functional literacy;
(f) evaluating the results of the pilot scheme; and
(g) if successful, preparing a second phase scheme.
3.        He would be directly responsible to the Senior Communications and
Training Officer. His duty station would be Bolgatanga and his initial
appointment would be for three years.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 14
Plant Breeder
1.        URPMU would contract with an international research institute,
preferably the International Institute for Tropical Agriculture (IITA), for
a Plant Breeder to be seconded to the Crop Research Institute of Ghana.
2.        The Plant Breeder should have an advanced degree in agronomy and
should have at least seven years experience in cereal breeding, specifically
maize, sorghum and millet. He should have a proven record and should be
fully aware of current research programs, particularly those at the Institute
of Agricultural Research, Samaru, northern Nigeria. The Plant Breeder would
receive back-up support from his parent research institute.
3.        The Plant Breeder would be stationed at Manga Research Center, in
the Upper Region, and would:
(a) carry out adaptive research trials on imported improved
seeds from Nigeria and elsewhere;
(b) conduct varietal trials on selected Ghanaian varieties;
(c) conduct fertilizer trials;
(d) conduct mixed cropping trials;
(e) test improved varieties of cowpeas, groundnuts and other
annual crops; and
(f) together with URPMU's Field Research Officer make annual
technical recommendations for crops to the Chief of
Field Operations.
4.        The Plant Breeder would be responsible to the Director of the Crop
Research Institute of Kumasi. His appointment would be for five years.
Irrigation Management Specialist
1.        The Irrigation Specialist should be a professional agronomist who
has extensive experience in managing irrigation schemes. He should have had
at least five years experience on a successful irrigation project.
2.        The Irrigation Specialist would be responsible to the Senior
Extension and Development Officer, and his duties would include:
(a) preparing a plan of management for Vea irrigation scheme, and,
after approval by URPMU and the Irrigation Department, being
responsible for its implementation;



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 15
(b) establishing a committee to control the scheme and training
a manager to operate the plan;
(c) planning, in conjunction with the Farm Management Section, farm
plans for individual irrigation farmers;
(d) advising the Field Research Section on the needs for irrigation
field trials;
(e) responsibility for small scale irrigation schemes in relation
to the dam development program.
3.        The Irrigation Management Specialist would be stationed at
Bolgatanga and his appointment would be for three years.
Cotton Development Officer
1.        The Cotton Development Officer would be a senior officer of the
Cotton Development Board and would be seconded to URPMU. He would be respon-
sible to the Senior Agriculturist.
2.        The Cotton Development Officer would be responsible for the opera-
tion of the special Cotton Development Section. His duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of the Section's budget;
(b) establishing proper technical packages for the farmers;
(c) training section staff;
(d) deploying staff;
(e) liaising with the Cotton Development Board in regard to
cotton marketing; and
(f) advising CRI on research requirements.
3.        The Cotton Development Officer would be appointed for five years
and would be stationed at Bolgatanga.
Managing Director (FSC)
1.        The Managing Director would be the chief executive of the Farmers
Services Company (UR) Ltd. (FSC).  He should have had at least ten years
experience in a large commercial company or corporation. He should have
a sound financial background, be a good administrator and have a detailed
knowledge on establishing and managing a selling organization. Experience
in Africa would be extremely relevant.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 16
2.        The Managing Director would be responsible to the Chairman of the FSC
Board of Directors.  His specific duties would include:
(a) establishing FSC as an operational farmers supply and services
company organized on efficient commercial lines;
(b) responsibility for the hiring and welfare of all Company
employees;
(c) preparation and execution of FSC capital investment program;
(d) initial development of FSC Commercial Services Department prior
to appointment of full time Commercial Services Manager;
(e) budget preparation and control;
(f) advising Board on sales policy, pricing, etc.;
(g) advising Board on limits for suppliers' credit to farmers,
and being responsible for the Company's loan portfolio;
(h) coordination with URPMU in determining input type and
quantity;
(i) training district salesmen and store keepers; and
(j) taking inventory of supplies and procuring additional inputs.
Chief Accountant (FSC)
1.        The Chief Accountant for the Farmers Services Company (UR) Ltd.
would be a qualified chartered accountant with at least ten years successful
experience in a commercial organization. Experience in a retail distribution
company would be important. He must be able to demonstrate his proven ability
to establish, operate and control company accounts. He should also be a good
administrator.
2.        The Chief Accountant would be responsible to the Managing Director
of the Farmers Services Company, and his duties would include:
(a) budget preparation and control;
(b) stores procurement and control systems;
(c) recommendations concerning Company's borrowing and investment
policies to Managing Director and Board;
(d) debt control;



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 17
(e) coordination with associated financial agencies; and
(f) Company accounting procedures, cost accounting and cost
retrieval.
3.        The Chief Accountant would be appointed initially for three years,
and his duty station would be Bolgatanga.
Agricultural Services Manager (FSC)
1.        The Agricultural Services Manager for the Farmers Services Company
should be experienced in agricultural mechanization and should be an agri-
cultural engineer. He should have relevant experience in contracting and
engineering.
2.        He would be responsible for the Farmers Services Company's agricul-
tural services to farmers, initially comprising contract hiring of tractors
and combine harvesters to farmers, control of workshops for FSC fleet
maintenance and maintenance of farmers' tractors. He would be responsible
to the Managing Director, FSC.
3.        His duties would include:
(a) budget preparation and control for the Agricultural Services
Section;
(b) deployment of machinery;
(c) maintenance schedules;
(d) advising Board on economic and commercial contractual rates
for hire of equipment;
(e) time recording of equipment use;
(f)  liaison with URPMU's farm management advisors;
(g) training staff.
4.        His initial appointment would be for three years and his duty
station would be Bolgatanga.
Agricultural Loans Officer (ADB)
1.        The Agricultural Loans Officer should be a fully qualified finan-
cial analyst with at least ten years experience working with farmers. He
should have been a senior member of a commercial bank or a development bank.
He should be able to demonstrate his success in farm budgeting and cash flow
analysis.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 18
2.        He would be responsible for advising and training loans officers
in the Agricultural Development Bank's branch at Bolgatanga. His duties
would include:
(a) reviewing all existing regional farm loans and advising on
appropriate action;
(b) reviewing new loan applications, including the ranch loans
included under the Project;
(c) training ADB Loans Officers; and
(d) liaising with URPMU Farm Management Officers and extension
officers to determine ADB Bolgatanga lending policies, including
a review of cooperative development and other forms of group
lending.
3.        He would be responsible to the Manager of the ADB Bolgatanga branch;
his duty station would be Bolgatanga, and his appointment would be for three
years.
Financial Controller to Upper Regional Development Corporation (URDECO)
1.        The Financial Controller should be a fully qualified chartered
accountant with at least ten years experience in company management, financial
control and accounting. Experience related to transportation and wholesaling
would be advantageous.
2.        He would be employed by the Upper Regional Development Corporation
(URDECO) and would be responsible to the Managing Director for the financial
operations of the Corporation. His duties would include:
(a) establishing proper accounting procedures;
(b) developing an internal auditing process;
(c) responsibility for the formation of wholly owned subsidiary
transport and wholesale companies;
(d) advising on borrowing policy;
(e) reorganizing URDECO management and staffing structure where
necessary; and
(f) training management and accounting staff.
3.        He would be responsible to the Managing Director of URDECO; his duty
station would be Bolgatanga, and his appointment would be for three years.



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 19
Specialist in Local Broadcasting (GBC)
1.        A Specialist in Local Broadcasting would be retained to advise the
Ghana Broadcasting Corporation and the Project on the development of local
radio broadcasting. The Specialist should be a fully qualified radio pro-
grammer with at least five years experience working with a recognized local
radio that caters to defined urban or rural listeners. The local radio
station from which he is drawn must have catered specifically to educational
purposes as well as to news and local affairs. If possible he should be
experienced in self-operating systems of program broadcasting.
2.        He would be responsible to the Manager of the radio station at
Bolgatanga. His duties would include:
(a) in conjunction with URPMU, assisting the Station Manager in
preparing a suitable broadcasting program;
(b) training broadcasting staff in local radio orientation and
self-operating techniques;
(c) assisting in selecting staff for short overseas training
courses;
(d) assisting in the preparation of suitable broadcasting material
during the first year of the Project, prior to the opening of
the station in Year 2;
(e) analyzing the existing sources from which local material can
be obtained; and
(f) training URPMU staff in techniques and requirements for
broadcasting material.
3.        The Specialist would be appointed for two years and his station
would be at Bolgatanga.
Radio Engineer (GBC)
1.        A Radio Engineer would be retained for six months to draw up
detailed engineering specifications for the establishment of a radio station
at Bolgatanga. The Engineer should have had at least ten years experience
in radio engineering and should have detailed knowledge of local radio re-
quirements.
2.        He would be responsible to the Manager of the radio station at
Bolgatanga and would coordinate his work with the Chief Engineer of GBC and
URPMU management. His duties would include:



ANNEX 3
Schedule 5
Page 20
(a)  substantiating the case for FM or AM mode of transmission;
(b)  preparing specifications for transmitters and receivers at
Bolgatanga, Tumu, Lawra, Wa and Bawku;
(c)  preparing specifications for broadcasting input equipment
(i.e., self-operating control desks, etc.), recording, editing,
etc.;
(d)  in conjunction with the architects, developing suitable specifi-
cations and designs for the transmitting hall, studios, offices,
etc.;
(e)  preparing overall cost estimates;
(f)  drawing up final specifications and tender documents.
For full details, see Annex 8, para 36.
2.        He would be responsible for establishing the radio broadcasting
system and for testing it out in conjunction with GBC engineers.
3.        His employment would be in three parts:
(a)  specifications and tender work;
(b)  evaluation of bids; and
(c)  supervision of construction.
4.        While in Ghana he would be stationed at Bolgatanga.
March 31, 1976

GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
The Project Area
A. General Description
1.        Location.  The Project area lies between latitudes 9 042  and 11010'
N and longitudes 0  and 2 051' W, occupying  a total of 27,312 km , about 11%
of Ghana's land area. It comprises the whole Upper Region which contains
seven administrative districts: Wa, Lawra, Tumu, Navrongo, Sandema, Bolgatanga
and Bawku. The area is bordered by Togo to the east and Upper Volta to the
north and west, the latter boundary following the Black Volta River.
2.        The area lies entirely within the catchment of the Black, Red and
White Volta rivers and is characterized by a gently undulating topography
with slopes of 3-4% predominating. Hills with granite outcrops and laterite
capped mesas rise to over 500 m from levels between 150 to 250 m and generate
considerable runoff which causes natural gullying.
3.        Climate.  Annual rainfall varies from around 950 mm in the north
east at Bawku to 1,150 mm at Lawra in the northwest. Isohyets linking
rainfall stations outside of the Region indicate a zone of lowest precipita-
tion which includes northern Bawku, Navrongo and most of Tumu district.
Rainfall distribution, which is monomodal with a main peak in late August,
is largely confined to the period April to September, giving an effective
wet season of 6-7 months; drought conditions are frequently experienced
during June, a critical period for crop growth. After periods of drought
and at the end of the dry season, rainfall is frequently preceded by hail,
high winds and dust storms.
4.        Mean daily air temperatures decline from a peak of around 320C in
April to a trough of around 25 C in August with corresponding low radiation
and daily sunshine of 4-5 hours. During the dry season the harmattan blows
off the Sahara from the north east and is intensely drying; daily maximum
air temperatures during this period can reach 45 C and result in high evap-
oration from open water.  Night temperatures, however, often fall below 15 C.
5.        Soils.  Upland soils have developed mainly from granitic rocks of
the basement complex. Sparsely populated areas, 50% of the Region, are
usually associated with soils having abundant iron concretions or eroded
coarse, shallow soils with frequent rock exposures. Elsewhere soils are
light, coarse textured, with varying amounts of loosely packed stones and
concretions; stones are frequently used to form contour terraces but



ANNEX 4
Page 2
serious sheet erosion still occurs during the rainy season. Clay content
varies from 6-10% in the surface soils to 20-50% in the subsoil matrix which
can be imperfectly drained.
6.        A distinct toposequence is usually found with shallow soils on the
hill crests and moderately deep soils with colluvial accumulation on the
middle slopes. Valley hydromorphic soils are of mixed alluvial/colluvial
origin and are either heavy or have a light surface and medium subsoil tex-
ture. Cultivation of valley soils is limited by extremely wet or water-
logged conditions during the wet season, but inherent fertility is higher
than adjacent upland soils which are characteristically low in organic matter,
base exchange capacity and total exchangeable bases with medium to medium
high base saturation. Soil reaction is neutral to moderately acid (pH 7 -
5.5).
7.        Ecology.  The Region falls within the Guinea Savannah zone except
for a small area north of Bawku which phases into Sudan zone ecology.
Natural vegetation is Savannah woodland but in the settled areas this has
been reduced to an open parkland where only trees of economic value have
been retained to give a stand of 10-20 per hectare. Predominant species are
Parkia filicoidia (dawa dawa) and Butryospermum parkii (shea butter), with
locally increased numbers of Acacia albidas. Fuel for cooking is scarce, and
sorghum and millet stalks are used for this purpose. Grasses consist of
Andropogon gayanus, Hyparrhenia spp and Hecteropogon spp with, in badly
eroded areas, Aristida spp and Cymbopogan spp.
8.        Riverblindness is endemic in most of the eastern and central and in
part of the western Project areas. As a result most fertile valleys are
unsettled, but increasing pressure of land following population increases
and run-down of the upland soils is tempting owners of ox and tractor drawn
plows to cultivate the main river valleys in sequences involving only short
visits to infested areas.
9.        Population.  The 1970 population census recorded a total of roundly
865,000 persons, which equated to a growth rate of only 1.4% per annum since
the previous 1960 census; this low growth rate is probably explained by
permanent and seasonal migration for work in southern Ghana.
10.       Population distribution varies markedly through the area and
largely reflects soil suitability for cultivation.  Paris of Wa and Lawra
districts have population densities exceeding 50 per km , but elsewhere in
the western and central sectors density falls to less than 5.  5ast of Tumu
district density steadily increases, reaching nearly 100 per km in Bawku
district (see Table 1).



ANNEX 4
Page 3
11.       The population is represented by nine ethnic groups with separate
languages, 1/ none of which serves as a lingua franca. Patterns of kinship
and basic social structure are, however, similar. By tradition, tribal power
is centered or the paramount chief who has subordinate hereditary chiefs.
Each subchieftaincy may consist of several villages each with their own head-
man or village chief who is usually a nominee of the subchief. While the
traditional juridical role of the chiefs is not formally recognized by the
State, they remain the principal authority in village life and close parti-
cipation in Project activities would be essential.
12.       According to the 1970 agricultural sample census, average family
size in the area is 6.8 persons. Settlement throughout is in the form of
scattered compounds usually housing from 2-5 families; a village may comprise
up to 500 compounds involving a number of clan lineages. Compound families
usually operate as basic social units who cultivate and store their subsis-
tance food crops communally, these operations having priority over individual
family farm cultivation. Groupings of compounds are quite common, as is
informal cooperation in certain farming activities. This in-built coopera-
tion will be developed into the formation, under the Project, of farmers
associations, through which extension and other services would operate.
13.       Communication.  The Region has no rail link and all freight has
to be transported either by road via Kumasi (approximately 800 km) or by
road from Tema port to Akosombo on the Volta Lake (85 km), then by barge
to Yapi and hence by road to Bolgatanga (210 km). Only one barge with a
capacity of 200 tons presently operates to Yapi taking four days for the
round trip. Proposals are under consideration, however, for up to nine
additional barges for the lake.
14.       The total road network in the area is roundly 2,000 km providing
partly bituminized connection to district headquarters and an adequate net-
work of feeder roads for an initial Project development phase. Rehabilita-
tion of much of this network is necessary, however, and is included in an
ongoing feeder road improvement program funded by Government under the
Ministry of Social Welfare and Community Development. 2/ Under the IBRD
Second Highways Project, provision is made for maintenance of 50% of Ghana's
22,500 km of feeder roads. Government has assured the Bank in a side letter
to the Second Highways Project that about 800 km of the Upper Region's feeder
roads would receive priority in road maintenance; with this assurance, the
proposed Project could operate without constraint.
1/   Kusaal, Gurenne (Frafra), Buli, Kasem, Sisaala, Chakali, Wali, Dagaari,
Kona.
2/   The responsibility for this program has now been transferred to the
Ghana Highway Authority (GRA).



ANNEX 4
Page 4
15.       The distance from Bolgatanga, the Region's headquarters, to Tamale
airport is 160 km by bituminized road. Telephone services are provided within
Bolgatanga but connection outside and to Accra is only by radio.
16.       Services.  The Region has seven hospitals (four Government, three
mission) with a total of 338 beds. In addition there are 20 health posts and
a further ten due for construction during the current fiscal year. Schools
number 303 primary (31,000 pupils), 130 middle (12,700 pupils), and 15 secon-
dary (3,500 pupils), and there are three teachers training colleges (830 trainees).
The educational effort has so far not achieved popular success, however, and
130 primary schools had to be closed in 1970/71 due to uneconomic enrollments.
Only 22% of the primary age group are in school (compared with 62% for Ghana
as a whole) and 66% of them drop out during the primary course. Some 80-90%
of the population is believed to be illiterate, but most adults are numerate.
17.       Reticulated water supplies are provided for the Region's main towns;
elsewhere people rely on surface (rivers and dams) supplies and shallow wells.
In the eastern sector there is an increasing incidence of dried up wells
which may indicate lowering of the water table, possibly due to increased
runoff resulting from unmaintained conservation works and generally rising
land pressure.
18.       A limited program of assistance for shallow well construction has
been provided in Sandema district, 1/ and an ongoing Canadian financed bore-
hole program aims at supplying one groundwater well for every 300 persons.
This target will not be met in the current phase, however, which provides for
drilling 1,100 wells by the end of 1976; moreover, the distribution of planned
wells shows a heavy concentration in the Navrongo area. A clearer picture
of requirements for wells can be obtained only after a comprehensive inven-
tory of existing water supply facilities. This, in connection with a survey
of rehabilitation requirements for existing wells, is planned under the
Canadian Technical Assistance program.
B. Agriculture
19.       Farming Systems.  Although the pattern of agriculture is fairly
consistent throughout the Region, production systems vary according to
population pressure, incidence of tsetse fly and results of previous devel-
opment undertakings by the Ministry of Agriculture. Thus in the sparsely
populated Tumu district a basic system of bush/fallow cultivation is prac-
ticed where land is cropped for two to four years and then allowed to revert
to fallow vegetation for five to ten years. During the fallow period plant
nutrients are accumulated through the fallow vegetation root system and
transferred to the surface soil through leaf wash and litter fall. A member
1/   33 wells were constructed on a self-help basis in 1973.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
of the land holding group who is first to cultivate a tract of land estab-
lishes the right to use that land for himself and his descendants for as
long as they continue to cultivate it; since farmers are aware of soil
differences and their suitability for different crops, this often results
in a number of plots being established at different sites to take advantage
of available soil types.
20.       To the west and east of the Region where denser settlement patterns
are found, bush/fallow cultivation is combined with, and to some extent re-
placed by, permanent cultivation of land immediately surrounding the compound;
fertility is replenished using household refuse, stock manure and night soil
as well as deliberate inclusion of legumes in the crop rotation. Compound
farms, which are used mainly for food grain production, are often cultivated
communally by compound families and the produce stored in a common granary.
According to land availability, additional individual bush farms are also
cultivated, often at considerable distance (up to ten km) from the compound.
21.       Although cattle are owned by most farmers in the Region, in the
western sector of Wa and Lawra these are normally herded away from the com-
pound by Fulani herdsmen. In the eastern sector, however, a mixed farming
system is often practiced involving use of animal power for land preparation
and deliberate production of farmyard manure which is applied to the per-
manently cropped compound farms. This system was introduced by the Colonial
Administration in the 1930's to check rapid soil deterioration resulting from
high population pressure. Common grazing areas for village cattle are agreed
at periodic meetings summoned by the village chief; farmers who have to
surrender part of their fields do so readily as this is done on a reciprocal
basis.
22.       Various estimates of farm size are available, but these show consid-
erable contradictions both in absolute terms and in the relative differences
between different sectors of the Region. 1/ In attempting to rationalize
these differences, farm size and production estimates have been compared and
overall cultivated area examined on LANDSAT photography. In view of the
rather substantial differences in the various data sources, only rounded
estimates, which reflect the crop production system's general order of magni-
tude, are feasible. A summary is provided in Table 2 and indicates an over-
all average cultivated area of 2.4 hectares per farm family.
23.       Except for commercial state farms and a limited number of mechanized
rice farms in the larger valleys, production is based on smallholder cultiva-
tion. About 120 tractors and around 8,000 pairs of bullocks operate in the
1/   These estimates include the 1970 Sample Census of Agriculture and the
subsequent series of Current Agricultural Statistics. Also available
are various localized surveys; for example, at Bawku district, Field
Study (1969) by Uchenda and Anthony, and crop production information
from the Ministry of Agriculture. Local opinion is usually accurate
on relative differences in farm size between different sectors.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
Region, expansion of the latter being limited by the high cost of bullocks 1/
and availability of ox-drawn equipment. Around 80% of farmed land is still
cultivated entirely by hand.
24.       Land Tenure.  Land is a communal asset allocated to community
members according to customary usufruct. Actual ownership is vested in
lineage, and the land, therefore, has a sacral character as the real property
of ancestors. Moral and religious authority over the land, in the name of the
ancestors, is vested in the Tengana or "Priest of the Earth," a custom extend-
ing back to the time before invaders from the north conquered the local
people and mixed with them. The chief's authority is, therefore, more recent
and political, though both share rights of land disposal and allocation.
Individuals normally acquire land by virtue of being a member of the kindred
group; such rights are inherited patrilineally. Newcomers may come to live in
the area by permission of the Tengana and must accept his authority.
25.       Crop Production.  Depending on farm size, 70-95% of cultivated land
is planted to foodcrops, of which early and late millet and tall sorghum
(Guinea corn) are the most important; the latter is secondary to millet as
a food grain, a large proportion of production being brewed into local beer
(pito). In the western sector maize and yams are important constituents of
the diet, usually at the expense of millet production.
26.       Principal cash crops are groundnuts and rice, the latter of more
recent importance following increased ox and tractor cultivation in the
large valleys. Groundnuts are grown by most farmers and attract a steady
trade to the south for oil crushing. Cotton, which is still a relatively
minor crop, is now in strong demand following recent substantial producer
price rises. 2/ Cowpeas and bambara beans, which are common and important
constitutents of the local diet, also enjoy a significant cash trade, and
there is some kenaf production promoted by the Bast Fibre Development Board.
An estimate of the proportion of holdings growing these crops in the eastern
and western sectors is given in Table 2.
27.       Few crops are grown in pure stand and admixtures of four or five
different crops are frequently encountered; as a result, no easy mathematical
definition of the crop production complex is possible, and cropped areas can
be realistically presented only in simple groupings according to the nature
of the mixtures in which they are found. Those presented in Table 3 are
drawn from the various data sources mentioned in para 22, with apparent discre-
pancies and contradictions rationalized as far as possible by observation and
local questioning.
28.       Nutrition.  Market records, nutrition surveys and crop yield esti-
mates indicate a sensitive balance of production in which many families are
susceptible to mal- and undernourishment, particularly in the eastern sector.
Surveys have shown that about 40% of preschool children are underweight,
especially those of poorer families, while family consumption at the end
1/   Presently around US $300-400 each.
2/   From ¢0.08 to 00.35 per lb.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
of the dry season when field operations begin and the work load is heavy is
often 60% of energy requirements, along with serious lack of protein and
vitamins in the foods consumed. The consequences are high infant mortality
due to undernourishment of the mother, retarded mental and physical develop-
ment, and lethargy in adults.
30.       An estimated food balance sheet, based on an average season, is
given in Table 4. Following increased production during Project implementa-
tion, a significant increase in regional food consumption can be expected.
C. Regional Development
31.       Background.  Concentrated attempts to develop agriculture in the
Upper Region date back to the 1930s during British Administration when a
demonstration farm was established at Garu, followed by an agricultural
station at Manga Bawku; operating from these centers, an attempt was made
to introduce ox cultivation and mixed farming systems to smallholders in the
eastern sector where rising population pressure was resulting in serious
sheet erosion and a gradual decline in soil fertility. Though interrupted
by World War II, the work progressed steadily for the next 25 years,
complemented during the 1940s by soil and water conservation works which
extended throughout the Region.
32.       By the late 1950s, ox cultivation and the use of farmyard manure
were widely adopted. District agricultural stations were established in each
of the then six districts 1/ and the Regional Soil and Water Conservation
Unit had established six land planning areas 2/ where controlled cultivation
and grazing systems were practiced, and had constructed 98 dams, 400 miles
of narrow based terraces, 1,670 acres of plantation, 320 acres of bunded
valley rice land and around 300 acres of organized dry season gardens. Out-
side of the land planning areas, bullock farmers were encouraged to follow
the line of permanent three-foot marker (contour) ridges located by the
agricultural extension staff using line levels and marked with an ox plow.
33.       Following independence in 1962, Government agricultural policy was
directed principally to large scale mechanized farming units which were estab-
lished under the United Ghana Farmers' Cooperative Council (UGFCC) 3/ at the
district agricultural stations. Attention to smallholders decreased corres-
pondingly; conservation measures were decried and either neglected or destroyed.
Ox cultivation was discouraged; a meat packing station established in the area
eliminated many animals.
1/   Sandema was originally part of Navrongo District.
2/   At Tomne, Dadora, Sielo-Tuni, Bumbugu, Suarungu and Wiaga.
3/   UGFCC was an integral part of the Convention People's Party.



ANNEX 4
Page 8
34.       UGFCC was disestablished in 1967 after the ousting of the Conven-
tion People's Party. Subsequent efforts have been made to revitalize the
agricultural stations to their original function and retrain farmers in
forgotten techniques. Though capable technicians and experienced demon-
strators exist within the regional extension service, their efforts and
enthusiasm are diluted by shortage of funds and a weak administrative frame-
work for development.
35.       A number of institutions and agencies, other than the Ministry of
Agriculture and its various boards and corporations which have been described
in the main text of this report, are directly or indirectly involved in the
Region's development; their close integration within the overall framework
of the Project would be essential.
36.       Local Government.  At regional level the Ministry of Local Govern-
ment coordinates the functions and activities of all the various ministries
with regard to overall policy objectives of the Government. It is directly
responsible for the district administrations, headed by a district administra-
tion officer, and aided by a district council and a district court. The
district administration operates as an entity separate from the traditional
authority, though one third of council members are representatives of the
house of Chiefs; the remaining two thirds are appointed by the Supreme
Military Council.
37.       Roads and Welfare.  The Ghana Highways Authority is responsible
for construction, rehabilitation and maintenance of primary and major secon-
dary roads, as well as a limited number of feeder roads. It operates from
a regional center in Bolgatanga and a number of district substations. The
Ministry of Social Welfare and Community Development is responsible for com-
munity development activities in the Upper Region including rural crafts,
self-help schemes, sanitation, rural housing (roof loan scheme) and an adult
literacy program.
38.       Trade and Industry are the responsibility of the Ministry of Trade
and Industry (MTI) whose commercial operations are directed through the
Ghana Industrial Holding Corporation (GIHOC). The Cannery Division of GIHOC
operates a tomato canning factory in the Upper Region with a daily capacity of
100 tons. Established with Yugoslavian aid, the factory has never been used
to its capacity; total throughput in 1974 was only 100 tons. The Region's
production of about 5,000 tons of tomatoes has mostly been absorbed by private
trade, which is able to offer more favorable prices than the factory, since
at the time of tomato harvest in the Upper Region, supply to the south from
other areas has largely stopped. To ensure supply for its factory, the
corporation has acquired its own farm at Vea irrigation scheme with 160
hectares and hopes to double the area under its own production at a later
stage.
39.       GIHOC Meat Products Division operates a slaughterhouse with a
meat canning factory in Bolgatanga. It buys cattle on the open market and



ANNEX 4
Page 9
transports carcasses in refrigerated vans to Kumasi and Accra for sale in
its own retail shop. The canning factory has a capacity of 25,000 cattle
per year but is presently only 25% used. As experience has shown in other
West African countries where similar canning factories have been established
(Niger, Chad, Cameroon), the present excess of fresh meat demand over supply
does not allow an economic utilization of the established canning capacities;
under the Project the Meat Products Division would compete as a buyer of
cattle produced on the ranches. The Meat Products Division also foresees
ranching operations in the Upper Region, but so far no preinvestment studies
have been made.
40.       The Cattle Development Board of MTI has operated since 1972 as the
monopoly for marketing all imported cattle. Up to March 1973, the Board's
total purchases were 18,380 cattle and 2,300 sheep and goats, but purchases
have considerably increased since then, and the Board is now buying at a
rate of 5,000 cattle per month. Under the Project the Board would act as
importing agency for cattle, either to stock ranches or as draft animals.
The Board plans a breeding/fattening ranch on about 6,000 acres in the
Sandema district; the investment study prepared by the National Investment Bank
foresees total costs for this enterprise of 02.5 million.
41.       Additional commercial undertakings are operated by the Upper
Regional Development Corporation (URDC) which was set up in 1972-73 by the
Ministry of Economic Planning with financial support and guarantee from the
Bank of Ghana.
42.       Special Projects.  Bilateral assistance from the Federal Republic
of Germany was provided in 1970 in the form of a fertilizer distribution
project which was subsequently expanded, with supporting technical assistance,
into the Ghanaian-German Agricultural Development Project (GGADP). Project
activities include fertilizer and variety trials, seed treatment and multi-
plication, land clearing (Northern Region only), rehabilitation of rice mills,
extension staff training and extension aids, bullock training and input
storage construction. In the Upper Region this project has provided seed
and fertilizer storage capacity for about 9,000 tons, mainly as central
stores at regional and district level. Fertilizer and seed distribution in
1974 amounted to 3,519 and 304 tons respectively. Most of the fertilizer
was used for paddy which accounted for over 90% of the seed distributed.
Multiplication of some improved sorghum, groundnuts and maize seed has only
started, but is planned to be expanded as increasing seed multiplication
capacity permits.
43.       Two technical assistance experts work permanently in the Upper
Region, engaged mainly in organizing input distribution and additional staff
training. Two volunteers are building up a training center in the Wa
district. The technical assistance staff work as advisors to the Ministry
of Agriculture. GGADP's final plans for expansion are yet to be finalized
but are expected to include expansion of the Crop Research Institute's
station at Nyankpala (Northern Region) and Manga substation (Upper Region),
with the participation of German universities.



ANNEX 4
Page 10
44.       The Christian Service Committee of the Christian Council of Ghana
operates five agricultural stations attached to missions in the Upper Region
(Garu, and Binana-Bawku district, Nandou-Lawra district, Wiaga-Sandema
district, Bongo-Bolgatanga district). Monitored by a Dutch agricultural
expert at Garu Station and assisted by six Canadian volunteers, these
stations have developed comprehensive agricultural and rural assistance pro-
grams for their surrounding areas. They comprise the following activities,
each of which represents a separate program: agronomic extension, dry season
gardening, tree crop development, poultry and rabbit development, farm stor-
age construction, well building, bullock training and supply of implements,
some farm credit and the formation of credit unions. These stations have
built up valuable experience, but apart from fertilizer supplied from GGADP,
they lack the necessary support to broaden these activities and to put them
on a permanent footing. The Project would benefit substantially from this
experience, which has had the opportunity to screen development techniques
through an intensive approach in a specific area. This work would be com-
plemented by the institutional support of the Project and the opportunity to
expand proven techniques on a regional basis.
45.       The CSC also conducts a nutrition program coordinated by a British
nutritionist. Rehabilitation centers for malnourished children are operated
in Bawku and Lawra where mothers are also taught practical meal preparation
and child care. Mass screening of malnourished children is carried out with
the help of a mobile clinic; a nutrition extension service has been built up
to demonstrate vegetable growing, poultry keeping, meat preparation and child
care. Very little experience of these successful efforts in an area of high
incidence of malnutrition seems to be reflected in the Regional Government
Nutrition Service. Since the church financed programs are in danger of being
discontinued, thus losing also the trained staff, the Project would attempt
to strengthen the Regional Government Nutrition Service by building upon the
experience gained from this program.
December 4, 1975

Page 1
GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Livestock Development
I. Introduction
1.        Livestock accounts for less than 5% of the output of the agricul-
tural sector (which in turn accounts for about 40% of GDP). Apart from a
well-developed poultry industry, Ghana's livestock subsector is little de-
veloped. About 750,000 head of cattle, 900,000 sheep, 750,000 goats, 125,000
pigs and 4 million poultry produce only about 40% of the domestic meat
consumption. While marketed milk production is virtually nonexistent, the
country is almost self-sufficient in egg production. However, Ghana's natural
grazing land could support three to four times the present population of
grazing animals.
2.        About three quarters of the national cattle herd, half of the
sheep, goats and pigs, and one quarter of the poultry are concentrated in the
Upper and Northern Regions. One sixth of the national herd is kept in the
coastal savannahs in the southeast. The remainder, mainly sheep and goats, is
scattered throughout the forest area, which separates the two main livestock
producing areas.
II. The Livestock Subsector
A. Production Systems
3.        Traditional Herd.  The majority of livestock is kept in the tradi-
tional sector, with multiple purposes such as (a) meat production; (b) stor-
age of wealth, easily converted into cash; (c) draft power; and (d) social
obligations and status. Even though there are strong incentives to increase
the herds, livestock producers lack management and know-how, and cattle are
susceptible to disease; thus neither optimum offtake rates are obtained nor
are desirable products marketed. Offtake rates are estimated at 9-10% for
cattle (allowing 1-2% herd growth) and 30-32% for sheep and goats. Little is
known about the productivity of pigs and poultry.
4.        Nearly all livestock is owned by sedentary subsistence farmers who
sometimes employ Fulani herdsmen. About one third of the Ghanaian farmers
own grazing livestock and about half of them poultry. However, in the Upper
Region (see table below) over one third of the farmers own cattle, over



ANNEX 5
Page 2
two thirds sheep or goats, and about nine tenths poultry. Herd sizes range
from 2 to 25 head, with only a few larger herd owners.
Upper                       Total
Region                       Ghana
No.                      No.
('000)       %           ('000)         %
Cattle Holders                 41         34              69          9
Sheep Holders                  53         44             184         23
Goat Holders                    79        67             220         27
Poultry Holders                103        87             456         57
5.        Management standards are low and, given the small size of herds, dif-
ficult to improve. Grazing stock are not integrated with crop production, and
livestock frequently graze unattended in the bush or on fallow land. During
the cropping season, livestock are herded to avoid crop damage. At night they
are driven into kraals without access to feed. The absence of supplementary
dry season feeding results in 10-20% losses in liveweight that can be avoided
only when crop husbandry standards improve. Apart from the vaccination cam-
paigns carried out by the Veterinary Services, there is no systematic control
of parasites and disease.
6.        Oxdrawn cultivation, which can increase the crop area per farming
family and reduce labor requirements, is not very common, except in the north-
eastern part of the country. Work oxen rarely receive food supplements
during the cultivating season, and their performance is unsatisfactory.
7.        Given the present neglect of livestock, improvements in animal
husbandry standards have a long way to go, in spite of the vast potential
for additional cash incomes and the growing importance for agriculture (soil
fertility, work oxen).
8.        Commercial beef production comprises 7% of the national herd, with
operations mostly owned by traders or businessmen around Accra. There are a
number of new ventures which could be of major significance for the develop-
ment of the cattle industry. The Bank of Ghana has employed Gunn Rural
Management (Australia) to develop a commercial cattle ranch (3,150 head on
13,500 acres) on the Accra Plains. The Ghana Livestock Company, a joint
venture betweeen Government, ADB and three commercial banks, and assisted
by a US$2 million IDA credit, develops three beef ranches in Pong Tamale,
Tadzewu and Branam, and assists 50 private livestock producers with ranch
development loans and technical services. The Cattle Development Board,
charged with the importation and distribution of slaughter stock, operates
a holding ground for cattle fattening near Accra and a small ranch (Kologu)



ANNEX 5
Page 3
near Bolgatanga. The Board is in the process of acquiring a holding ground
near Prang and at Yapei, both bordering Volta Lake, with the intention of
finishing feeder steers originating from neighboring countries to the
north.
9.        Dairy units, with imported cattle from Canada, the Netherlands and
the UK, have been started in recent years by the Animal Husbandry Division
of the Ministry of Agriculture and by the universities, with mixed results.
There is so far no dairy industry of importance in Ghana.
10.       Commercial sheep and goat farming has been started by URDECO on two
farms, but results have so far been mixed.
B. Livestock Breeds
11.       Cattle.  Over 90% of Ghana's cattle are of the West African Short-
horn type, one of the smallest cattle breeds in the world. Pure N'Damas
and Zebus represent 4% and 1% respectively, and the remainder (about 3%) are
Sanghas - crosses between the humped Zebu and the humpless typanotolerant
W.A. Shorthorn or N'Dama.  Mature W.A. Shorthorn males weigh about 550 lbs;
carcass weights are about 275 and 225 lbs., respectively, for males and
females. They are well adapted to the adverse local environment and have
several advantages: they are trypanotolerant; have an appreciable degree of
resistence to streptothrichosis; respond to higher levels of feeding and
management, and under such conditions are fertile, early-maturing, and produce
carcasses of reasonable quality. However, under existing management condi-
tions, productivity is low, with calving rates about 55%, calf losses 30%,
immature losses 8%, and adult losses 5%; these permit an annual offtake of
9-10% and 1-2% herd growth. The W.A. Shorthorn is unsuitable for milk pro-
duction.
12.       The N'Dama, originating from Guinea, is the largest of the trypano-
tolerant cattle. Under field conditions, mature males weigh about 650 lbs.
and mature females 550 lbs. While there is no proof of the greater efficiency
of the N'Dama in converting herbage into beef, it has a number of definite
advantages over the W.A. Shorthorn: in a situation of low stocking rates,
the heavier N'Dama is able to utilize more grazing; bullocks are stronger,
and therefore more useful as work oxen; and carcasses are bigger and more
acceptable to the urban market. Upgrading the W.A. Shorthorn with the
N'Dama is, therefore, fully justified.
13.       Zebu cattle (mainly White Fulani) occur in very small numbers in
the Upper Region, on Government stations or commercial ranches. They are
not trypanotolerant. Under local management standards, mature males are
estimated to weigh about 770 lbs and mature females 650 lbs. In a tsetse-
free environment or under tsetse protection, the Zebu is preferable to the
N'Dama for upgrading the W.A. Shorthorn because of its larger size, its
better suitability for draft purposes, the superior milk yields of suckling
cows, and the more desirable carcass weight.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
14.       The Sanga, deriving from the cross of humpless cattle with the
Zebu, has several advantages. Being trypanotolerant to a certain extent,
Sanga cattle are more suitable than the humpless cattle breeds under con-
ditions of low tsetse challenge because of their superior size and power.
Mature Sanga males weight about 700 lbs and mature females about 600 lbs
under existing farming conditions.
15.       Sheep and Goats.  The predominant sheep is the West African Dwarf
with a mature liveweight of about 40 lbs. Sometimes they are crossed with
the bigger Fulani sheep. There are plans to import larger sized sheep from
the Sudan to be used for upgrading the local stock. At a lambing rate of
about 90% and death losses of about 25%, the annual offtake is 30%. Worm
infestations and foot rot are regarded as the most important constraints to
the full utilization of the production potential.
16.       The indigenous dwarf goat, weighing only about 35 lbs at maturity,
is extraordinarily fertile, frequently producing twins and reproducing more
than once a year under favorable conditions. Although they receive hardly
any attention, the goats have an estimated offtake rate of about 32%, result-
ing from a reproductive rate of 110%, and 30% mortality for young animals.
Major causes of the latter are internal parasites and pneumonia.
17.       Pigs.  The indigenous pig, a hardy small, long-bodied, black animal
with good disease resistance, is kept mainly as a scavenger on smallholdings.
Commercial piggeries, centered around the cities in the south and based
mainly on imported feed, prefer Large Whites, which are quite hardy in a
tropical climate. The prospects for increased pig production in the Upper
Region are poor because of the high price of pig feed and the long distance
to the consumer centers.
18.       Poultry.  Indigenous fowl, which is mainly kept by backyard poultry
farmers, is small (about 3 lbs liveweight), very hardy, and lays about 30
eggs of about 35 grams each in a lay. They are frequently kept together with
Guinea fowl in a semiwild state and contribute an important source of protein.
Guinea fowl suffer greatly from Newcastle disease; vaccination against the
most important diseases would substantially increase their productivity.
19.       Under improved husbandry, the performance of hybrid poultry is so
much superior that local fowl are not competitive. Due to relatively high
feed prices and long distances to the market, commercial poultry production
has only limited prospects in the Upper Region.
C. Animal Health
20.       Animal health is one of the main constraints to improved livestock
production. Most of the common West African animal diseases remain endemic
and a serious threat to the industry, due to uncontrolled livestock movements,



ANNEX 5
Page 5
unwillingness on the part of the owners to report outbreaks, and inadequate
funding of the veterinary services. Cattle diseases of major commercial
importance are described below (see also the following table):
No. of Animal Disease Outbreaks in 1974
Upper Region        Total Ghana
Rinderpest                                  -                     3
CBPP                                        21                   57
Anthrax                                       1                   9
Blackquarter                                  1                   8
Trypanosomiasis                               7                  16
Tuberculosis                                  1                   1
Newcastle Disease                          N.A.                  41
Rabies                                      -                    40
FMD                                         37                  143
21.       The most important epizootic cattle diseases are rinderpest and
contagious bovine pleuropneumonia (CBPP). Rinderpest is now almost under
control but CBPP threatens cattle in many parts of the country in spite
of vaccination cover of one third of the herd. In order to improve the
health situation, it is necessary to increase the annual coverage of
CBPP vaccinations to 80% of the cattle, and to continue vaccinating all
calves once against rinderpest. Anthrax, blackquarter and haemorrhagic
septicaemia occur sporadically, but can be brought under control through
vaccination of all cattle in affected areas before the rains.
22.       Trypnosomiasis, a disease transmitted by the tsetse fly, is
widely spread, and the main cause for the absence of cattle in the forest
and forest savannah zones. It also occurs adjacent to rivers in the open
savannahs of northern and southeastern Ghana. As local cattle possess
a certain degree of tolerance to the disease, acute outbreaks are rare.
Provided that open areas are selected for beef ranching, and that adequate
tsetse clearing methods, or, if necessary, preventive/curative treatments
are used, trypanososiamis should not be an impediment to developing a
cattle industry with imported Zebu cattle, which are more productive than
the local W.A. Shorthorn (para 14).
23.       Foot and Mouth Disease (FMD) is widespread, occuring in a number
of different strains. Beef output from indigeneous cattle is but little
reduced by disease outbreaks. Apart from quarantining cattle from affected
villages and controlling the health of market livestock, no further measures
are required at this development stage of the livestock industry.
24.       Streptothrichosis, a skin disease, is rampant, and most frequent-
ly fatal to all imported temperate-zone breeds under ranch conditions.
Their crosses with the Zebu, such as the Santa Gertrudas, are also suscepti-
ble. Sahelian Zebu catle are susceptible, but to a much lesser degree



ANNEX 5
Page 6
than exotic cattle. The indigeneous humpless cattle display the greatest
degree of resistance.
25.       Other important diseases are rabies and tuberculosis.  Contagious
abortion appears to be widespread, surveys having shown a positive reaction
of 23% among cattle in the Accra Plains and similar proportions in other
parts of Ghana and in neighboring countries (southern Upper Volta and Ivory
Coast.) Although no investigations have been made in the Upper Region, the
animal health division has been engaged for the past three years in a country-
wide vaccination campaign which, for lack of sufficient funds, covers only
part of the existing cow population.
26.       Worm infestations and starvation are the most important constraints
to increased production, causing high losses and low weight gains, especially
in calves, sheep and goats. Treatment with anthelmintics gives a good measure
of control with a substantial reduction in mortality. At the same time, more
hygienic livestock management, such as improved kraaling and stock watering,
could greatly reduce the hazard of internal parasites. Ticks and tickborne
diseases (redwater, anaplasmosis, heartwater) are relatively unimportant and
can be controlled by regular spraying or dipping.
D. Services
27.       Veterinary Service.  Animal health services are supplied free of
charge and are carried out by the Veterinary Services Division of the Ministry
of Agriculture. Its activities consist of:
-    vaccination for the control of epidemic and endemic
diseases;
-    control of internal and external parasites through
deworming and dipping;
-    control of imported livestock through quarantine
stations;
-    meat inspection in the urban centers;
-    some poultry extension;
-    laboratory testing; and
-    running a veterinary training school at Pong
Tamale.
28.       Animal health services to the Upper Region are subdivided into three
sections. Each section serves one to three districts, which in turn are
subdivided into two to six stations (details in Table 5). The service is



ANNEX 5
Page 7
generously staffed and well managed, but inadequate funds for the renewal
of equipment and the purchase of vaccines and drugs restrict its effective-
ness. Diagnostic services are carried out by the laboratory at Pong
Tamale, but are inadequate and need to be improved. The Ghanaian Govern-
ment has approached Canada for technical assistance in this field. Vac-
cination and deworming campaigns of the Division are shown in the following
table:
