29.       Animal Husbandry Service.  The Animal Husbandry Division is
responsible for extension, breeding work and the distribution of improved
stock. Until 1971 the Division promoted poultry very successfully,
operating poultry production and processing centers, including hatcheries



ANNEX 5
Page 8
and feed mills. In that year it handed over its operations to Pomadze
Enterprises Ltd., and since has concentrated on ruminants, mainly cattle,
which are bred on one dairy station and eight beef cattle stations with
about 3,000 animals altogether.
30.       In the Upper Region, animal husbandry services have so far
been restricted to administrative tasks and to operating the five animal
hubandry centers at Doba (pigs and poultry), Babile and Bazua (cattle),
and Busa and Wa (sheep, goats). Due to shortages of staff and funds
(see also Table 16), livestock producers have so far had little benefit
from extension activities, and a strengthening of the service is badly
required. Improved breeding stock is being distributed to farmers from
the animal husbandry stations at subsidized prices: for example, N'Dama
bulls from Wa are sold at 070 and Sanga bulls at 090, equivalent to about
one quarter of their market value. Furthermore, weaner pigs and poultry
are being sold to farmers at subsidized prices.
31.       Research.  The livestock research effort is shared between the
Animal Husbandry Division of the Ministry of Agriculture, the Animal Research
Institute of the Council for Scientific and Industrial Research (CSIR), and
the universities. Work is concentrated in the southern part of the country
centering around pasture improvement, poultry, dairy and beef production.
Pasture, beef, sheep and goat production research for the Northern and
Upper Regions is conducted at Pong Tamale, but this suffers from staffing
problems and financial constraints. UNDP has been approached to give sup-
port to a sheep and goat development project which would operate from a
station in the Ashanti Region and also assist in extension and investiga-
tional work all over the country.
E. Demand and Supply
32.       Demand.  Annual consumption of animal products is estimated to be
about 2,500 tons carcass meat from cattle, sheep and goats, 8,000 tons
poultry meat, 7,000 tons eggs, about 20,000 tons of locally produced milk
from cows and goats, and about 05 million worth of imported dairy products.
At a population of 9.5 million, annual per capita consumption is about 7.6 lb
of carcass and poultry meat, 0.7 kg eggs and 7 kilos fresh milk equivalent.
33.       Price controls and exchange restrictions have led to a de facto
limitation on meat and milk consumption through shortages. There is strong
evidence that more meat would be consumed if it were available. By 1980,
the population would have reached about 11 million, and about 39,000 tons
of carcass and poultry meat, over 8,000 tons eggs, and 78,000 tons eggs,
and 78,000 tons of milk equivalent would be required to maintain present
per capita supply levels.



ANNEX 5
Page 9
34.       Supply.  The bulk of domestic supplies of slaugherstock are con-
sumed in rural areas and towns in northern and central Ghana. The larger
cities in southern Ghana are mainly supplied by slaughterstock imported
on the hoof from neighboring countries to the north (Upper Volta, Niger,
Mali), as indicated in the following table:
Year          Cattle         Sheep         Goats
1969         69,380        17,308        30,313
1970         60,001         9,714        27,889
1971         57,657         9,748        28,123
1972         37,022         4,836         7,560
1973         47,422        11,626        20,369
1974         50,975         9,996        19,451
During 1975, the supply of slaugherstock from Sahelian countries virtually
dried up because the price offered by the Cattle Development Board (CADB) --
40.30 per lb liveweight -- is not competitive with prices offered by other
importing countries (Ivory Coast, Nigeria). CADB is currently importing
low quality meat products from the world market in order to satisfy local
demand. Due to foreign exchange difficulties, the two livestock importing
organizations, CADB and GIHOC, have specific allocations for slaughterstock
imports. In view of the deteriorating supply situation from Sahelian
countries, Ghana will have to import increasing quantities of meat from the
world market if it allows internal demand to be met.
35.       Market demand for milk and dairy products is almost exclusively
met from imports.  Locally produced milk is absorbed by the subsistence
sector. Considering that marketing of milk from indigeneous cattle and
smallstock is not a worthwhile proposition and that a commercial diary
sector is nonexistent, increasing quantities will have to be imported to
meet internal demand.
36.       The commercial poultry sector has increasingly filled the supply
gap of meat. However, partly based on imported feeds and on milling by-
products, this industry is highly dependent on world market grain prices.
F. Marketing and Pricing
37.       Since 1971, foreign slaughterstock suppliers have been required to
channel their herds through five border control posts where they undergo
a nine-day quarantine and compulsory vaccination against rinderpest. In
principle, CADB has the sole right to purchase imported livestock through
the Paga and Mogonori quanrantine stations but recently GIHOC has obtained
special permits to import cattle and meat independently.



ANNEX 5
Page 10
38.       Livestock are weighed when leaving the quarantine stations,
marked with their weight, and transported to various regions by truck.
Most go to Kumasi, Accra, the Bolgatanga meat factory (operated by
GIHOC) and Sekondi/Takoradi. Since demand exceeds supply, the cattle
are allocated to butchers by a "reception committee", whose head, at the
main market, is an army officer. Local slaughterstock are freely traded.
39.       The main final buyers of slaughterstock are butchers who, to-
gether with the livestock dealersk are organized into a national associa-
tion. Slaughter is generally carried out in abattoirs which are owned and
operated by the city councils. In Accra, however, the city council pro-
vides the necessary facilities and also transports the animals and carcasses
at a charge, but slaughtering and dressing are done by personnel employed by
the butchers. Meat retailing is done through supermarkets or through
butchers' shops.
40.       The Meat Products Division of GIHOC is the largest meat processor
in the country. It operates the Bolgatanga Meatworks (a slaughterhouse
and canning factory), the Tema slaughterhouse, a storage depot at Kumasi, and
three meat cottages (meat retail shops) in the Accra-Tema area. Meat is
retailed mostly as "bone-in". Hides are sold to the tannery in Kumasi.
41.       The Bolgatanga Meatworks, a well-equipped modern factory, is not
efficiently used because of shortages of slaughterstock; with a capacity to
slaughter about 25,000 head of cattle per year, the factory has been utilized
only up to 25% since operations began in early 1966. GIHOC has seven refri-
gerated vans to transport carcasses and meat products from Bolgatanga to
the south; these take four days for a return trip to Accra. GIHOC's Meat
Products Division is operating at a deficit. GIHOC has plans to develop a
large pig operation of 1,000 pigs at Bolgatanga, which would produce an
additional 1,700 or so pigs per year for slaughter. The question remains
whether it is possible to produce pork profitably.
42.       Import Prices.  While domestic slaughterstock is freely traded,
CADB is obliged to offer ¢0.30 per lb liveweight for imported cattle, sheep
and goats upon leaving the quarantine stations. Imported stock is sold by
the Board to butchers at a nation-wide standard price of 00.40/lb liveweight,
based on weight marked on the animal at the frontier. Recently the Board has
found it difficult to operate on a spread of t0.10/lb liveweight, including
the payment of 2.5% import tax, of kraal fees for the district councils
(cattle 40.60 - ¢1.00, sheep and goats 00.30 per head) and the transport
cost of slaughterstock to any destination in Ghana.
43.       In 1974, CADB was able to pay competitive prices to attract slaugh-
terstock from Sahelian countries. At an exchange rate of ¢1 = US$0.8667 =
CFAF 208 the border price of ¢0.30/lb liveweight was equivalent to CFAF
137/kg liveweight or about 10% above going rates. Because of the declining
value of the US$ the exchange rate temporarily declined to ¢1 = CFAF 173 in
1975, and the supplier in Upper Volta would only receive CFAF 114/kg



ANNEX 5
Page 11
liveweight or about 10% less than going rates: this explains the failure of
CADB to buy any cattle from neighboring countries in May and June, 1975.
World market beef prices are presently competitive with those required to attract
Sahelian cattle. Thus it is expected that in the long-term, Ghanaian cattle
prices will follow world market levels as the cheapest alternative source
of supply.
44.       Meat Prices.  Although there are price controls for meat, domestic
slaughterstock prices have increased rapidly in recent years. At the time
of appraisal in 1975, prices in the Upper Region had moved to over 00.40/lb
liveweight for local cattle and to about %0.50 for sheep and goats.
45.       The Prices and Incomes Board controls the prices of a considerable
range of commodities, including meat. Current price ceilings set for beef,
mutton and goat are ¢0.90/lb for bone-out and ¢0.85 for bone-in meat. Pig
and poultry prices are not controlled. At the going market rates for
slaughterstock, butchers would not have adequate profit margins; however, meat
is sold in heaps which are not normally weighed. The size and composition of
the heaps containing meat, offals and bones are variable, and this leaves the
butcher enough leeway to disguise the actual meat price charged. There
appears to be no advantage in fixing meat retail prices because the
only effect, if any, would be to reduce the incentives for livestock producers
to invest in the industry and would, therefore, perpetuate the reliance on live-
stock and meat imports.
G. Government Strategy
46.       One of Government's constant aims has been, and still is,
improving nutritional standards in general and protein supply in
particular. In line with this objective, Government has implemented suc-
cessfully a number of poultry and fish production programs over the last
ten years and has paid particular attention to the control of animal disease.
Ghana has thus become self-sufficient in poultry meat and eggs; most epidemic
diseases are presently under control. However, a rapidly increasing popula-
tion, declining slaughterstock supplies from Sahelian countries, uncertain-
ties in the availability and prices of feeds, and a shortage of foreign
exchange, have shifted Government's priorities towards the development and
exploitation of the national pasture resource.
47.       Beef Production has first priority because the main inputs re-
quired -- pasture and, to a certain extent, cattle -- are available. Govern-
ment encourages the development of commercial ranching by parastatal organi-
zations in order to create management know-how and nucleus herds of improved
livestock. Private cattle producers are encouraged to apply for ranch
development loans in order to speed up the growth of the national herd and



ANNEX 5
Page 12
to increase beef output. Government is determined to increase the output
from the subsistence sector through better animal health services, improved
watering facilities and an intensified extension effort to introduce better
production techniques. Government also encourages the fattening of under-
finished slaughter cattle originating from the Sahel. In the Upper Region,
the strategy is to develop cattle ranching where free grassland is presently
underutilized.
48.       Second priority of Government's livestock strategy is given to the
1.7 million sheep and goats in the country; however, this development task is
more difficult than for cattle, because flocks are widely scattered and almost
exclusively kept in the traditional sector. Herd growth and productivity will
be improved by the vaccination and parasite control program of the Veterinary
Services Division. The Animal Husbandry Divsion, assisted by the UNDP sheep
and goats project, is expected to develop practical smallstock production sys-
tems on its stations and demonstrate them to farmers.
49.       The poultry and pig industries have third priority in Government
planning. Poultry is already well-developed, utilizing international tech-
nology. This industry is risky because it relies partly on feed imports,
the prices of which have fluctuated considerably in recent years.
50.       Considerable emphasis is laid on the development of dairying, in
order to meet growing demand and to substitute imports. Current milk producer
prices (about 01.20/gallon) are very favorable, but they preclude milk be-
coming accessible to lower income classes. Milk production with imported
European or Canadian dairy cattle has not yet achieved anticipated yield
levels, and it is questionable whether this high-cost/high-yield production
system is viable under Ghanaian conditions. Crossbreds of European dairy
cattle with indigenous breeds or improved diary Zebus (e.g. Sahiwal) would
probably be more appropriate because they require lower standards of husbandry
and feeding, but they would also produce lower yields. Further investiga-
tional work is required in the dairy sector before extensive investment
programs could be envisaged.
51.       The proposed livestock component for the Upper Region Agricultural
Development Project agrees with Government's livestock development strategy;
its successful implementation would assist in further formulating Government
policy for the subsector and could lead to similar developments in other
regions of Ghana.



ANNEX 5
Page 13
III. The Livestock Development Component in the Upper Region
Agricultural Development Project
A. General
52.       The livestock development component would be the first phase of
a long-range program to increase livestock production in the Upper Region
through animal health measures, development of private ranches, and strength-
ening the animal husbandry service. It would comprise the following subcom-
ponents:
(a) Animal Health: Strengthening of the Upper Region's Veterinary
Services Division, enabling it to carry out efficient vaccination
campaigns against the important endemic diseases and provide regular
treatment of calves, sheep and goats against internal and external
parasites.
(b) Tumu Ranching Scheme: Credit and technical assistance would be
given to cattle producers to develop ten breeding/fattening ranches
on unoccupied grazing land in the Tumu District.
(c) Animal Husbandry: Strengthening of the Upper Region's Animal
Husbandry Division to enable it to carry out investigations on
farm-level pasture and fodder production, small scale beef fatten-
ing, and small scale sheep and goat production; and furthermore,
to intensify extension work to livestock producers.
B. Animal Health Services
53.       Under this subcomponent, provision would be made to undertake the
following treatments for livestock in the Project area:
(a) annual vaccination of all cattle under one year against rinder-
pest;
(b) annual vaccination of all cattle against contagious bovine
pleuropneumonia (CBPP);
(c) vaccination against anthrax and blackquarter of all cattle in
areas where outbreaks occur;
(d) following a survey, annual vaccination of all females under one
year against contagious abortion;



ANNEX 5
Page 14
(e) upon request, vaccination against other livestock diseases
(e.g., lamb dysentery, rabies, Newcastle disease);
(f) regular treatment of calves, sheep and goats against internal
parasites;
(g) upon request, dipping/spraying of livestock for tick control;
and
(h) upon request, prophylactic/curative treatment of livestock against
typanosomiasis.
54.       The veterinary service in the Upper Region is adequately staffed
to operate the improved animal health services envisaged under the Project.
In fact, it would be able to make available some of its junior staff for
other Project subcomponents, such as the Tumu Ranching Scheme. However,
the existing vehicles and veterinary equipment, which were purchased during
the JP 15 vaccination campaign in 1965-68, need replacing.
55.        Under the Project, twelve existing veterinary stations would be
rehabilited, and five new veterinary service centers and one district head-
quarters at Sandema would be constructed, the headquarters to include office
space, cattle handling and spraying facilities, and housing for officers.
Vehicles and equipment required for the efficient operation of the service
would also be purchased.
56.       Vaccinations.  The veterinary field staff would be reorganized
and trained up to a level necessary to carry out the vaccination campaign
as detailed above. All the proposed routine vaccinations would be carried
out at the same time. Thus it would be possible to handle cattle for this
purpose only once a year. Each vaccination team would consist of two to
three vaccinators and three assistants. On average, each team would cover
an area with a cattle population of about 25,000 head, of which at least
80% should be reached by the service.  This would leave sufficient staff
time for deworming programs, spraying or dipping for tick control, and
for the treatment of sick animals.
57.       Deworming and Dipping.  Currently the veterinary service deworms
and dips about 50,000 cattle, sheep and goats annually.  Under the Project,
the deworming campaign would be intensified to cover about 190,000 head/
year at full development. Watering facilities would be provided at each
rehabilitated and new veterinary service center as well as at existing dips,
in order to enable them to render efficient tick control services upon re-
quest. Government intends to continue compulsory vaccinations free of
charge but plans to start asking payments for treatments. The Project
would assist this policy by providing funds for the deworming and dipping
of about 60,000 cattle, 75,000 sheep and 75,000 goats free of charge for
a two-year period. It would provide a revolving fund for drugs to be
sold from the third year on. The intensified animal health program



ANNEX 5
Page 15
would be introduced in three phases, commencing in each one of the three
veterinary sections in consecutive years.
58.       Diagnostic Services.  Government would improve its diagnostic
services at Pong Tamale, possibly with Canadian technical assistance. A
qualified and experienced bacteriologist would be employed to operate the
diagnostic laboratory.
C. Tumu Ranching Scheme
59.       The area selected for ranch development under the project lies
in the western part of Tumu District, about 120 miles west of Bolgatanga
and is bounded by the Gwolu-Zini-Han road to the northwest, the Gwolu-Bullu-
Jeffisi Road to the east, the Jeffisi-Han road to the south and the Dazam
River and Tumu District boundary to the southwest (see map). It is part
of a vast area -- bounded by the Kulpawn and Sisili rivers to the west
and east and by the White Volta to the south -- that is largely flat but
with major depressions along these rivers. The slight undulations of the
terrain form water sheds at its center, separating the catchment areas of
the rivers.   Rainfall averages about 1,100 mm per year, extending over
a six-month rainy season from May to October.
60.       The area has about 40,000 inhabitants (20/sq mile), distributed
over some 120 villages. Away from the main communication routes, the
Region has remained underpopulated and underdeveloped for historical and
health (onchocerciasis) 1/ reasons. There has been more emigration than
immigration, and its population has slowly decreased.  Subsistence agricul-
ture and animal husbandry are the basic economic activities. The road
system of about 160 km of feeder-roads is presently in poor condition but
parts are being repaired by the regional administration; the Region will
then be accessible from the east via Tumu and from the west via Fian-Wa.
61.       In the absence of precise statistics, the area livestock popula-
tion -- belonging to the settled farmers -- is estimated at about 15,000
cattle and 25,000 sheep and goats. Some 4,000 cattle from the Bussie and
Fian areas use the bottomlands during the dry season. There is some wild-
life along the Kulpawn valley, and Government intends to create a game
reserve in an area of about 4,000 sq miles.
62.       Thick soil and lateritic overburden conceal the underlying
geological formation. Rock outcrops are mainly of tor-forming granite.
The formation is not favorable for obtaining water from boreholes. A
number of streams provide natural drainage but are dry most of the year.
As most of the rain is provided by storms, ranch water supplies would
1/   Eradication to begin 1976.



ANNEX 5
Page 16
depend on run-off water collected in valley tanks or dugouts at the foot
of the most favorable catchment areas.
63.       Most of the soils are generally suitable for grassland but in-
adequate for crop production. The ten ranches under the Project would be
established in a suitable, easily accessible but sparsely populated
area of about 50,000 acres along the Dazam river, about 30 miles west of
Tumu. Should they prove successful, a large number of additional ranches
of this kind (about 200) could be established.
64.       The natural vegetation is a fire-climax tree savannah composed
of leafed deciduous trees and perennial and annual grasses. The commonest
tree species are Combretum, Butyrospermum, Parkia, Daniella and Acacia sp.
with a variable density up to 100 trees/ha in places.  Due to regular
burning, the grass/herb spectrum is narrow. The dominant grasses are
Andropogon gayanus and pseudapricus, Pennisetum polystachium, Hyparrhenia sp.,
Loudetia sp, and, alongside the main valleys, Hyparrhenia rufa, Vetiveria and
Echniochloa. The main drawback is the uneven protein and fiber content of
the herbage, in particular its low palatability during the dry season.
At least ten acres are required per beast, in order to permit selective
grazing; through controlled burning, selective bush clearing, deferred
grazing and the establishment of 125 acres grass/legume pastures for each
5,000 acre (2,000 ha) ranch, the grazing requirements per head could be
reduced to eight acres. Weight losses during the dry season would be reduced,
thus increasing overall output.
65.       The ranching area selected has a low population density of about
30 per square mile; about 3% of the land is utilized for shifting cultivation.
These people, represented by their chiefs, customarly hold the right to occupy
the land 1/. When interviewed, farmers in the area favored the development
of modern cattle ranching. Under the Administration of Lands Act 1962, Go-
vernment would declare the ranching area as "grazing land", register it, and
transfer the lend to the ranchers under a long term lease contract accompanied
by a statement of conditions. Payment of compensation to present land users
would be kept to a minimum by selecting ranch sites which are not presently
used for agriculture. The few existing fields within planned ranch boundaries
could be fenced off and continue to be used for cropping until they have to
be abandoned because of declining soil fertility.
66.       Extensive hearings would be held between Government, the Tumu Tra-
ditional Council 2/, the chiefs and headmen of villages adjacent to the ranch-
ing scheme and local cattle producers on the nature and operations. Further-
more, the area would have to be surveyed before Government could declare the
1/   See State Lands-Executive Instrument No. 87 of 11/7/63, Subsection 1 of
Section 7 of Administration of Lands Act 123 - State Printing Press.
2/   Set up by Parliament Act No. 370, 1971.



ANNEX 5
Page 17
area as "grazing land". Disbursements of loan funds by ADB would be be
contingent upon Government's publication of a decree transferring the land
to the ranching scheme.
67.       Company Ranches.  Each ranch would have an area of about 5,000
acres, which would provide initial grazing for 500 and, after development,
600 head of cattle. This herd size would provide an income sufficiently
attractive for a progressive group of livestock producers while at the same
time not becoming too large for the available management staff. Each ranch
would be selected with suitable catchment areas and valleys in order to
channel water runoff into dams or dugouts to be constructed.
68.       Minimum physical developments necessary would be provided, in
order to permit the ranching company to control the enterprise and make
satisfactory progress. This would consist of:
-    Land.  Full control of land and the exclusive right of
occupancy through a long-term lease obtained from
Government, after basic ranch development had been
completed.
-    Access roads, water reservoirs (dugout or dam with
100,000 m3 capacity), firebreaks and fencelines (four
paddocks) would be developed with ranch loan funds.
-    Improved grass/legume pastures would be developed
with the help of contractors on a small proportion
of each ranch (about 125 acres) in order to improve
dry season feed supplies.
-    Cattle purchase loans would be given to buy improved
bulls (Zebu, Sanga or N'Dama) from Government stations
and feeder steers for fattening, from Upper Volta.
-    Technical assistance would be provided through an
experienced ranch development officer, who would
plan and organize basic ranch development and would
guide ranchers from the beginning in solving man-
agerial problems.
69.       Livestock producers eligible for the Tumu ranching scheme should
be living in the Project area. A Ranch Selection Board, consisting of the
District Officer, the Section Chiefs, the Village Headmen, representatives
of the Veterinary and Animal Husbandry Divisions and ADB, and the Ranch
Development Officer, would be appointed by URADPEC to select applicants
with business acumen, integrity, experience and general capacity. Ghana
company law permits up to fifty shareholders in a company. The expected
average number of shareholders is fifteen per company, who will each con-
tribute about twenty of their cattle.



ANNEX 5
Page 18
70.       Leases.  Ranch leases to be approved by URADPEC would initially
be given for forty-five years. The leases would contain 'good husbandry'
clauses, requiring the entry of at least 200 but not more than 400 animals
into the ranch, separating the herd into sex groups and ages, constructing
certain minimal improvements, practicing regular disease and parasite con-
trol, and following appropriate pasture management patterns. If defaulted,
these clauses could allow for withdrawal of title. Each ranching company
would be a single legal entity which would not be allowed to subdivide the
holding among shareholders, but would have to operate a single unit of which
the company would be the sole owner of the cattle and other assets, while
the shareholders would have a claim only to equity.
71.       Land Rent. Instead of the present cattle tax, which is collected
by the District Council 1/, ranching companies would pay a land rent, the
amount of which would be established by URADPEC, in order to recover the
development and operating costs incurred by Government (initial surveying,
costs of the Ranch Development Officer and Accountant, etc.).
72.       Ranch Loans.  Each ranching company would be eligible for two
types of loans offered by ADB:
(a) Ranch Development Loans: for a 13-year term with five
years grace, at an interest rate of 12%. This loan
would be required to pay for ranch development carried
out by Government before ranch allocation and for fixed
investments carried out in Years 1 to 4.
(b) Cattle Purchase Loans: would be available for a 20-month
term, at an interest rate of 12%; they would finance 80%
of cattle purchase (para 69). The Ranch Development
Officer would assist the ranching companies in registra-
tion procedures and in the preparation of ranch develop-
ment plans and budgets, upon which an application would
be forwarded to ADB for a ranch development loan. Cattle
purchase loan applications would be prepared annually by
the Ranch Development Officer and approved by ADB upon
receiving satisfactory evidence that the required 20%
contribution will be produced by the shareholders or
the ranching company management.
73.       Management.  There are no qualified ranch managers available
in Ghana suitable for the Tumu Ranching Scheme. Overseas training of
ranch managers would delay the scheme, make trainees familiar with in-
appropriate technology, and generate income expectations far in excess
of what the ranching companies will be able to pay. Ranching companies
would, therefore, employ Ghanaian staff who are used to working with
1/   ¢1.50 per head of cattle over two years, collected from about two
thirds of existing herd.



ANNEX 5
Page 19
animals, show initiative and, due to lack of academic qualifications, have
no prospects for promotion within the Government service (e.g., Senior
Veterinary Guards). These foremen would be selected in cooperation with
the Ranch Development Officer and the respective ranching company.
Initially, they would run the day-to-day affairs of the ranch under close
supervision of the Ranch Development Officer. Contingent upon satisfactory
performance on the ranch, the foreman would receive instruction in record
keeping and accounting, to enable him gradually to become a fully
competent ranch manager.
74.       The executives of the proposed ranching companies would require
special training to enable them to cope with their new tasks. Upon allocation
of a ranch to a certain company, its directors should participate in manage-
ment seminars to be held at the staff training center at Navrongo, to teach
them functional literacy and the basic principles of the operation of a
company.
75.       The Ranch Development Officer, who would be stationed at Tumu
as part of the Animal Husbandry Division's staff, would stake out the ten
ranches following the hearings to be held (para 67) and Government's decree
to declare the area a "grazing area". Basic ranch infrastructure (para 69)
would be developed under his planning and supervision, in consultation with
the Ranch Selection Board and the Project Manager. After selection of a
participating company, he would assist in preparing ranch development plans,
budgets, loan applications, the running of ranches and the training of ranch
staff and shareholders. He would also supervise the Ranch Accountant to be
placed at Tumu and would assist any other potential investor in the Upper
Region in drawing up ranch development plans. He would be understudied by the
District Animal Husbandry Officer, to be posted to Tumu, who would assume full
responsibility as Ranch Development Officer from Year Six. It is anticipated
that the development of five ranches would commence in Year One and the re-
maining five in Year Two.
D. Animal Husbandry Services
76.       The Animal Husbandry Division would be strengthened to enable it to
carry out a meaningful investigational program and to render effective exten-
sion service to farmers. This would consist of:
(a) development of improved pastures and fodder crops
within a crop rotation system, to be utilized by
cattle, sheep and goats;
(b) investigations on the use of crop residues and by-
products as animal feeds on smallholdings;



ANNEX 5
Page 20
(c) investigations on the feasibility of smallholder stall
feeding of cattle, sheep and goats;
(d) development of feed rations for work oxen based
on local feeds;
(e) development of optimum sheep and goat management
systems at the village level;
(f) distribution of improved breeding stock from Government
stations to farmers;
(g) demonstration and advice for all livestock producers in
the Region.
77.       Staffing.  Animal husbandry staff would be substantially increased
to 28 from the present number of about 15. The five main livestock districts
would have an Animal Husbandry Officer, the remaining two a Senior Technical
Officer; the five Animal Husbandry Service Centers (Babile and Bazua for
cattle, Busa and Wa for sheep and goats, and Doba for pigs and poultry) would
be headed by Technical or Senior Technical Officers. The Ranch Development
Officer would be stationed at Tumu but would be responsible for all advisory
work on ranch development in the Upper Region. He would be assisted by an
accountant for the Tumu Ranching Scheme.
78.       Adequate officer housing and office and transport facilities would
be provided, as well as finance to buy improved breeding stock and to carry
out the investigational work.
79.       Overall responsibility for planning and supervision of the applied
investigational work would rest with the Farm Management Section of the Agri-
cultural-Technical Services Department in close liaison with the Regional
Animal Husbandry Division, the Animal Research Institute of the CSIR, and
the expected UNDP/FAO sheep and goat development project.
80.       The extension effort would be based on a clear assessment of the
farm-level situation. It would be one of the first tasks of the Animal
Husbandry Service to collect information on herd productivity and its
principal constraints. This information would be analyzed in cooperation
with the Farm Management Section of the Agricultural Technical Services
Department and form the basis for the extension approach and for further
investigations.
E. Costs and Benefits
81.       Ranching.  Development costs of the- ten ranches are estimated
to be 41.0 million, to which nonquantifiable general service costs would



ANNEX 5
Page- 21
have to be added. The financial rate of return of company ranches is
calculated at 19% without the general service cost. The ranching scheme
would not reach its full production until Year 14, when the cattle herd
would have doubled from 3,000 before development to 6,000. Incremental
annual output would be 720 slaughter cattle or 135 tons carcass beef, worth
0350,000. Incremental incomes per participating family would reach 01,000.
82.       Veterinary and Animal Husbandry.  The incremental benefits deriving
from the veterinary and animal husbandry subcomponents, as given in Tables 1
to 3, are partly dependent on the availability of additional crop residues
and better watering facilities which are costed under other components of the
Project. Over a twelve-year development period, the cattle herd of the
Region would increase by 25%, and annual herd offtake would increase from
11.7% to 15.7%.  Offtake from the sheep and goat flocks would increase from
30% and 32%, to 35% and 39%, respectively. At an investment cost over five
years of M2.4 million, incremental output in Year 5 would be 6,670 slaughter
cattle, 16,000 sheep and 20,000 goats with a carcass equivalent of about 1,100
tons and an incremental value of 02.9 million. At full production in Year 12,
incremental output would be 25,000 slaughter cattle, 16,000 sheep and 20,000
goats, with a carcass equivalent of about 3,100 tons and worth 08.2 million.
Incremental annual incomes of about 45,000 cattle producers would be about
t140, of the 55,000 sheep holders about 012, and of the 800,000 goat holders
about 09 per holding.
83.      Overall, the livestock component would substitute imports of 3,300
tons carcass meat from Project Year 14 at a value of 08.6 million. In addition,
the livestock component would have important nonquantifiable benefits, including:
(a) demonstration of the beneficial effects of improved
land use, pasture and herd management methods;
(b) development of an integrated crop/livestock production
system for smallholders which can form the basis for
future development projects;
(c) training of Ghanaians in animal husbandry and ranch
management;
(d) introduction of land use rights opening the way for
sustained radical improvement of grazing land;
(e) introduction of credit to traditional livestock pro-
ducers; and finally
(f) improvement of the quality of animals and meat produced.

farmers would pay for the drug thereafter; only 80% of the farmers each assuned to continue asking for treatment.

ANNEX 6
Page 1
GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Commercial Services
General
1.        The Ministry of Agriculture is presently responsible for the supply
of farm inputs which until recently have been available in the Upper Region
on a scattered and sporadic basis. As a result of the Ghanaian/German Agri-
cultural Project, distribution has improved, but regional utilization is
still small (3,500 tons of fertilizer in 1974) and is consumed mainly by
larger farmers. Low consumption of inputs is at least as much due to non-
availability as to ignorance or antipathy on the part of farmers; the estab-
lishment, development and maintenance of a dynamic input supply organiza-
tion would therefore be critical to the success of the Project.
2.        Dynamism is essential if continued development is to be assured;
it can be achieved most effectively through a commercial company dedicated
solely to the provision of technical supplies and services necessary to
complement the extension effort. Expert and experienced direction and
management would be imperative from the outset, with some form of active
farmer participation also desirable in order to provide a constant spur
to management and clientele. Ideally, this should be attained through
both equity participation and Board representation.
3.        A suitable organization would therefore be set up under the
Project that would aim at providing physical inputs as well as commercial
atmosphere and opportunities, all of which are essential for the adoption
and maintenance of improved production practices. Specifically, the
company would:
-    establish contact with farmers at all levels through
a chain of retail outlets;
-    procure, distribute and sell recommended farm inputs;
-    provide agricultural services appropriate to the needs
of individual farming areas;
-    ensure farmers' adequate purchasing power by operating
a deferred payment (credit) scheme;
-    provide incentives for transportation and farmers
association development through a bulk discount system;



ANNEX 6
Page 2
provide facilities for produce marketing; and
operate agency services for statutory organizations
such as Cotton Development Board, Agricultural
Development Bank, and the Food Distribution Corpora-
tion.
4.        Establishment and Organization.  A company to be called the Farmers
Services Company (Upper Region) Ltd. (FSC) would be established in Bolgatanga
under the 1963 Companies Code Act, with an initial authorized capital of
W12.0 million; draft regulations and organization chart are at Appendix 1 and
2 of this annex. The initial shareholders would include Ministry of Finance
(representing Ghana Government), and Ghanaian banking institutions. The
latter would subscribe about t4.0 million. The final equity composition
and voting power would be established at the time of Company incorporation,
but in any event, Government would initially be the majority shareholder.
5.        Farmer equity would be developed through a mandatory 2-1/2% mark up
on the landed CIF store cost of farm inputs (base cost); these shares would
be held in trust for the farmers by the Bank of Ghana. Since all recipients
of inputs can be identified, eventually dividends could be made payable to
individuals or groups of farmers. In any event, voting rights of farmer
representatives on the FSC Board would increase annually as their equity
expanded. Eventually, farmers' equity could be held in blocks representing
district or even farmers associations. It is expected that by Year 5 farmers'
equity would have reached 12% of issued capital. It is intended that control
of the Company would one day fall into the hands of the farmers. Mechanisms
would be worked out so that shareholdings from outside the Upper Region would
eventually be replaced by farmers' capital.
6.        The Board of Directors would comprise:
-    Project Manager of URPMU (Chairman);
-    One representative each from:
Bank of Ghana,
National Investment Bank,
Commercial banks (three);
-    Two representatives from the Ministry of Finance;



ANNEX 6
Page 3
-    three farmers, appointed initially by the Project Manager
but eventually to be elected by shareholding farmers
association members;
-    Managing Director (FSC);
-    Chief Accountant (FSC) (Secretary).
7.        Company organization is shown at Appendix 2.  Prime activity would
be generated by Commercial Services and Agricultural Services, the former
handling all procurement, distribution and sales activities, and the latter
all agro-contracting operations. These would be supported by Administration
and Finance, there being a separate Internal Audit section reporting directly
to the Board.
8.        Staff Recruitment and Training.  The exacting task of quickly
establishing a sound company capable of profitably anticipating and meeting
the needs of the agricultural community requires overall management expertise
together with operational and financial discipline of a very high order.
Two key executives, the Managing Director and the Chief Accountant, would be
recruited internationally. Projected activity build-up rate is high, which
should facilitate the training and selection of a Ghanaian successor to
the Managing Director by the end of Year 4. Initially the Managing Director
would also carry out the duties of Commercial Manager, with the intention
of filling this position (probably from one of the four 1/ key section
managers in the Division) by the end of Year 2. The person so selected
could be suitable for subsequent promotion to Managing Director.
9.        Continued Company success depends on the availability of well
trained and oriented staff; a properly designed training program would
therefore be implemented from the outset, probably in cooperation with
the commercial banks. Experience has shown that training and internal
auditing are naturally complementary, particularly as good audit work is
concerned more with preventing mistakes or defalcations before they occur
rather than in detecting them subsequently. Consequently, these two
functions would be combined under the Auditor. Basic staff training
would be run on a sandwich basis, formal classroom work being carried out
in the slacker season, field training being continued through mobile
audit teams.
10.       Financing. The Company would receive commitments from Ministry of
Finance and the local banking institutions for equity valued at 05.1 million.
This equity in cash or kind would be paid in over the first five-year period.
Ministry of Finance equity would cover the cost of vehicles and equipment;
1/   Field Operations, Procurement, Transport and Accounts.



ANNEX 6
Page 4
banks, Bank of Ghana, and the National Investment Bank would provide equity
for working capit;il and for the construction of buildings. Government,
through the Ministry of Finance, would take up additional equity in exchange
for land, buildings and machinery taken over by FSC.
11.       Funds to cover the incremental foreign exchange cost of farm in-
puts would be provided in the form of a long term Government loan, 06.2 mil-
lion at 8.85% for 25 years, with seven years grace for principal repay-
ment. 1/ The incremental local costs of inputs, estimated at 03.5 million
including contingencies, would be financed through short term overdraft
facilities from commercial banks at 8-1/2% per annum, repayable within 12
months. As the long term loan is repaid the commercial banks would increase
their short term lending. Initially the commercial banks would lend about
t0.5 million; by Year 8 the amount would have risen to 05.8 million.
12.       Service Centers.  Farmer contact and market penetration would be
achieved through a series of retail ventures operated at strategically sited
service centers, from which extension staff would also operate. Ninety
centers would be built during the first three years of the Project, each
designed to meet and service the agricultural input needs of some 1,300 farm
families (Tables 1 and 2). Construction would, where possible, be undertaken
by local contractors. If insufficient contractors are available then the
Ministry of Social Welfare and Community Development could be contracted
to provide building services. It is expected that service centers would
become focal points of agricultural and farmers association activity; site
selection and demarcation would be made on this premise. The basic center
would consist of a secure store (in an easily replicable modular design)
equipped with cash register or similar accounting machine, and a platform
scale. Essentially, this basic structure would be a sales point for inputs.
Although planned extensions include the addition of covered and open storage
for produce and special marketing facilities for crops such as cotton and
paddy rice, as well as work areas for local artisans, the prime orientation
would remain that of consolidating a steadily increasing sales turnover.
13.       It is envisaged that of the 90 service centers, about ten would
be situated in areas where intensive mechanized farming is, or could be,
profitably carried on. In addition to suitable storage facilities (500-ton
covered, 500-ton open plinth), the centers (SCI) would include a machinery
servicing area with basic facilities, such as inspection pits or ramps
and a chain hoist, where farm equipment could be serviced by farmers or
approved mechanics; tools would not be provided. Arrangements would
also be made with the oil companies for the supply and installation of
underground fuel tanks. Two smaller sized centers (SC2 and SC3) having
150- and 75-ton covered stores would also be constructed.
1/   06.2 million is an estimate in 1975 constant terms; unallocated funds
are available to increase the loan to cover inflationary trends.



ANNEX 6
Page 5
14.       Specific requirements would vary between areas.  In order to ensure
the availability of appropriate and economic physical facilities, establish-
ment and development would occur and be administered on a district basis.
Such a scheme would also allow a well-balanced staff structure from the
outset, with consequent career attractions and enhanced opportuniies for
effective inservice training. District managers would operate from offices
(with integral 500-ton stores) sited at respective headquarter towns, and
would have overall responsibility for service centers in their districts. For
budgeting purposes the construction program has been phased as follows:
Type of Unit          Year 1        Year 2       Year 3
DHQ                   7             -            -
SC1                   3             3            4
SC2                  20            20           13
SC3                   -             7           13
Additional storage capacity required to accommodate preseason stock buildup
would be met from that already constructed by GGADP 1/.
15.       Input Marketing.  Recommended farm inputs would be purchased for
subsequent distribution and sale. The General Manager would have overall
responsibility for procurement in accordance with Board policy, with ICB
procedures to be followed for contracts exceeding US$100,000. Board approval
would be required for individual nonbudgeted items exceeding US$50,000, or
10% of budget contingencies, whichever is less. Sales would be made for
cash or deferred payment. Medium term or extended credit would not be
granted, although the Company would act as agent for ADB in the provision
of credit for oxen and selected farm equipment (see paras 16-18). Arrange-
ments would also be made with ADB, NIB and the commercial banks to supply
inputs to farmers who had received 'in kind' loans for this purpose; for
ease of accounting, such supplies would be released only after receipt of
payment from the credit source. The Company would not involve itself
in credit recovery activity except in cases where it had acted directly
as lending agent for ADB. Estimated sales for the first five years are
as follows:
1/   Totals 9,225 tons, of which 6,000 is situated at Bolgatanga and 1,175
at Wa.



ANNEX 6
Page 6
Year 1    Year 2    Year 3    Year 4    Year 5
-------------'-----  OOO    - ---    _________
Fertilizer                1,528      2,460    3,731      5,195     7,239
Seeds                       214        503      597        822       879
Insecticides                397        716    1,178      1,669     1,874
Other chemicals              21         48       89        160       235

Radio receivers              -         294      294        147        74
Oxen                         36         58       72         90       108
Total                2,322     4,363    6,305      8,540    10,950
16.       Pricing Policy and Operating Procedures.  Inputs would be sold only
to registered Upper Region farmers.  All seasonal inputs would have a basic
unit price to cover cost overhead and profit, with discounts and premiums
related to particular types of sale. Premiums would apply to deferred
payment sales and discounts to quantity orders. An indicative pricing
structure, based on forecast prices, is given in Tables 5-7. Within each
calendar year deferred payment terms would be available to both groups
and individuals, but the latter would have to belong to a group or asso-
ciation registered with the Company. Individual applications would be
endorsed by the group, thus affording increased security through collective
resposibility; group applications would require approval by village head.
Additional endorsement as to size and make up of the desired package would
be required from relevant URPMU staff. Service Center Technical Officers
would approve loans to individual farmers, but, initially, group loans
would require endorsement by the District Development Officer. The com-
pleted application would be exchanged for goods at the appropriate service
center, payment of the posted price being due by December 31. Purchasers
could extend the deadline to January 31 at a basic cost of 01.00 per
850.00 of goods, or to February 28 at a cost of 03.00 per 050.00 of goods.
Payments outstanding on March 1 would be subject to legal recovery action.
Defaults, both individual and collective, would result in removal of deferred
payment privileges from all concerned. Posted prices for deferred sales
would be, roundly, 12% above cash prices and would take account of the cost
of money, bad debts and additional administration costs. Each purchaser and
authorized group representative would have a laminated identity card or
similar device containing his photograph, serial number (identifying village,
section or group, and service center) and signature or mark. The possibility
of using the identity card as an accounting document would be investigated.
Because of the outstanding debt situation of some cooperatves, cooperative
members, societies or unions who wished to buy on deferred terms would have
to furnish a freedom-from-debt certificate from the cooperative or appropriate
Government department.
17.       Available information in regard to the range of farm sizes makes
it impossible to gauge the likely extent of first-time individual deferred
payment requirements; however, the scheme is primarily aimed at increasing



ANNEX 6
Page 7
input availability and sales to the mass of small farmers. Consequently,
initial limits would probably be equivalent to the value of a truck load
of approved inputs (approximately 01,750 to 02,500 at unsubsidized
prices), which would allow the discount benefits of quantity orders and not
make an undue proportion of Company risk depend on the knowledge of a perhaps
inexperienced extension worker. In addition, ADB and commercial bank
facilities would continue to be available for higher value purchases under
arrangements similar to those described in paras 16-18. The deferred payment
scheme would be reviewed regularly by the Board and amended with experience.
18.       General agricultural credit is presently operated by ADB, as the com-
mercial banks do not meet the needs of the majority of farmers, due primarily
to operational considerations. For farm equipment, oxen, radios, and other
capital items, the Project would seek to remedy the situation through FSC.
The Company would provide the means of making and maintaining the necessary
contacts and overcoming the long delays between application and approval which
bedevil the present lending procedures.
19.       All recommended farm equipment, including radios, would be available
for cash sale from selected service centers. Where necessary, financing
arrangements would be made with ADB for approved borrowers. For this purpose,
FSC would act as agent for ADB and prepare and process relevant credit applica-
tions; the ultimate lending decision and risk responsibility, however, would
remain with ADB. All loans would have a five-year duration, bear 12% annual
interest and depend on a 20% down payment by the borrower. Application forms
would be prepared at service centers after evidence of deposit had been pro-
duced, countersigned by subsection or village head and field assistant, and
passed to FSC district office for scrutiny and endorsement before transmission
to head office and ADB. Applications resulting in a total credit commitment
exceeding ¢1,500 would have to be approved by a Credit Advisory Committee,
established at district level. The articles would be released to the buyer
after the receipt of funds from ADB and on payment of the deposit. Repayments
would be made at the appropriate service center, the clerk being responsible
for reasonable monitoring of the debt. FSC commission would be 1-1/2% of
the gross amount of the loan and interest, and would automatically be deducted
by FSC as repayments are made.
20.       Oxen would also be sold by FSC on behalf of the Project.  Due to
the expected limited supply, requirements of credit buyers would be met only
after supplying the cash market. All potential purchasers would have to own
one ox, together with suitable yoke set, chain, etc., before being eligible
to buy from FSC; a plow/ridger would also be mandatory but could be made
available to approved applicants on credit terms. Oxen credit procedures
would be the same as for farm equipment, except that no cash deposit would
be required for the purchased ox. Successful applicants would also have
to take their oxen through the Project ox-training course; the oxen would
not be released until satisfactorily trained.



ANNEX 6
Page 8
21.       Distribution.  As much use as possible would be made of private
and State transport services, particularly those available from URDC. These
capabilities are now rather stretched; in order to reduce the chance of
delays FSC would initially own and operate ten seven-ton trucks. These
vehicles are intended as relatively short term gap-bridgers, with the
objective of progressively reducing direct FSC participation in transport
rather than maintaining a steady or increasing share of tonnage handled. A
further ten trucks would be purchased and leased by FSC to URDC to facilitate
transport of Project supplies to the Upper Region.
22.       The Agricultural Services Division would be established to provide
mechanized services to farmers; the manager would report directly to the
Managing Director. FSC would take over suitable machinery and equipment from
MA and URDC at agreed value, together with competent personnel now engaged
in similar operations. The Division would be costed on a profit-making
cash basis which requires a far greater machine utilization than is now
being obtained. This wo-uld be achieved by concentrating service acreages
and improving machine maint2nance. Particular attention would be given to
the possibility of cooperating with banks engaged in financing medium and
large scale farming projects. Td ensure desirable and logical development
of mechanized agriculture and reduce the possibility of an uncoordinated
proliferation of activity, the Division would cooperate closely with URPMU's
Technical and Land Development Services.
23.       The Section would take over the operations of the Ministry of
Agriculture's farm mechanization workshops at Zwarungu for all heavy main-
tenance and repair work, but would have two two-man teams to carry out emer-
gency repairs in the field and to provide an inspection and advisory service
for private driver mechanics. Each team would have a properly equipped
four-wheel drive vehicle, fitted with radio linked to the Project network.
24.       Subsidies.  All FSC operations have been costed on the basis of
farmers paying the full price for inputs, with a percentage mark-up applied to
secure the necessary returns. This percentage would be totally inadequate if
used on subsidized prices; indeed, a margin of 100% would be required on some
of today's prices to cover costs. In the event of a subsidized input and
uniform pricing policy being maintained, Government would reimburse FSC for
the consequent loss in earnings. Payment, based on the sales budget, would be
made preseason (before the end of January each year). At the same time final
adjustment would be made for the previous season. Government payments would
reflect a previously agreed percentage figure related to the true (unsubsi-
dized) cost of inputs; they would not cover expenses or losses occurring
through, for example, FSC failure to meet sales targets, unless these were
directly attributable to Government policies.
25.       Accra Office.  FSC would operate an Accra office which would ser-
vice its own requirements as well as those of URPMU. The office, which



ANNEX 6
Page 9
would be rented, would be managed by an executive officer, and assisted
by an accounts clerk and messenger. It would have radio communication
linkage with Bolgatanga. URPMU would share the costs equally with FSC.
April 7, 1976

ANNEX 6
Appendix I
Page I
GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Draft Regulations of the Farmers Services Company (Upper Region) Ltd.
1.        The FSC would be organized under the Companies Code, 1963, and its
regulations would specifically include the following causes in addition to
or by amendment to standard clauses:
(a) Name and Location: The name of the company would be
Farmers Services Company (Upper Region) Ltd., and its
principal office would be located at Bolgatanga.
(b) Objectives: The Company would:
-    set up service centers strategically sited in the
Upper Region as a chain of retail outlets;
-    procure, distribute and sell fertilizers and other
recommended farm inputs in the Upper Region;
-    provide agricultural services in the Upper Region
appropriate to the needs of individual farming
areas, including agricultural machinery hire
services;
-    ensure farmers' adequate purchasing power by
operating deferred payment schemes for those
items required on a seasonal basis;
-    provide incentives for transportation and farmers
association development through a bulk discount
system;
-    provide facilities for produce storage and marketing;
and
-    operate agency services for statutory organization.
(c) Board of Directors:
(i) Membership
-    Project Manager of Upper Region Agricultural
Development Project (Chairman):



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 2
one representative from each of the participating
banks;
one representative from the Bank of Ghana;
one representative from the National Investment Bank;
two representatives from the Ministry of Finance;
three farmers from the Region appointed by the Project
Manager until such time as farmers' equity demands farmers'
elected representatives;
-    Managing Director (FSC);
-    Chief Accountant (FSC) (Secretary).
(ii) Rotation: The members of the Board other than the Project
Manager, Managing Director, and Chief Accountant would be
appointed for two years, three to retire each year and be
eligible for reappointment. There would be no retirement
during the first year. Thereafter three would be retired
every year.
(iii) Fees: To be limited to a reasonable per diem for each day
of attendance at Board meetings plus reasonable out of
pocket traveling expenses. Reasonable directors' fees
may be fixed only after the surplus account is equivalent
to at least 25% of paid-in capital.
(iv) Powers: The Board as the policy making body of the Company
would:
-    approve the annual budget of the Company;
-    draw up procurement, distribution and sales policies;
-    appoint and fix the salaries and terms and conditions
of employment of all senior staff;
-    determine the amount of dividends and transfers to
surplus in accordance with regulations (see para g (i)
and (iii));
-    appoint the auditor.
(v) Meetings: The Board shall meet at least once a month and
at such times as the Chairman may direct.



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 3
(vi) Quorum: Five directors would form a quorum, one of whom
shall act as chairman in the absence of t*h Chairm.-
(d) Capital and Subscribers:
-    The authorized capital of FSC would be 012.0 million.
-    The initial shareholders would include the Ministry of
Finance and Ghanaian banking institutions.
(e)  Shares:  All shares would have equal status and would be tra-..,-
ferable only by consent of the Board. Voting rights would be
proportional to equity held or represented. Farmers' equity
would be created through a mandatory mark up on the landed
store cost or base cost of 2-1/2% on all items sold by the
Company. Farmer's equity would therefore grow with sales.
The Bank of Ghana would hold in trust farmers' equity rights
until such time as properly elected farmer directors are
appointed to the Board.
(f) Special Powers and Privileges:
(i) The FSC would act as agent for the Agricultural Development
Bank in the provision of credit for oxen and recommended
farm equipment; for this service FSC shall be paid a com-
mission of 1-1/2% based on the gross amount of the loan
and interest.
(ii)  If subsidies for farm inputs are not entirely removed or are
reimposed during the life of the Project, FSC would receive a
subvention from Government on an annual basis, payable as and
when required for the cost of selling at subsidized rates.
(See Annexure 1 for subvention formula.)
(iii)   During the first five years, FSC would be exempt from
import duties and taxes, and during the same period also
be exempt from import licencing.
(g) Dividends, Surplus and Reserves:
(i) Whenever a positive balance in the undistributed profit
account becomes available (i.e., after the accumulated
loss from the first few years of operations has been
eliminated), it will be allocated to a capital reserve
account until the balance of such reserve equals 100%
of paid-in capital.



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 4
(ii)  No dividend shall be declared until the balance in the
capital reserve account is equal to 100% of paid-in
capital.
(iii)  Dividends may be paid only from unappropriated surplus
after conditions in (i) and (ii) above have been met.
(h) Annual General Meeting:
-    to approve accounts and direcor's report;
-    to confirm dividend payments;
-    to appoint auditors (directors to fix their remuneration).
April 14, 1976



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Annexure 1
FSC Fertilizer Store Cost and Government Subvention
1.        FSC Store Landed Cost for Fertilizers (see Annexure 2).  The FSC
store landed cost shall be computed by adding the following charges to CIF
Tema:
(a) landing charges computed on a per ton basis;
(b) transport cost from Tema to Bolgatanga on a per ton
per km basis;
(c) finance charge - 5% of CIF Tema, landing and transport
charges; and
(d) local (Upper Region) transport and handling costs,
estimated to be 04/ton.
2.        Mark-up (Farmers' Equity and Margin).  Selling price of fertilizers
would be the FSC store landed cost plus a 22-1/2% mark-up (this mark-up
applies to all farm inputs except oxen); 2-1/2% of which would cover the
farmers' equity in FSC. The 20% would substantially cover operating costs of
FSC. This margin appears reasonable since operating costs from Years 1 to 5
average about 20% of farm input costs over the same period. It must be
noted that the 20% mark-up is an arbitrary figure, based on current costs
and estimated turnover. The percentage mark up is subject to modification,
and would normally be established at a level that would enable reasonable
returns on sales investment.
3.        Government Subvention.  If fertilizers are sold at subsidized
prices Government shall pay FSC annually a subvention equal to the difference
between the selling price (para 2) and the subsidized selling price. For ease
of accounting, fertilizer sales would be recorded at the subsidized price.
At regular intervals FSC would prepare statements for each kind of fertilizer
showing the FSC store landed cost per ton, selling price, subsidized selling
price and Government subvention. This should preferably be done when finan-
cial statements (monthly or quarterly) are to be prepared. To facilitate
determination of the Government subvention at any given time a factor should
be obtained by dividing the Government subvention by the subsidized selling
price as shown in the statement mentioned above. This factor should be
multiplied by the sale figures for fertilizer as shown in the book of
accounts (see Annexure 2).

Appendix 1
Annexure 2
ILLUSTRATION OF STEP-BY-STEP APPROACH TO CALCULATE GOVERNMENT SUBVENTION
1.   Calculate unit store cost of fertilizer.
Example:    CIF Tema (¢ per ton)                      208
Add:  handling charges 1/ 2/              10
transport cost 1/ 3/               44
Subtotal                              262
Add:  distribution & finance cost 4/   17
Unit store cost (¢ per ton)              279
2.   Determine total basic selling price(TBSP) of fertilizer and total
requirement in tons.
Example:     (in ¢)
Total Store  2-1/2%       20%     Basic Selling
Fertilizer   Requirement   Unit Price        Costs      Equity   Markup         Price
(tons)     (per ton)
Nitrate          2,240         279 5/       624,960    15,624   124,992        765,576
Phosphate        2,690         229          616,010    15,400   123,202        754,762
Potash              20         301            6,020        150      1,204        7,374
Total tons    4,950                        Total basic selling price:   1,527,712
3.   Obtain subsidized unit selling price (see Annex 11, Table 7, Footnote 2).
Example:    Year 1      ¢100 per ton
Year 2      0140 per ton
Year 3      0180 per ton
Year 4      0200 per ton
Year 5      0220 per ton
1/ As estimated in report; subject to variations which could affect calculations.
2/  Cost per ton for landing  charges.
3/  Tema to Bolgatanga:  800 km, at ¢0.055 per ton/km.
4/ 5% plus 04.00 local transport and handling.
5/ See step 1 above.



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Annexure 2
Page 2
4. Calculate Government subvention.
Total subsidized selling price = Total requirements x Subsidized unit
in units           selling price
Government subvention = Total basic _ Total subsidized
selling price    selling price
Example:     TSSP = 4,950 x 140 = 693,000
GS = 1,527,712 - 693,000 = 834,712
Short Formula to Determine Government Subventions
Total basic selling = Total store cost (TSC) + 2-1/2% TSC + 20% TSC
price (TBSP)
Total subsidized selling = Total requirements   Subsidized selling
price (TSSP)                in units            price per unit
Government subvention = TBSP - TSSP
