GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Seed Multiplication and Research
A. Seed Multiplication
Background
1.        There is no commercial seed industry in Ghana; seed multiplication
is undertaken by the Ministry of Agriculture. In the Northern and Upper
Regions the responsibility rests principally with the Ghanaian-German Agri-
cultural Development Project (GGADP) and the Cotton Development Board (CDB),
though the Grains and Legumes Development Board also proposes its own founda-
tion seed farms within the 1975/80 five-year development plan. Acquisition
and provision of breeder seed is the responsibility of the Crops Research
Institution of the Council for Scientific and Industrial Research (CSIR) 1/.
2.        Cotton seed multiplication at all stages is handled by CDB and
passes through four bulking stages. Breeder seed of IRCT origin is either
imported or collected by balanced mass selection within the CDB foundation
seed farms. Foundation seed of Allen 333 is bulked on a CDB farm at Damongo
from which progeny is distributed to growers in Bolgatanga for bulking to
certified seed. For variety BJA 592, breeder and foundation seed is planted
at Nyankpala Research Station from which it passes through an additional
bulking stage involving registered growers in the Wa district. Subsequent
bulking for certified seed is also undertaken at Wa.
3.        Prior to 1975, all commercial inputs 2/ were provided free to cot-
ton growers.  For the 1975 season a flat cess of 0150/ha (¢60/acre) was
introduced, to be deducted from market proceeds. All cotton seed is
dressed (with Dieldrex A) but is not fully delinted.
4.        CDB staff involved in the seed multiplication program report
three main areas of concern:
1/   CSIR falls within the Ministry of Economic Planning.
2/   Includes seeds, fertilizers, insecticides and sprayers on loan to be
returned at the end of the growing season.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
(a) sprayers used during base multiplication are un-
satisfactory and have resulted in low yields
(around 825 kg/ha);
(b) varietal impurity has resulted from careless
handless and mixing of varieties during warehouse
storage; and
(c) the problems of (b) above have been compounded by
the lack of any financial incentives to registered
growers to rogue.
5.        These problems are recognized by CDB and an expansion and improve-
ment of its seed multiplication capability is already planned; this
would be sufficient to service Project requirements with supporting super-
vision of registered and certified growers by Project field staff.
6.        Seed multiplication for crops other than cotton is presently under-
taken by a Seed Multiplication Unit (SMU) established within the Ministry of
Agriculture by the GGADP. Standards and regulations, and their enforcement,
are the responsibility of a National Seeds Committee for which SMU is the
certifying authority. Bulking of foundation seed is undertaken by contract
outgrowers whose crops are subject to field and store inspection prior to
purchase. Attention has hitherto been directed almost entirely towards paddy
seed production for which SMU has a large cleaning, dressing unit at Tamale
with storage capacity for about 60,000 bags (5,000 tons), which expects
to distribute around 50,000 bags for the current season; a similar unit
with 40,000-bag storage capacity is under construction at Bolgatanga. Seed
multiplication is highly subsidized since paddy is purchased for 015.00 per
bag from contract growers (a margin of 00.50 per bag over the guaranteed
price) and registered/certified seed is sold for 017.65 per bag; a break-even
sale price is estimated to be around 030.00 per bag.
Seed Requirements
7.        The crop production proposals of Annex 2 assume that varying
proportions of farmers' seed requirements would be met by replanting home-
grown seed; provision of simple seed dressing equipment backed by neces-
sary instruction from extension staff would be included under the Project.
Assumed seed replacement rates and estimated annual requirements of new
seed are given in Table 1; this requirement would be derived through
the stages from breeder to certified seed given in Table 2.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
Organization
8.        The existing GGADP (MA) Seed Multiplication Unit at Bolgatanga, which
would automatically become responsible to the Project Manager, would be well
suited to handle Project seed requirements though some additional machinery
would be required to cater for a wider range of crops than are presentedly
handled. Contract growers who are deemed to comply with the standards and
regulations laid down by the National Seeds Committee would be located by
Project extension staff and subject to regular field inspection by SMU staff
prior to crop purchase (GGADP would be able to initiate registered seed pro-
duction sufficient for Year I Project requirements prior to Project implementa-
tion). For ease of supervision, contract seed production would center, where
possible, on large farm units and smallholders involved in the Vea Irrigation
Scheme; foundation seed production would be undertaken at District Agricultural
Stations.
9.        The substantial seed requirement of the Project, particularly
when viewed in the context of national improved seed requirements, requires
fundamental reappraisal of Government's somewhat ad hoc policy on seed sub-
sidies, and it is quite appropriate that the cost be borne, at least in part,
by the farming community itself. Furthermore, the present high subsidies
will undoubtedly encourage wasteful use of seed 1/ and limit the overall
coverage and distribution that would be possible. Under the Project, there-
fore, the seed costs included provide a reasonable price incentive to contract
growers to adequately maintain and rogue their crops and also a commercial
sale price which would cover variable operating costs (casual labor and
transport) of SMU. These cost assumptions have been used in Table 3 to cal-
culate a trading balance for SMU. Certified seed would be purchased directly
from SMU by the Farmers Services Company (UR) Ltd. and distributed to farmers
with a minimum mark-up of 22-1/2%
B. Research
Background
10.       With the exception of cocoa, all research in Ghana is the responsibi-
lity of the Council for Scientific and Industrial Research (CSIR) within the
1/   For example, a grower of new improved seed with access to seasonal
credit facilities, would have little incentive to replant homegrown
seed, for he could sell his seed requirement for cash to the SMU, have
it collected, cleaned, graded and dressed, and subsequently repurchased
on credit. This would encourage annual seed replacement which when
expressed on a large scale would involve heavy and unnecessary Govern-
ment capital and recurrent expenditure while contributing little to
overall improvement.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
Ministry of Economic Planning; the agricultural programs of CSIR are under-
taken by the Soils Research Institute (SRI) and the Crop Research Institute
(CRI) both with headquarters at Kwadaso, near Kumasi.
11.       Research within the Northern and Upper Regions is centered at
Ngankpala Research Station, which is situated 19 km west of Tamale at
9  25'N, 00  58'W.  The Station was established under the Ministry of Agri-
culture in 1947 1/ and operates within 1,000 hectares, of which the annual
research area amounts to about 50 hectares. Trials in the Upper Region
are mainly located at Manga substation, with occasional district trials
sited at District Agricultural Stations. Manga substation is situated 5 km
southwest of Bawku at 11 001'N, 0 016'W and has about 90 hectares of land.
12.       Research in relation to smallholder agriculture was largely cur-
tailed in 1963-66 when attention was directed to large scale mechanized farm-
ing operations. Although interesting and relevant work has been undertaken
subsequently, effective operation has been prevented by a shortage of staff
and funds. Substantial financial and technical assistance has recently been
requested from, and is under consideration by, the Federal Republic of
Germany.
13.       In addition to CRI, applied trials and demonstration programs
have been operated by Development Boards and MA, the latter sited mainly
at District Agricultural Stations. Prior to 1963, these stations served
as district headquarters for extension staff where recommended practices
were both tested and demonstrated and where staff and farmers were trained.
During 1963 to 1966, the stations were taken over by the United Ghana
Farmers Cooperative Council (UGFCC) and converted to mechanized farming units.
Most of the conservation works were destroyed during this period and although
efforts have been subsequently made to revive their original status they
continue to serve as evidence of soil susceptibility to sheet erosion along
with questionable demonstration of improved technology.
Research Requirements
14.       The substantial improvements in primary research facilities which
are already programmed for the north, should, with some strengthening (see
para 17), suitably serve the area's ongoing development needs. Research
attention under the Project would aim at complementing these improvements
by regional dissemination of recommended practices through a network of
applied trials and demonstrations. This would be achieved by reactivating
the District Agricultural Stations (DAS), which would serve as land planning
1/   The station was transferred to the Scientific Division in 1960 and to
CRI in 1963.



ANNEX 7
Page 5
and crop management centers where staff and farmers would have the opportunity
to observe husbandry practices and techniques specifically geared to the
district's climate, soils and farming systems. The statfon would also under-
take foundation seed production according to the requirements of the seed
multiplication program.
15.       Each DAS would be suitably staffed with a station manager responsi-
ble for day-to-day operations under the direct supervision of the District
Agricultural Officer. Overall responsibility for planning and supervision
would rest with the Farm Management Section of the Agricultural-Technical
Services Department which would include an Applied Research Officer responsible
for trial coordination and close liaison with research workers at Nyankpala,
Manga and Kwadaso.
16.       A realistic approach to research would be essential with trials
directed towards the specific problems of Project management and farmers. This
requires a thorough knowledge and understanding of the traditional farming
systems and an efficient and effective feedback from farmers undertaking
improved cropping practices.
17.       Basic research in developing improved cereal varieties, particularly
maize, sorghum and millet, has not received due attention. In order to ensure
long term progress for crop development in northern Ghana, the Project would
fund the costs of a plant breeder, specialized in grains, who would be seconded
to CRI from an international research institution, such as IITA, and who would
be posted to Manga. Funds would be available for a three-year secondment
agreement to cover costs of a plant breeder and the necessary supporting
infrastructure including housing and equipment. It is expected that the con-
tract would take effect from the second year of Project implementation. De-
tailed costs are at Annex 10. Apart from the direct benefits of improved
cereal breeding and selection, the plant breeder, having the support of an
international institution would be able to introduce new ideas and breeding
material being developed elsewhere in West Africa. Appointment of the plant
breeder would be subject to terms-of-reference, qualifications, and conditions
of employment acceptable to the Bank.
December 4, 1975

GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Communications and Training
A. Background
1.        The Upper Region of Ghana has great potential for agriculture, but
the rate of development and the degree of population participation will be
limited unless action is taken to remedy serious deficiencies in communica-
tions, education and training.
The Linguistic Situation
2.        The principal problem is the difficulty in communication caused
by the complex linguistic situation. The following 17 different languages
are spoken in the Region:
(a) Indigenous Spoken Languages
Estimated number of persons
speaking the language in the

of the Bimoba in the Upper
Region also speak Kusaal.

not widespread.

not widespread.

3.        Thus about 75% of the population can be reached through three local
languages, Kusaal, Gurenne and Dagaari, but it is not possible to exclude any
one of these languages without cutting off many thousands of people, nor to
combine one of them with another, as they are distinct languages.
4.        Written Languages.  Officially there are only three written
languages in the Upper Region, Kasem, Dagaari and English; written materials
are produced for them by the Bureau of Ghana Languages. Primary school
children in the Region are instructed in almost all the different languages.
Education and Literacy
5.        The formal education system in Ghana follows the British pattern
but is of longer duration. It includes a four-year middle school inserted
between six years of primary education and seven years of secondary, and is
based largely on a memorization and reproduction concept of education.
6.        Successive governments have made efforts to improve the educational
standards of the people of Ghana. In the early 1960s free and compulsory
primary education was introduced, resulting in substantial increases in
primary school enrollment: 450,000 in 1959/61; 1,140,000 in 1965/66.
(Detailed education enrollment figures for the Upper Region are in Table 1.)
Numerous other forms of Government, private and mission sponsored formal and
nonformal smaller scale education and training schemes have been tried and
continued, often on an uncoordinated basis, over the years.
7.        The various educational efforts in the Upper Region have not had
the desired effect. Some 90% of the population remains illiterate 1/. Only
1/   Estimates vary from 5% to 20% literacy rate.



ANNEX 8
Page 4
22% of the primary age group are in school (compared with 62% for Ghana as a
whole), and 66% of them drop out during the primary course. Due to their
uneconomic enrollments, 130 primary schools had to be closed in 1970/71; entry
into the first year of primary school has not changed significantly for the
last six years 1/. It will take at least 40 years under present policies (and
there is so far little indication of change) for even half of the primary age
group to reach a satisfactory level of literacy 2/.  There is now, furthermore,
clear evidence of resistance, on the part of parents, to education both for
their children and themselves; it is unlikely that the education provided by
the Ministry of Education can bring about substantial improvement of condi-
tions in the Upper Region within the near future.
B. Adult Functional Literacy
8.        The first efforts towards literacy training for adults started in
Ghana in 1948. In 1952, after four years of research, a mass literacy cam-
paign using the Laubach method was launched. In the Upper Region, written
material was prepared in Gurenne, Dagaari and Kasem, and rural newspapers
were published in Dagaari and Kasem. By 1956, however, the program began
to falter; further work has been limited. The reasons for failure were that:
(a) literacy did not result in any improvement in living standards;
(b) voluntary instructors lost interest, as no rewards were given
for their efforts;
(c) mistakes were made in applying the Laubach method too rigidly,
in often not selecting suitable instructors, and in discourag-
ing initiative on the part of individual literacy groups;
(d) local languages were never used in official administrative or
commercial documents, and the rural press did not always publish
material of interest to readers; and
(e) eventually, the Department of Community Development, which was
responsible for the campaign, changed the emphasis of its work
to other aspects, including self-help schemes and well construc-
tion, and withdrew material support from the literacy program.
9.        There remain, however, some traces of the campaign in the Region;
a number of people are able to read and write in Dagaari and Kasem, and
classes continue on a small scale, particularly in Dagaari. The new con-
cept of "functional" literacy is virtually unknown.
1/   Although in 1973/74 there was an increase in enrollments in the first
year of primary education, which is believed to have been due to a deter-
mined effort by chiefs and Government officials to persuade parents to
send their children to school.
2/   Assuming that present enrollment trends persist and that 'retained
literacy' is measured as the completion of four years of primary
education.



ANNEX 8
Page 5
10.       In 1971, the Institute of Linguistics launched an experimental
literacy program in the Bimoba language, which is spoken mostly in the
Northern Region, and is showing very promising results. Instruction is
given in the local language for two years 1/ and then participants learn
English. The first year results in the acquisition of basic reading
and writing skills; the second year makes these skills fully functional.
Special "bridge materials," carefully devised to take account of the struc-
ture of the local language, are then used to introduce English in the
third year. Great care is taken in selecting instructors; they are nor-
mally chosen by the villagers, who tend to reject former school pupils
who are no longer integrated into village life. Classes begin with 15
students and two instructors; after a month, the group is divided into two.
One group is likely to complete the whole three-year program; the other
often closes down after basic skills have been acquired, in which case the
instructors responsible for the latter then commence with another group.
C. Agricultural Training
11.       Degree courses in agriculture are offered by the University of
Ghana (UG), and the University of Science and Technology (UST); annual
outturn is 30-40 and 50-60 respectively. Horticulture diplomas are
offered at UST (annual outturn 40) and diplomas in agriculture, animal
health and home economics at UG (average combined annual outturn is limited
to 35 through lack of residential accommodation). All diploma courses are of
two-year duration, for which Ministry of Agriculture Technical Officer grades
are eligible provided they are in possession of a school certificate; Ministry
staff at the B-grade are eligible for degree training provided they satisfy
minimum academic entry requirements.
12.       Certificate courses, the basic agricultural qualification, are
available at agricultural colleges at Kwadaso (intake 181), Nyankpala (intake
70) and Ohawu (intake 51). Minimum entry qualification is GCE/school certifi-
cate; course duration is two years, followed by one year as a trainee in the
field. Of the 181 vacancies at Kwadaso, 50 are available for Ministry of
Agriculture junior employees who are middle school leavers with a minimum of
five years field experience. For this category a probationary first year
residential course in basic sciences is provided covering physics, chemistry,
biology, maths, English and introductory economics; successful students then
complete the two-year residential agricultural course but are exempt from the
trainee year in the field.
13.       Present output at the higher level (degree and diploma) is suffi-
cient for Project requirements, but the Upper Region's share of certificate
level outturn is likely to fall short of requirements; this would be compen-
sated by induction and inservice training under the Project at Navrongo
Farm Institute.
1/   A "year" means, in fact, the 3-4 month period during the dry season.



ANNEX 8
Page 6
14.       Navrongo Farm Institute was established in 1964 with USAID
financing as a joint institute for Upper and Northern Regions. The
Institute has dormitory capacity for 120 but beds and bedding for only
70 students. It has workshops and a residential teaching staff of five.
The Institute also has a farm, but it is located some five miles from
the site. The main thrust of the training program is directed towards
Form IV school leavers with agricultural backgrounds who receive a one-
year general agriculture course. The training objective is to have
trainees who have successfully completed the course return to the land
to form a cadre of technically more competent and commercially aware
farmers.
15.       The training program manifests in many ways Government's frus-
tration at, and neglect and disesteem of, the traditional smallholder,
who has consistently shown himself to be a more efficient and constant
producer than his commercial counterpart. Less than 60% of trainees
return to the land after the course. At present there is considerable
underutilization of facilities. A shortage of spare parts has produced
a graveyard of unserviceable and sometimes unused equipment.
D. Communications and the Use of Radio
16.       Rural Broadcasting.  Ghana has long experience of rural broad-
casting, having started its first specialized programs in 1962. In 1965,
UNESCO supported a large scale scheme for setting up rural radio forums
for organized groups for (a) group listening; (b) subsequent discussion            (
and action; (c) feedback to the ratio station, with advice to the authorities
on problems and feelings in the villages; and (d) participation by villagers
in actual programs. Some 230 farm forums function today.
17.       A staff of 14 GBC professionals produces programs which include
farm magazines, dramatized serials, discussions, question and answer pro-
grams, farm forum programs and specially designed musical programs with an
agricultural slant. The rural broadcasting unit broadcasts in five of the
principal Ghanaian languages, in programs varying from 30 minutes per week
in Nzema to three hours per week in Akan, but not in any of the languages
of the Upper Region. It is not surprising and of some concern to the
authorities that many listeners in the Upper Region, therefore, turn to
Upper Volta for programs which they can understand. Although GBC has a
small relay station at Bolgatanga, no special material is produced for
the Upper Region.
18.       Yet radio is potentially the most important communication/educa-
tion training input available to the Project: it can make an impact in a
relatively short period of time and in a geographically defined area; it
can reach illiterates as well as literates and establish close and conti-
nuous links between Project management and the people the Project is designed
to serve. The Project envisages a series of tasks and goals which can be



ANNEX 8
Page 7
much more easily attained where there is active motivation, participation
and understanding of the process involved. Hence, the need for an input
which "educates" both people in the field (chiefs, farmers, extension
workers, officers in charge of service centers, literacy instructors)
and the management of the Project, which needs to be aware continuously
of problems being encountered, and able to respond to questions and
suggestions put to it.
19.       For the individual farmer, radio can provide news of direct
relevance, practical advice adapted to seasonal conditions, and informa-
tion on farmers' rights and how they may be-asserted--the absence of
which factor often results in sullen discouragement and an obstacle
to development. The members of the family can be reached regularly
on problems of sanitation, nutrition and family planning, and radio
can also fill a void in entertainment and relaxation: the flight from
rural monotony is one of the elements in the exodus of the young.
20.       Transmission and Reception.  Reception is largely through
transistor radios; it is not known how many of these exist in the Region.
Listeners in towns are able to subscribe to a subsidized rediffusion
service, but this is proving excessively expensive and is likely, even-
tually, to go out of use. At present, the Government has a 'short wave
only' policy, and plans to market a new preset receiver, able to receive
only GBC programs, at a cost of about 025 per set--less than half the
cost of the most common AM/FM/SW sets now available.
21.       The Government has also decided, and has made provisions
in its five-year plan, for the introduction of regional FM broadcast-
ing facilities. The reasons are that the sound quality in FM broad-
casting is much better, transmitters are easier to install and maintain,
and programs can be beamed directionally, thereby giving maximum effec-
tiveness with limited transmission power, a factor of considerable
importance for the Project, which intends to broadcast programs mainly
to an Upper Region audience.
22.       The principal disadvantages of FM broadcasting are (a) the
higher cost of receivers (production lines in most industrialized
countries do not produce FM/SW receivers although these would be the ideal for
the proposed Project); and (b) since existing radio sets in the Upper Region
would not be able to receive FM regional programs, it would, therefore, be
necessary to ensure rapid dispersal of new receivers to potential listeners.
E. Other Communications Media
23.       Ghana's extension services use other means of communication
to aid them in their work. Although there are at present five mobile
cinema vans in the Upper Region, plus others operated by the Ghanaian/



ANNEX 8
Page 8
German Agricultural Development Project and the family plannng unit of
the Ministry of Health, serious difficulties have been encountered
in their use: the cost of films is so high that few have been produced
which are of relevance to the Upper Region; the units are so thinly
spread that individual villages can be visited only very infrequently;
and producing commentaries in languages other than that of the film
is a delicate task, since the images have a stronger impact than words
and words are not really understood unless clearly related to the image.
24.       A number of mission stations and other institutions in the Upper
Region have equipment for the projection of slides and film strips accom-
panied either by live or tape-recorded commentary. Still images, in color,
frequently instruct more effectively than moving images, except where
movement of itself is crucial (as in the case of animal husbandry).
Although film strips are cheap and simple to produce, the medium is little
explored or available in the Region. The introduction of video (portable
television) has proven particularly effective in rural areas in other
countries in training and social communication, but its introduction into
the Project would encounter technical and distribution problems. Simple
audio-visual aids (flip charts, flannelgraphs, graphics, overhead projection)
are also of undoubted value in training and extension.
F. Traditional Channels of Communication
25.       The people of the Upper Region share a common past, linguistic
roots and customs, but the contemporary existence of distinct languages
leaves each group relatively ignorant of and unconcerned by the activity
of others. Traditional forms of expression include dancing, music played
on flute and string instruments, and the singing of women. Of special
importance is story telling by old and young; its role in the transmission
of Project policies could be emphasized through radio. Radio should also
become, in time, a catalyst for these different forms of expression,
welding the population into common awareness and effort through dissemi-
nation and explanation of the diverse local customs.
G. Project Proposals
Radio Broadcasting
26.       The Project would establish a regional radio broadcasting service
for the Upper Region that would initially broadcast in the three main lan-
guages. The facilities would be owned and operated by the Ghana Broadcasting
Corporation, and would, with advice from the Project Management Unit, broad-
cast news, entertainment and educational material to support the overall
objectives of the Project.



ANNEX 8
Page 9
27.       The Project would follow Government policy in regard to developing
FM broadcasting, and would be conceived in such a way as to minimize opera-
tional costs. The proposed system would:
-    utilize existing GBC installations;
-    provide simple facilities so that existing staff could operate
the equipment; and
-    have a low power consumption.
The broadcasting facility would consist of five medium-effective power trans-
mitting stations, to be operated as a network, at Bolgatanga, Bawku, Tumu,
Lawra and Wa. A single production center would be located at Bolgatanga to
produce and control all Upper Region programming activities.
28.       Transmission.  Topography and vegetation information confirm that,
from a signal propagation aspect, FM is practical and preferable. AM trans-
mission could also be a practical alternative, with an initial investment
cost of + US$50,000 of the FM installation cost (the latter estimated at
US$64,500). However, the power consumption for AM would be double that of FM.
The FM system would incorporate at a low incremental cost a good quality two-
way communication system that would allow transmittal of Project material
from distant locations to Bolgatanga for either direct or deferred broadcast,
which would be a great asset in a region where telecommunications are vir-
tually nonexistent. The main drawback to FM is that most of the existing
receivers in the Region are thought to be AM/SW or SW only. However, adequate
FM/AM receivers are available and some 22,000 would be sold to Project farmers
on credit.
29.       Production Facilities.  The operational concept of the studio is
based on a one-man operation. Both studio and recording facilities would use
a horseshoe configuration of equipment. The studio would be equipped with a
dual-channel console to allow two functions to take place simultaneously.
The main studio would be supplemented by a small recording studio. Details
on the equipment are in the consultant's report dated November 30, 1975.
30.       Transmitting Facilities.  Five transmitting stations, each with
omnidirectional pattern and circularly polarized radiation characteristics,
would be established. The Bolgatanga station would be the mother station;
Bawku and Tumu would be fed off-air from Bolgatanga, and Lawra and Wa would
be fed off-air from Tumu. The following summarizes transmission parameters:



ANNEX 8
Page 10
Approx.       Tower         Approx.
Locations             EHAAT /1       Height          ERP /2    Polarization
(feet)        (feet)         (KW)
Bolgatanga              500           500            7.1          circular
Tumu                    350           400            5.5          circular
Lawra                   350           400            5.5          circular
Wa                      225           300            5.7          circular
Bawku                   150           200            6.0          circular
/1   EHAAT - electrical height of antenna above terrain.
/2   ERP - effective radiation power.
31.       Transmission frequencies have not been and need not be selected at
this time. Final choice should be made while keeping in mind downstream and
upstream off-air and image frequency relationships.
32.       In the interest of economy in regard to both initial investment and
operational costs, no new sites would be developed, but use would be made of
existing GBC relay stations and personnel, although in one or two cases some
additional land might be required.
33.       Subchannel Feature.  FM broadcast is inherently capable of easily
carrying two subchannel facilities. It is proposed to implement one upstream
subchannel towards Bolgatanga from each of the other four stations. By the
same token a downstream facility is included for cue purposes. The upstream
facility permits feeding local reports from each of the four distant stations
towards Bolgatanga. The technical quality of this communications feature
would be comparable to outside broadcast equipment, and would thus permit
either direct broadcasting or recording for delayed playback, editing, etc.
Such reports could be contributed by part-time network reporters, and would
be particularly attractive to the listeners.
34.       Equipment Operation.  GBC would be required to supplement its
existing regional engineering staff by a specialized maintenance technician
who would be based at Bolgatanga and would proceed to the other four loca-
tions on a regular basis to check all equipment and restore it to proper
operation. Since the equipment is very simple, a single engineer would be
sufficient to maintain it. The existing GBC personnel located at relay
radio stations would attend the transmitter, turn equipment on and off,
record meter readings, report symptoms of improper operation and operate
the subchannel and associated equipment.
35.       Equipment Procurement.  A consultant systems engineer would be
employed to prepare, in cooperation with GBC authorities, detailed specifica-
tions for the towers, transmitters, and transmitting antennas and feeders.
For the balance of equipment, for which no single item exceeds US$10,000, a
shopping list approach would be taken to achieve optimum value and equipment



ANNEX 8
Page 11
mix. Erection of towers and installation of antennas and feeders would be
undertaken by the tower supplier and would form part of the tower specifi-
cations. A separate installation crew, directed and supervised by an
installation engineer, would install transmitters and studio equipment.
Details of transmitting and recording equipment costs are at Annex 11, and
are detailed in the consultant's report.
36.       Consultant Requirement.  A consultant(s) is required to assist
GBC staff in detailing equipment specification and supervising installation.
The consultant(s) should have a minimum of four to five years experience
in the field of broadcasting if he is from an engineering company whose
senior members have a minimum of ten years experience. If in private
practice, he should have a minimum of ten years experience. For systems
engineering, he should have no interest in the manufacture or supply of
equipment. For installation engineering, he should have at least a minimum
of experience in the field of installation. The consultant(s) responsibi-
lities would, in conjunction with GBC, include:
(a) for systems engineering:
-   systems design;
-   preparation of equipment list;
-   preparation of purchase specifications for towers, antennas,
and transmitters;
-   preparation of shopping list for balance of equipment;
-   evaluation of bids and recommendations;
-   preparation of systems and installation drawings; and
-   preparation of final coverage patterns.
(b) for installation engineering:
-   general project management and coordination;
-   supervision of installation and general field engineering
as required;
-   checking out all major equipment including antennas;
systems adjustments;
systems operational check out;
field intensity measurements to confirm proper operation
of systems;



ANNEX 8
Page 12
preparation of final report;
provision of a minimum of one-month on-site training
for operational and maintenance personnel.
H. Broadcasting Programming Proposals
37.       The introduction of languages would be phased; Kusaal, Gurenne,
and Dagaari would be broadcast from the inception of broadcasting in the
second year of the Project. Suggested program content, hours and alloca-
tion of time for regional material and direct relay of GBC 1 from Accra
are shown in Table 3, and envisage 86 hours of broadcasting per week,
of which 55 would be produced in the Region; however, program content would
have to be adapted to local conditions and would vary with the seasons and
school year.
38.       Group Participation and Feedback.  A utilization section would be
set up at the Communications and Training Department of the Project to assist
local communities in organizing listening groups, to allocate receivers to
groups and assure their maintenance to stimulate feedback, to analyze feed-
back and feed it to program producers and Project management, and to cooperate
with the evaluation section of the Project in evaluating the success of pro-
grams. Staff speaking each of the three languages would regularly visit the
appropriate parts of the Region.
39.       Staff Requirements and Training.  GBC, like other organizations in
Ghana, appears overstaffed; one of the objectives of Radio Bolgatanga would
be to develop a self-operating system whereby the programming staff under-
take operational functions as well. Bearing in mind that the Project would
provide considerable back-up support in program preparation, incremental
staffing for Radio Bolgatanga is proposed as follows: 17 program staff,
eight news reporters, one engineer, and nine administrative staff including
subordinate daily paid workers. Details at Annex 3.
40.       The station would be an integral part of GBC; station staff would
operate under GBC terms and conditions, although good quality staff would be
attracted by the provision of adequate housing. The annual regional pro-
gram would be prepared by GBC in consultation with the Senior Communica-
tions and Training Officer of the Project, and would be approved by
URADPEC.
41.       Special training courses to commence in PYI would be given at the
GBC training school in Accra, especially for those speaking the languages
of the Upper Region, which are not currently spoken at GBC. For adminis-
tration, program direction and technical operations, however, the Project
would rely on experienced staff transferred from Accra. Funds would be
available to key staff for short overseas training courses in local broad-
casting. A reserve of recorded material, including music and stories,
would be assembled in advance of the opening of the three language programs.



ANNEX 8
Page 13
I. Functional Literacy
42.       This program component, with its emphasis on the functional nature
of literacy, represents a new approach for Ghana; previous literacy campaigns
have been traditional in approach and unrelated to economic development. The
proposals are, therefore, experimental and the results would need to be eva-
luated. It would try to avoid past mistakes and benefit from recent research
in literacy methods conducted by the Institute of Linguistics in Tamale.
Recommendations for a Region-wide, functional literacy program would be made
only if the pilot scheme proved successful.
43.       Groups.  Sixty groups would be formed, totaling some 1,500 partici-
pants; each group would commence with 25 participants, of whom 15 to 20 would
be expected to complete the program. Thirty groups would be instructed in
an area served by two service centers in Lawra District and 30 groups by two
service centers in Bolgatanga District. These areas have been chosen because
they contain the two largest language groups in the Region, representing some
two thirds of the population. Lawra District was chosen in preference to
Wa, which is in the same area, to avoid difficulties which might arise due
to rivalry between Dagaari and Wali speaking people. Participants would be
in the age group 15 to 40 years old, and consist primarily of farmers using
the service centers. The actual location of groups would be decided during
Project implementation in response to requests from villagers.
44.       Instruction.  During the first weeks, instruction would be based
on documents used by farmers in the overall program (for purchase of inputs,
sale of production, simple calculations, etc.); later, other literature
would be introduced on improved methods of cultivation, calculation of
areas, production costs, etc. A knowledge of what is said on official
forms would help to dissipate suspicion, which often arises amongst farmers,
on the motives of persons dealing with their financial problems. Therefore,
basic program documents used by farmers in the two areas would be printed
in the local languages as well as in English.
45.       Instruction in the first year (meaning, in effect, the dry season
from December to May of the second year of the Project) would be given in
the local languages, as this is the most satisfactory way of ultimately
teaching English -- a knowledge of which is widely desired in the Region.
Some participants would be expected to abandon the instruction at the end
of the first year either because of difficulties encountered or, more
probably, in order to join the annual exodus to the south to seek work;
these participants would have received some usable knowledge which would
enable them to understand simple rural newspapers.
46.       In the second and third years (fourth and fifth years of the Proj-
ect), the use of written and spoken English would be gradually introduced.
The instruction would be supported by the use of slides at least twice per
month and collective listening to regional radio programs. Those who



ANNEX 8
Page 14
completed the full program would reach a level enabling them to assist in
the operation of service centers, and to initiate, participate in and
lead group activities and farmers associations or cooperatives.
47.       Staff.  The pilot scheme would be prepared and organized by a
specialist with practical experience of functional literacy in the rural
environment, preferably in Africa (for three years); he would instruct
Ghanaian personnel (one at the level of principal community development
officer, two at that of community development officer and four at the level
of assistant community development officer; two speaking Dagaari and two
Gurenne) in the planning, organization, material production and implementa-
tion of the program. An illustrator/photographer would also be recruited,
plus three secretaries capable of typing in local languages. To ensure
adequate evaluation, technical assistance would be provided for two months
during the second year of the overall Project and for two months in the
fifth year.
48.       Because of the lack of uniformity in typical groups, two instructors
would be chosen for each group. One subgroup could then be instructed at a
more rapid rate than the other. Special attention would be paid to (a) the
necessity of choosing persons fully integrated in village life, and hence (b)
selection of instructors by the villagers themselves, and (c) the frequent
unsuitability for literacy instruction of teachers trained to deal with
children. To avoid the creation of a "bureaucratic" spirit and excessive
expenditure in any subsequent expansion, motivation of instructors would be
augmented by awarding simple gifts, e.g., uniforms, badges, certificates and,
in cases of particular success, issuing to the instructor, at the end of a
program, the collective radio used by the group. Particular importance would
be attached to moral support and visits by Government personalities.
49.       Relations with Other Agencies.  The pilot scheme would be an
integral part of the Communications and Training Department of the Project;
relationships with the Community Development Department of the Ministry of
Labor, Social Welfare and Cooperatives would be achieved through the Project
Coordinating Committee, by exchange of information on progress being made
and, through assistance given by the Department, the release of appropriate
personnel to implement the scheme.
50.       Preparation of Teaching Materials.  Syllabi and reading matter in
local languages would be prepared during the first year, English language
material in the second year. For the transition from the local languages
into English, special "bridge materials" would be prepared with the assist-
ance of the Institute of Linguistics (for Gurenne) and the Language Study
Center of the Catholic Mission at Wa (for Dagaari). A cyclostyled local
language press containing information directly of interest to farmers,
regional news and correspondence from instructors, would be established for
each language group, using the printing facilities of the Project. The
Bureau of Ghana Languages at its Tamale office would assist in producing
simple brochures designed for the elementary stages of the program; at a
later stage, such brochures would also be produced in English as part of



ANNEX 8
Page 15
the overall Project. Sets of 15 slides with tape-recorded local language
commentaries would be prepared with the help of Catholic Mission audio-
visual centers. The regional radio station would also provide programs
of (a) motivation (items illustrating the value of literacy, announce-
ments of local language publications and interviews with new literates);
(b) encouragement (news of progress being made in the different centers,
visits by personalities, ceremonies for awarding certificates, news of
successes); and (c) educational content for group listening.
51.       Construction, Furniture and Equipment.  The Project would provide
office accommodation and equipment at Bolgatanga with two staff houses
plus an additional house for each of the two districts where the pilot
scheme would operate; transport would be provided for staff and audio
visual aid equipment for the unit.
52.       Each literacy group would be provided with a lamp and "kit" compris-
ing the necessary instructional material; fuel would also be provided for the
lamp so as to ensure that a lack of this would not become an obstacle during
the pilot scheme. Purchase of batteries for the group radio would be the
responsibility of the participants.
53.       Evaluation.  During his first visit the evaluator would:
(a) examine the situation existing before the start of the
program, including, in particular, the level of knowledge,
simple arithmetic, agricultural techniques, the capacity
of potential participants to utilize the services of the
Project and to form interest groups, and their attitudes
towards literacy and towards local lanuguages;
(b) define the objectives to be attained, including the level of
knowledge, the number of participants to be trained, and
the best utilization of the knowledge to be gained;
(c) collaborate with the head of the scheme in planning the
work program, producing material, training instructors,
and experimental aspects;
(d) introduce a method of collecting data during the scheme,
with appropriate arrangements where subsequent changes
in the work program have to be made.
54.       During the absence of the evaluator, the head of the scheme would
ensure the collection of all appropriate data including enrollments, drop-
outs, levels attained, dates of courses, facilities available, help given
by the radio, press, official and private agencies, opposition, obstacles
met, expenses incurred and results achieved; also adjustments in planning
the work in light of progress made.



ANNEX 8
Page 16
55.       During his second visit (in the fifth year), the evaluator
would examine:
(a) the way in which the experiment had progressed, with particular
reference to its planning, the number of participants (men, women,
dropouts), and the factors which had played a role in its success or
failure;
(b) knowledge acquired by participants in local languages, English,
arithmetic, new agricultural techniques, farm budgeting, sanitation,
etc.;
(c) efficiency of teaching methods, use of visual aids, contribution of
radio;
(d) use made of knowledge acquired; reading of the rural press, brochures,
participation in the overall Project, organization and management of
interest groups, correspondence, etc.;
(e) as far as possible, the economic profitability of literacy, its real
costs and benefits, including a comparison with the costs of tradi-
tional education;
(f) the direct benefits of the program, e.g., greater motivation, atti-
tudes towards local languages, towards literacy, progress made by
Ghana in accepting the concept of functional literacy, influences on
the evaluation of the education system.
56.       The evaluator should also make recommendations for the future
and, in particular, for the design of a larger scale program of functional
literacy for the Region. Evaluation of this Project component would be
combined with the work of the Project's Planning, Evaluation, and Monitor-
ing Department.
57.       The Future.  At the end of the fifth year, follow-up of the new
literates through continued provision of appropriate English and local
language written material should be assured through collaboration with the
Community Development Department and/or the Ministry of Information.
J. Agricultural Staff Training
58.       A fundamentally new approach to training would be established
under the Project. This would commence conceptually with a downgrading
of sophistication for both staff and farmer training to a level commensurate
with proven research within the area and the environment within which it
would be applied. This would be followed by a thorough inventory of
existing infrastructure in the Region and a re-allocation of buildings
and equipment to uses which would most effectively serve the overall
development objectives of the Project.



ANNEX 8
Page 17
59.       Courses specifically for school leavers would be discontinued
at Navrongo which would, initially, be fully involved in general staff
induction courses and in specific training of the various extension
service task forces. Staff refresher courses would be a continuing
commitment; the degree of involvement in induction training would depend
on the proportion of agricultural assistant trainees that the Project
requires prior to sufficient outturn from preservice training institu-
tions.
60.       Lower echelon farmer training would be undertaken by extension
field staff in group meeting houses at service center level; instruction
at this level would be informal and based on day courses since the
essence of the syllabus would be to provide specific, relevant and
regular tuition catering to those unable to leave their farms for long
periods. Such training would also by supported by day courses and
visits to district agricultural stations where farmers would have the
opportunity to observe applied research specifically directed towards
their farming systems problems and ecological conditions.
61.       At a higher echelon, this would be supported by creating a
main field research base at Navrongo where district agricultural station
managers and trainee staff could observe and be trained in new technology.
As the Project progresses, the Institute would also cater to more advanced
and longer duration farmers training; farmers so selected would be those who
emerge as receptive to advice and as influential leaders within their commu-
nities. Such courses would be part of an integrated program involving
necessary follow-up supervision by extension staff on completion of the
course.
62.       This trend would also mark the gradual evolution of the Institute
into an expanded spectrum of activities catering to basic commercial and
artisanal needs among Project staff and farmers that are expected to
emerge parallel with the area's overall economic growth. All such courses
would have a strong practical bias with short term training assistance
provided by staff from other Project departments.
63.       At the management level, the Project Management Unit would ensure
that a satisfactory managcmcnt training program is implemented for Ghanaian
staff. The development of local managerial capability would be an important
objective of the Project; Government would ensure that Ghanaian staff with
high technical and managerial potential are posted to key local appointments.



ANNEX 8
Page 18
K. Organization of the Communications and Training Department
64.       The Department would be headed by an internationally recruited
Senior Communications and Training Officer who would be directly responsible
to the Project Manager. As head of the Department he would be responsible
for the coordination of training and communications activities, and would
have a particular responsibility to see that training courses were properly
organized and that the training content fully reflected the needs of the
Project.
January 7, 1976

ANNEX 9
Page 1
GHANA
UPPER REGION AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Land Development
A.   Background
1.        The Project area falls within a climatic zone that allows for a
35-40" rainfall distributed over 5 - 6 months, followed by a 6 - 7 month
dry season that is rendered even harsher by the harmattan that brings very
dry dusty air from the Sahara. This cycle results in high water runoff,
particularly during the early part of the rains; the problems are further
compounded by the fairly dense human and livestock populations, resulting in
serious sheet and gully erosion. Stream flow is erratic, and except for
the major rivers most streams dry up for 4 - 5 months. All these problems
are accentuated in the eastern half of the Region.
2.        Prior to the 1960s, soil and water conservation programs had been
established by MA. In all some 220 dams have been constructed with capa-
3
cities varying between 50 - 400,000 m . The majority (60%) are in disrepair
due to underdesign of spillways and lack of maintenance, but many could be
repaired. In association with water conservation, efforts were made to
conserve catchments through tree planting and enforcement of grazing control.
Most of the past progress has been lost through the absence of maintenance and
an expanding population that now cultivates and grazes in areas that in theory
should be protected.
3.        The provision of water for humans and livestock is critical to any
long term development plan for the Region. The Canadian Government, through
CIDA, has initiated a project throughout the Upper Region to provide for
about 1,200 village hand-pumped wells. These are adequate for humans but
not for livestock.
B.   Projcct Proposals
4.        The Project would establish, under the Field Operations Department,
a Land Development Division which would be responsible for land planning,
soil and water conservation, and the operation of contingent plant and
equipment. It would also have responsibility for village wells and farm
storage.
5.        The Land Development Division would:



ANNEX 9
Page 2
repair and improve about 100 existing dams;
construct about 120 small earth dams to be located
in strategic areas for the provision of livestock
watering points, dry season gardening, fish ponds
and general water conservation:
provide adequate conservation works to conserve the
catchment areas of existing and new dams including
the protection of associated irrigation works;
rehabilitate, where possible, the soil conservation
works within the former land planning areas of the
Region; and
with the other sections of the Field Operations Depart-
ment develop suitable agricultural systems to complement
the physical soil and water conservation works described
above.
6.        The organization required to operate the Land Development Division
is detailed at Annex 3 and would comprise a planning component and a number
of functional field units, including a special Soil and Water Conservation
Working Account (SWCA) that would operate on a semi-commercial basis and
would be responsible for construction.
C. Soil Conservation
7.        Conservation of Dam Catchment Areas.  The average area would be
about 5 km (2 sq miles) and would require conservation works to reduce
runoff, thereby protecting the dam against excessive flood waters and
siltation. It is proposed that the conservation of any given catchment
area be undertaken in two stages:
(a) In the first year, natural or artificial waterways would
be located approximately 1,000 m apart. Each waterway
would be grassed (using self-help village labor). Master
ridges, 1 m wide and about 30 m apart, would be marked
on the contour with a tractor-mounted disc plow, as a
guide to farmers when ridging their crops. For the
average catchment 55 km of marker ridging would be required.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
(b) In the second year, V ditches, or cutoff drains, would be
constructed to a grade of 1:400 and would be spaced about
100 m apart. Each ditch would run for a maximum of 600 m
to a waterway. The 2ditches would have a cross sectional
area of about 0.8 m (37 cm deep and 3.6 m wide). The
ditches would be constructed by a 125 hp motor grader
(see Table 2 for output data). The average catchment would
require 20 km of ditches.
8.        To allow for the procurement of plant and equipment, actual con-
struction of the works would not commence until the second year of the Proj-
ect. It is likely that conservation works would be required to protect
120 new dams and about 200 existing dams, in all 160,000 ha. Thus 1,280 km
of natural or artificial waterways, 17,600 km of marker ridges, and 6,400 km
of V ditches would be constructed during the four-year period.
9.        Conservation of Other Areas.  Outside the dam catchment areas it
is important that ridge cultivation follow the contour. The latter is a long
term program and would be best undertaken by the extension staff using line
levels. Master ridges to be left uncultivated would be marked out 30 m apart
for slopes of between 0-5%, and every 15 m for slopes exceeding 5%. Farmers
would assist extension staff in locating the master ridges, the latter being
marked with an ox plow. The farmers would make their crop ridges between the
master ridges; the Project would encourage the practice of tie ridging to
further conserve water; the ties would be 1-2 m apart and half the height of
the ridges. This would give maximum absorption in the early light rains. In
the heavy rains the ties would break and allow the excess water to run off.
Ties would be replaced near the end of the rains to assist in improving
infiltration at that time. Other measures including the practice of not
cultivating near gulleys and water ways would be re-introduced. Tree planting
would also be part of an overall conservation policy and is described under
Section H of this annex.
10.       Conservation methods as described in para 10 would be extended
to the flat valley bottoms most favored for rice production. The main
purpose would be to retain water, and where appropriate simple water con-
trol measures could be undertaken. Thus extension staff would locate
master ridges on the level contour, 20-30 m apart, that would be developed
by the farmers into water retaining bunds. In this way it should be possi-
ble to improve water retention, which should allow continued plant growth
after the end of the wet season. Further sophistication in design, to
include concrete water control structures, etc., is not envisaged under
the Project.



ANNEX 9
Page 4
D.   Water Conservation
11.       The Project would continue the past program of constructing small
earth dams in order to spread the availability of dry seasop livestock
watering points; provide for small dry season irrigated gardens; and
provide for a general improvement in downstream water flow. The Project
would also rehabilitate some of the existing dams.
12.       Hydrological data are generally poor but sufficient for small
dam development purposes. Annual rainfall in the area averages from
700-1,200 mm, and a runoff yield varying from 0.080-0.150 mn hm 2can be
expected. Evaporation from the natural water surface is very high, being
about 300 mm per month in the dry season, or about 1,500-1,800 mm per
annum.
13.       New Dam Construction.  A careful analysis of topography,
availability of local construction materials and hydrology indicate that
3
a network of small dams with reservoirs of about 120,000 m capacity should
be built.  Each would have a catchment of about 5 km  in area.  The dams
would be equipped with outlets to provide, if necessary, a gravitational
water distribution system for small irrigated gardens. The wall and spill-
way of each dam would be fenced and if a road passes over the dam, cattle
grids would be provided at either end.
14.       A typical earth dam for the Region would have specifications as
detailed below:
(i) Features of average dam:
-   total maximum height                7.00 m
-   free board                          1.50 m
-   depth at spillway level*            5.50 m
-   average wall length               400.00 m
-   total capacity                                      120,000 m3
-   dead storage or sediment space                       20,000 m3
-   useful storage capacity                              100,000 m3
-   spillway overflow with concrete sills with riprap in between
(it may be necessary to peg down the riprap with gabion wires
in special cases);



ANNEX 9
Page 5
-   dam - earth fill with clay core, having a 4 m top
width and 1/2 and 1/3 side slopes with riprap on
the upstream face, containing about 15,000 m3 of
fill (for 400 m crest length);
-   foundation - treated to impervious zone; core would be
installed to an impervious foundation;
-   two outlet pipes of 15 cm in diameter laid in concrete
with at least three concrete flanges, and equipped
with downstream gate valves.
(ii)  Useful life of dam:  20 years; allowance made for 20,000 m3
of sediment.
15.       Repairs and Rehabilitation for Existing Dams.  According to the
Annual Report of the Ministry of Agriculture (1968), 219 dams have been
built in the Upper Region. District distribution and estimated storage
capacity are given below:
District Distribution           No.    Storage Capacity ('000 m )       No.
Bolgatanga                      41                0 -  50               17
Bawku                           67               50 - 100               39
Navrongo/Sandema                61              100 - 150               19
Wiaga                           31              150 - 200               18
Wa                              19              200 - 400               26
over 400                 7
not specified            93
TOTAL               219                                      219
3
Average storage capacity per dam would be 70-80,000 m , with a catchment
2
area ranging from 0.5 to 7 km
16.       Absence of a toe drain, and the practice of planting trees on
or at the top of the wall, cause minor leakage, while insufficient
impermeable material in the core of the wall accounts for significant
water losses. Silting occurs in cropped catchment areas, whereas in
reforested catchment areas this has been prevented. Fifty percent of
the dams need repair, the damage being due mostly to cattle movements
on the dam and insufficient maintenance. Ten percent of dams have been



ANNEX 9
Page 6
washed out completely and cannot be repaired. Most of the dams have natural
spillways, while some have no spillway at all (dugout). Only 20% of them have
cut spillways protected by concrete of which half need repairing. Although
some dams have been fenced, very little remains. Fencing the dam wall is
necessary.
The following rehabilitation works would be undertaken:
-    reshaping and refilling, for 50% of dams, by replacing
upper 60 cm with fresh material and adding riprap on the
upstream side;
-    repairing leakages on bigger dams by providing impermeable
material on the upstream embarkment and in the immediate up-
stream bottom of the lake;
-    rehabilitating spillways by repairing the concrete sills
and fortifying natural spillways;
-    inserting outlets for irrigation on those dams with
sufficient water by constructing a small concrete inlet
chamber with a 15 cm pipe entrenched in the dike;
-    fencing the dam and installing cattle grids on both
sides if there is a road on its crest.
E. Irrigation
17.        Most of the dams have a potential surplus of water that could be
used for small scale irrigation schemes. It is conservatively estimated
that a minimum of 750 ha of land could be irrigated. Thus each new dam
to be constructed would be carefully located as to storage potential
and irrigable land that it might command. Two 150 mm outlet pipes,
one for each side of the dam, would supply unlined irrigation canals
(unlikely to exceed 500 m each). Between the parallel canals a simple
conservation and land leveling scheme would be developed by participating
farmers, allowing for drainage down a medial line. The area would be
fenced (about one km per dam). Details of the crop production aspects
are at Annex 2.



ANNEX 9
Page 7
F. Fish Ponds
18.       Three fish pond complexes would be developed on a pilot basis in an
attempt to quantify more accurately their economic and financial potential.
Each complex would comprise 20 half-hectare ponds which would be located below
suitable dams.  Construction would involve moving some 10,000 m  of earth and
would be undertaken by the SWCA. No additional construction funds are required
since the excess capacity built into SWCA would suffice. The fish ponds would
be used for breeding carp and tilapia fingerlings to stock the Region's dams,
and for practical demonstrations of fish farming methods. There is a ready
market within the Region for the harvested fish. Project Management Unit staff
would be available to operate the ponds, and a specialist fish culturist would
be employed to determine more accurately the parameters of fish farm management.
G. Maintenance of Works
19.       Dam construction and associated small scale irrigation works would
be undertaken only after an association of potential users had been formed.
The association would be responsible for:
-    protection of soil conservation works;
-    maintenance of dams, intake structure and irrigation
facilities; and
-    allocation of land and responsibilities for water
distribution.
20.       The association in most cases would be the same as the farmers
association formed for each service center as described in Chapter VI of the
report. It should be made quite clear that there is no legal recourse
or penalties that can be applied for nonmaintenance of works. Maintenance
will occur only as a result of the farming community perceiving the benefits,
particularly those relating to income increases. In the past, in Ghana
and elsewhere, neglect and disrepair of water and conservation works have
nearly always occurred where production benefits were absent.
H. Tree Planting
21.       Afforestation by the Forestry Department was started in 1949 in the
Dedora Tankera Land Planning Area. Species grown throughout the Upper Region
included: Tectona grandis (teak), Khaya senegalensis (mahogony), Azadirachta
indica (Neem), M. tragyna inermis and Anogeissus lerochapus.



ANNEX 9
Page 8
22.       In recent years the Forestry Department has restricted its
activities to replanting programs in the forest areas in the Upper
Region. Its staff, consisting of one Forestry Officer and 12 TOs (three
in Lawra, five in Navrongo, and four in Bawku district), operate some
tree nurseries below dams. The slow growing teak is preferred as it
resists bush fires. Additional funds have been earmarked for village
tree programs but no program has yet materialized.
23.       The Project would support a continued tree planting program
consisting mainly of protecting catchment areas and developing small
village woodlots.
24.       The most suitable trees available are the fast growing Gmelina
and Acacia species, both resistant to drought. Eucalyptus, although
needing protection from animals, would be a valuable supplier of wood.
Final recommendations should be made in cooperation with the Forest
Research Institute at Kumasi. Farmers, who would pay for the seedlings,
would be advised in land preparation, hole digging and planting of one-
year-old trees supplied from nurseries in polythylene pots. Fire traces,
6 m wide, would be cleared and maintained around plantations. It is
proposed that two nurseries per district would be developed. See costs
at Table 4.
25.       Fruit trees would be grown for dietary improvement and for sale
at local markets. No processing is foreseen. The climate and soils are
suitable for growing a number of trees. The success of mango and
citrus introduction by agricultural stations and missions has shown there
is a high demand by the local population. At Wiaga station more than
1,000 grafted mango and orange trees were sold for 0O.80 each; similar
success was achieved in Lawra.
26.       Among the trees to be considered mango would be the simplest
to introduce as it is grown easily with initial watering of the plant
and protection from animals by a mud wall or thorns. Sweet oranges
and guavas, which have proved to grow well once sufficient water is
available, are further possibilities. Cashew nuts are untried in
the Upper Region but grow well in the Bole area of the Northern Region.
27.       The Project would promote fruit tree growing around the com-
pounds and in those areas with sufficient water. As a first step nur-
series would be established for the supply of rootstock and grafted
trees. Rather than creating new Government stations, it should be
tried - as in Upper Volta - to train progressive local farmers in
nursery establishment and grafting so as to put improved plant produc-
tion and supply on a commercial basis. In the early stages some Gov-
ernment experimentation and training would be required. Estimated costs
are at Table 4.



ANNEX 9
Page 9
28.       The fruit tree program would be operated by a horticulturist within
the Project's Forestry Division whose responsibility would be to:
-    establish two central nurseries per district for variety
selection and initial plant supply, and
-    advise farmers associations in planting and maintenance.
Initially the Project would rely on imports of grafted trees from Upper Volta
that would be sold for cash to farmers, prior to Project stock becoming
available.
I. Research
29.       The Land Development Department would work closely with a proposed
USAID-financed conservation research unit that is to be established in the
Tamne Valley of Bawku District. The proposed research unit would determine
more closely the causes of soil erosion and would quantify the rate of erosion,
runoff factors, etc. It would also research methods of erosion control.
J. Aerial Photography
3o.       Present coverage for the Upper Region as a whole comprises 1961/
1965 photography flown at 1:30,000 scale, and some selected areas at 1:10,000
scale flown in 1949 and 1968. The Project would require new photography; and
it is proposed that 1:20,000 black and white, and 1:40,000 infrared color
(cibrachrome film) would be flown at the same time using a 3" and 6" lens
respectively. The 1:20,000 would have some distortion at the edges of the
photographs and would be used for the siting of dams, conservation works,
and general layouts. They would not be used for detailed mapping. The
1:40,000 infrared color would be distortion free and could be used for de-
tailed mapping. However, under the Project it would be used for quantify-
ing essential physical data including vegetation, crop location, soils,
hydrology, cultural features, etc., which are vital to effective crop in-
ventory and land use planning. Because infrared color has good resolution and
haze penetration capability, shows moisture well by tone differences, and
shows the infrared reflection of live vegetation in magenta color lines
that record the chlorophyl content of viable vegetation, it is possible,
by combined accurate ground truthing and computer scanning, to quantify
and record on transparent laydowns selected physical data. Initially, some
six separations of data would be analyzed, including area under cultivation,
cereal (sorghum), groundnuts, rice, fallowland, wetness and compound locality.



ANNEX 9
Page 10
Further separations would be possible if required. The time of flying
is critical and should be undertaken from mid-September to mid-October,
weather permitting, when optimum differentiation between crops would be
possible. The flying, photography and computer analysis would be con-
tracted to an international company. Costs are at Table 4.
K. Grain Storage
31.       The Project would assist farmers in improving onfarm grain
stores. The Garu Agricultural Station of the Christian Service Committee
(CSC) has developed a simple type of improved farm storage that can be
constructed, under guidance, by the villagers themselves at a very low
material cost. The same type is also being promoted by GGADP.
32.       The store consists of a cylindric container 3 m high built from
mud bricks with concrete reinforcement and plastering inside and outside.
Two sizes are presently built: (a) for 900 to 1,000 kg (10 bags) with an
inside diameter of 0.91 m, and (b) for 1,400 to 1,500 kg (16 bags) with a
diameter of 1.12 m. The silo is built on an isolated rock foundation,
has a slanting floor with a chute and a concrete slab on the top that
allows almost airtight storage, thus reducing losses considerably. The
materials consist of the reusable wooden frames to produce the mud-
bricks and framing, plus two bags of cement for the smaller type and three
bags for the larger.
33.       The Department of Social Welfare and Community Development, in
coordination with Project Technical Officers, would be responsible for
demonstrating these new stores and teaching farmers to build them. The
Project would procure the cement for sale to farmers through the Farmers
Services Company stores. The target would be to reach 10% of the farmers,
12,000, by the end of the fifth year of the Project. Costs are at
Table 4.
L. Village Wells
34.       Currently the Ghanaian Government and CIDA are installing communal
hand-pumped boreholes (up to 100 feet deep), and by end of May 1975 had con-
structed 600 out of their target of 1,200. They have also undertaken ground
water studies. The Department of Social Welfare is also engaged in providing
covered wells with fitted pumps. The wells are used for village water
supply and for small dry season vegetable gardens. Village wells promoted
by the Department of Social Welfare and Ministry of Agriculture are generally
4 to 6 m deep and have a diameter of 1.2 m. Walls are reinforced above the



ANNEX 9
Page 11
rock substrate with bricks or concrete fillings, using metal shuttering
and simple tools. The casing is extended to 0.90 m above the surface
and has a concrete apron.
35.       The Project would assist in constructing 100 improved wells
per district (total 700 wells). Construction would be undertaken on a
self-help basis by the villagers, though the Project would assist in
pumping out the wells during construction so that an adequate depth could
be reached. The Project would equip each with a wheel-operated hand pump.
The Department of Social Welfare would be responsible for implementing the
proposals. Costs are at Table 4.
M. Soil and Water Conservation Working Account (SWCA)
36.       Contractors are limited in number and are unable to construct
the rather complex soil and water conservation works. Therefore, under
the Project SWCA would be established (see para 6.06 of main report)
to implement the proposals. SWCA would be operated on commercial lines
with commercial accounting and cost structures, and, most importantly, the
authority to pay commercial rates and production bonuses to plant operators.
In the long term, SWCA would provide a heavy plant and equipment service
for the Region, and its plant, if available, could be hired out at commer-
cial rates for works not included under the Project.
37.       SWCA would take over the workshops currently operated by the
Ministry of Agriculture's Mechanization and Irrigation Divisions at
Zwarungu (near Bolgatanga). The workshops would be suitably staffed and
equipped and would service SWCA and URPMU plant and vehicles. It would
also service at full cost vehicles belonging to the Farmers Services
Company and to the Irrigation Department. Full details of plant and
equipment costs are included in Tables 1-3.
December 3, 1975
